FOREWORD TO THE

SECOND EDITION

This updated edition by Takei and Alston is a much-welcomed update of their excellent work. The book is aimed to help Americans, and other westerners, learn more about the cultural and business practices of their Japanese clients, customers and other partners and be more effective at building productive relationships that can transcend different languages and cultural and business practices.

Takei and Alston are to be praised for coming back to the issue after several years have gone by. The fact is that many cultural, business and economic realities in both nations have changed markedly. While fundamental aspects of cultural preferences may be the same, specific applications, especially those pertaining to individual and business communication strategies, have taken on new life and new borders and limits. Greater dependence on social media and the Internet are new realities that must be acknowledged and prepared for.

In addition to these profound and new communication realities, there are other important changes that have taken place in both countries that need to be raised and faced so that better cooperation and understanding can be established. First, the international community for both nations has become more problematic as the growth of

Chinese power and North Korean adventures create challenges for both Japan and the U.S. Issues of long-term trade and security must take these realities into account and demand new, and often, difficult, conversations about predictability and

trust.

Economic realities have also changed in both nations. The recession of 2008 had severe effects on the confidence and economic well-being of both nations. New fears, new obligations and new measures of progress and failure must be factored into older patterns of interaction. New calls for national protectionism and exclusivity, in both nations, create new challenges for old patterns of cooperation and support.

So, the timing of this new book is very welcomed indeed. Takei and Alston's excellent advice on how U.S. and western businesses and consumers can continue to improve their interactions with their Japanese partners remains a very important lesson. Acquiring a greater understanding of their traditions, values and goals, in this context of this new era of the individual, is essential knowledge. This new era brings on changes in Japanese and U.S. management practices, hiring practices and national economic policies. Takei and Alston prepare their readers on how to navigate these new realities while not obscuring enduring cultural themes.

Arnold Vedlitz

Bob Bullock Chair in Government and Public Policy Bush School of Government and Public Service Texas A&M University College Station, Texas USA

FOREWORD TO THE

FIRST EDITION

There is a Japanese word "kosobayui." This word contains subtle nuances translated as "sensitive" and "subtle." When I read Japanese Business Culture and Practices, I experienced the feeling of "kosobayur" because this book describes and explains Japanese spirituality and culture in a skillful way and with a delicate sensibility.

I still remember the strong shock I felt when I first read a book on Japan written by an American anthropologist: The Chrysanthemum and the Sword by Ruth Benedict (1946). I used this book for my class readings as well as for my research. I was surprised to read that Benedict wrote about Japan in great detail as if she were Japanese.

Needless to say, Japanese Business Culture and Practices is different from The Chrysanthemum and the Sword. However, the analyses and insights are extremely subtle and this book is just as important as the work by Benedict. Japanese Business Culture and Practices is one of the best-referenced and most interesting books for those who want to know about Japan and for those planning to conduct business with Japanese. This is a very good reference for U.S. American businessmen who conduct business with Japanese. I highly admire the two authors' efforts. Because I am Japanese, those who say "yes" and "no" very clearly, like U.S. Americans, remain

a wonder although I have had a long academic career in the area of international relations. I

am also impressed with those who can bring a conversation to an end quickly and decide on issues quickly and concisely. By contrast, as the book explains, Japanese do not directly express their preferences and dislikes. The same holds true for saying "no." Numerous researchers have studied this unique Japanese culture. However, their opinions vary in their viewpoints, ideas, and research methodologies. Different people use different perspectives and analyses to reach various conclusions. I respect the approach and presentations of Japanese national character found in the Alston and Takei book.

I am strongly opposed to looking at the Japanese people and Japanese culture in stereotypical ways. I read books only when they deal with concrete Japanese behavior and characteristics in detail rather than through the use of stereotypical generalizations. I determine the value of books by looking at the extent to which they are written objectively. In this sense, Japanese Business Culture and Practices is a work consistently objective as well as detailed. This book is very useful to the readers who are interested in business relations and negotiations with Japanese.

While contemporary Japanese enjoy the benefits of cutting-edge technology and science, they have nevertheless maintained their traditional culture. This balance of old and new is crucial for the Japanese. While accepting Western thought and Western modern spirit, the Japanese also maintain a traditional samurai spirit. Japanese still follow the demands of the sempai and kohai relationship in modern business environments. While

appreciating baroque buildings and paintings, Japanese also follow a simple and pure aesthetic sense, as seen in haiku poems, that is admired throughout the world. Different from the western drama expressing a manifestation of emotions, Japanese traditional drama conveys the internal (spiritual) world by silence and stillness. Martial arts that start and end with bows to opponents and teachers still prosper. Japan is a society where the logic of "winning the game, but losing the fight (or match)" continues to make sense. Winning means nothing if you lose face and pride. By contrast, sympathy for and support of the weaker person are part of the Japanese heart. Western heroes are not necessarily the heroes in Japan.

As the title, Japanese Business Culture and Practices: A Guide to Twenty-First Century Japanese Business suggests, there are on-going changes in the Japanese environment though the core foundations of Japanese society are stable and unchanging. As the increasing number of free- ta (temporary workers) shows, the central idea of working for one employer for a lifetime is changing among younger Japanese. Western principles of competition and payment based on ability do not directly fit into the traditional Japanese society. It has generally been thought that those who have ability to be overachievers and paid larger amounts of remuneration as a result violate the traditional Japanese sense of equality. Now, however, the payment systems based on seniority and age are changing, as this book points out.

Japanese, who have an innumerable number of gods, traditionally have a different view of life and sense of values from Westerners who believe in one absolute god. In front of a tutelary deity, Japanese

are essentially ujiko (local residents worshiping the same guardian deity) and are equal. Japanese dislike differentiation and individualization. As early as the sixth century, Japanese have lived in social harmony, wa, which became the most important teaching for a respectful way of living. In spite of many twists and turns, the teaching of 1,400 years of history is still the base of today's teaching among families and society.

As Japanese Business Culture and Practices notes, corporate presidents and employees in Japan eat together at the same cafeterias. They also take part in corporate excursions, and presidents and employees together share a hot spring communal bath. Having a heart-to-heart talk means people trust others and understand the humanity of others across different social statuses and positions. It is easy to declare that U.S. Americans are individualistic and Japanese are group-oriented. I oppose the idea that this rough categorization is enough to describe the differences between U.S. Americans and Japanese because such stereotypes are mistaken. This book offers details that avoid stereotypes.

As we like different kinds of flowers in their full glory in a flowerbed, contemporary Japanese need to be aware of the perspectives from different nationalities and multi-cultural ideas. Japanese Business Culture and Practices describes in clear language and presentation Japanese business negotiations and cultures and offers the reader detailed explanations of real situations. I recognize the value of this book. It is very easy to blame Japanese businessmen and Japanese culture for consistently ambiguous statements. Those who negotiate with Japanese should understand the

background of these attitudes and behaviors; if

you look at Japanese history, culture, educational system, religion, thought, aesthetics, human relations, family system, and customs, you will better understand how Japanese have succeeded in developing a modern and stable society in a group of small islands with few natural resources. Japanese Business Culture and Practices briefly explains how these important segments are related to the business behavior of Japanese.

Japanese Business Culture and Practices makes me think about Japan and Japanese culture. This book will be an important reference for those who are going to start business negotiations with the Japanese as well as for those who go to Japan for other business and for other purposes.

Saburo Sato

Vice President,

Dean of the College of International Relations, and Professor,

Nihon University, Japan

Fall 2004, at the foot of Mt. Fuji

INTRODUCTION

The Japanese traditionally lived in close proximity to each other, whether living in small houses with paper walls, working together in rice fields, or protecting family and village members from dangerous natural and man-made environments. Further, living in a country the size of California, U.S.A. with only fifteen percent of the land capable of supporting agriculture (seventy percent of Japan's land mass is forested mountains), being able to work harmoniously with others has been critical (half of Japan's protein intake comes from fish, another crop demanding cooperation, often by whole villages). Without the safety valves of a frontier or emigration, the Japanese were forced to develop standards of behavior that de-emphasized the individual while emphasizing the importance of conformity to the group. The result was the development of formal ways of behavior (kata) forcing conformity to society A kata is a routine allowing people to interact smoothly. Face, or social honor or reputation, became supremely important, and everyone learned to control any emotion and behavior threatening social harmony.

The comment "Our etiquette begins with learning how to hold a fan and ends with the rites for committing suicide" implies that all of a Japanese's life is ordered and defined by kata (nouns do not indicate plurality in the Japanese language). This book describes and explains the kata of

the business-related parts of modern Japanese society. An intuitive translation of this term is "good manners." This book also describes the common etiquette that allows business dealings with Japanese to go smoothly. No one can possibly succeed when conducting business with Japanese unless he or she is familiar with the kata that are in force today.

The Japanese themselves study closely their customers' cultures and customs, including their languages. Proper etiquette toward one another and strangers is paramount and expected of everyone. Etiquette in Japan, however, means more than a set of loosely defined behaviors such as how to eat properly or how to greet and thank a person. More formal than societies in the west, Japanese culture contains strict expectations for proper behavior. In the past people were told how to dress, how to sit, and how to address various ranks; samurai warriors could instantly behead those who did not conform to these and many other standards of behavior. It was safer to conform to many kata by following custom and the expectations of the powerful.

While contemporary Japan does not demand as much conformity as in the past, Japanese still have fewer choices in how to behave than citizens in the U.S. These kata, or social forms or formulas, so permeate the society that it is hard to imagine a Japanese not behaving in terms of his culture's kata. There remains much conformity in Japan today, though most contemporary Japanese do not know how to wrap a gift properly or hold a fan correctly. Increasingly, more Japanese expect to change employers several times during their working lives when once the ideal employment offered complete job security until retirement.

Both authors of this book are left-handed. Yet

both write and eat using their right hands. They write with their right hands because both grew up in very kata-oriented societies, in this case Japan and France. Both hold their chopsticks and forks in their right hands when they eat because, again, kata demands holding eating utensils with one's right hand. Using the left hand in Japan is considered impolite. Such distinctions are less important now but good manners nevertheless demand holding one's chopsticks with the right hand. Parents and mentors taught each author to eat in the prescribed manner. French and Japanese schools both teach students to write right-handed. In such countries, conformity to meurs (French for "customs") and kata is more important than the comfort or wishes

of individuals.

The Japanese educational system is an example of the emphasis placed on conforming to kata expectations. School rules regulate clothing and hair length as well as what students are allowed to do outside of school. A teacher might visit a student's parents in their homes to report their son has been seen in shops not considered respectable for a student of that particular school. Many students in kindergarten through high school wear the appropriate school uniforms and carry the correct book bags. They are taught to be polite and respectful towards teachers and adults in general. Younger students often distribute school lunches to their classmates; students then take turn by classes to sweep the schoolyard and classrooms. Teachers often punish the unacceptable behavior of individuals by punishing every member of the

group.

In the same manner, Japanese adults seek

direction from others, especially from their group leaders as well as their peers. A typical U.S. American leader might say, "I don't care how you do it; just get it done." By contrast, a Japanese leader is more likely to say, "Don't do it unless you do it in the right way (kata]." This general attitude stressing conformity is more likely as the Japanese population ages and the average worker becomes older and more conservative. In many ways, doing things the "correct" way is more important than doing something successfully, or in a newer, more efficient manner. While younger Japanese workers are less conservative and have become slightly more individualistic, those in higher management- promoted primarily because of their seniority and acceptance of traditional kata- remain traditional in attitude and behavior. These older leaders will expect foreigners to know Japanese kata-related business behavior.

Japanese learned to act in formal ways in the past and they continue to act in prescribed patterns during the twenty-first century; a person acting against the interests of the group or in nonconformist ways is said to be "immature," as if he or she does not yet know proper etiquette. This book explains the kata of Japanese business including how to start a speech, how to exchange business cards, how to drink, how to write e-mails, and how business meetings are conducted.

Japanese expect foreigners to behave in the Japanese manner. Those who do not follow the strict rules of Japanese society risk being defined as immature or ridiculous, or worse, as insulting. The chances of successfully conducting business in Japan or with Japanese expatriates throughout the world decrease when non-Japanese do not follow

the basic rules of Japanese behavior (Japan External Trade Organization [JETROL, 1999). Further, Japanese feel that following kata forms of behavior is a sign of goodwill and respect for Japanese culture and Japanese counterparts. Those who adapt to some extent to the Japanese ways of behavior are more likely to be successful when conducting business with Japanese than those who do not. As important is knowing why Japanese executives and negotiators behave the way they do.

Total conformity to one's society or groups is impossible even among the Japanese. Often one has to decide which kata to observe and which others to ignore. This book, however, presents the most prevalent expectations of behavior that reflect the Japanese national character. The knowledge of how --and why-Japanese businessmen behave in the way they do is central to any successful business dealing with the Japanese.

Japanese have transformed their society three times: once when influenced by Chinese culture; the second time when Commodore Perry and his U.S. Navy "black ships" forced Japan to deal with the outside world; and the third time after the Japanese experienced the loss of the World War II, the post-war "years of the hungry cage," and Allied Occupation. Japan has also experienced the development and loss of a colonial empire, rapid industrialization, urbanization, the introduction of democracy and land reform, and the development of a modern mass society.

Japan is currently going through more changes due to shifting demographic and economic conditions, and may be entering the beginning of Japan's fourth transformation. Due to population decline, Japanese companies are forced to further

expand their overseas markets. Japan is also facing new economic competitors, namely China, South Korea, and perhaps Vietnam and southeast Asia in general. In the next few decades, Japan may undergo major changes in the rules and systems associated with work and civil life in general.

Moreover, we believe Japan is prepared to become a global leader during its next economic cycle. Its educational system is modernizing, and most elementary school students will soon be taking mandatory English and computer programming classes. The quality of learning is high; PISA (Progamme for International Student Assessment) reports that, in 2015 in seventy-two countries, Japan ranked second (U.S.: 25) in science literacy and fifth (U.S.: 40) in mathematics. Interestingly, Japanese students self-reported that none had missed a school day in the two weeks prior to the PISA tests.

Japan's scientific institutions are in the world's top rankings. In 2014, Japanese applied for 325,986 patents, ranking third among the world's nations (U.S.: 578,802; China: 928,177). In addition, twenty Japanese residing in Japan have been awarded Nobel prizes in the sciences since 1970. Japanese scientists have been awarded Nobel prizes in sciences during the years 2010, 2012, 2014, 2015, and 2016. Japanese companies have continued to produce world-class products and are at the cutting edge in a number of technologies, including robotics, consumer electronics, transportation (including high speed "bullet trains"), next- generation vehicle development, life sciences, and clinical medicine. Japanese agriculture continues to be one of the world's most efficient.

Contemporary Japan is experiencing numerous

problems and changing demands associated with its declining birth rate and a corresponding increasing older population. At present, Japan's median age is 46.3 years old (U.S.: 37.8). Older leaders in business and government may become too conservative to adapt adequately to foreign competition. In addition, increasingly more resources must be devoted to taking care of the growing elderly (and non-working) populations instead of improving Japan's infrastructure and its educational and other institutions.

Families have become smaller as the nuclear family is becoming more common, resulting in weakened human relations in families and neighborhoods. More of the elderly are living alone or in retirement homes, and some have recently been found dead alone in their homes, a radically new situation that is becoming more common. Decreasing birth rates during the last twenty years will cause increasing labor shortages, forcing companies to improve their work conditions (including less expected conformity) to attract increasingly scarce younger workers. One non- traditional practice is to hire more women in more responsible positions. Career women are marrying later, or not at all, further forcing changes in the Japanese society.

When labor is scarce, large companies begin interviewing and hiring a year or so before the school year ends. Traditionally, selection of new hires began a few months before graduation. The term used to denote recruits hired a year or so before graduation is aota-gai, or "harvesting green plants." The reality of fewer young adults entering the labor force, a pattern already begun, will force more changes in the society and in large

corporations.

Companies have begun to shorten working hours and the amount of unpaid overtime while trying to make managers more productive, effective, and hopefully, more adaptable. Some service sectors such as restaurants and department stores (still very popular among Japanese) are no longer open 24 hours a day, and even give workers time off during the workweek. During the collapse of the "bubble economy" and its resulting recession of the 1990s-2010s, Japanese questioned some of their society's work-related kata, such as the negative consequences of wa (social harmony), girl (debt of gratitude), and enryo (self-reserve or restraint).

As the Japanese economy rebounds, however, there has been an emergence of acceptance of more traditional modes of behavior. Managers increasingly realize that deep, total relationships with coworkers and supervisors are indeed valuable and result in more productive workplaces. In essence, the efforts to become more western (especially U.S. American) in terms of work protocols have been reversed and business behavior has returned to more traditional kata.

The Japanese are becoming more traditional in many ways, with the addition of continuing to learn from the west ("Japanese spirit; western machines"). Even though Japanese work kata have changed to some degree (including a greater desire for more leisure away from work), knowledge of the traditional ways Japanese conduct business is still necessary for foreigners.

A reason for presenting a second, revised edition of this book is that we expect a renewed interest in how to deal with Japanese within a changing work environment. While the core of Japanese business-

related behavior has not changed greatly, there has been a certain amount of change in values and behavior in the last decade or more. This second edition deals with the way contemporary Japanese work, including the changes that have occurred.

Japanese on the individual level have shown great originality and an entrepreneurial spirit during periods of national stress. Akio Morita, founder of Sony, Inc., is typical of many postwar Japanese entrepreneurs. Morita lived in the U.S. in order to learn the U.S. American culture and how to market to U.S. consumers. He saw the possibilities of the transistor when few others did and, in 1957, produced the first transistor radio for the market. Under his leadership, Sony, Inc. produced a remarkable series of electronic products. Sony was the first Japanese corporation to internationalize by expecting all of Sony's executives to learn English. During the 1980s, the junior author found that most of the upper- and middle-level Sony executives he met spoke a high level of English.

Japanese society is now less group-oriented than in the past; many younger Japanese find the traditional levels of conformity inhibiting and uncomfortable. Nevertheless, there continues to be a more traditional "Japanese way of doing business" that must be understood by those wishing to conduct business with Japanese. Japanese society is not as conformist as it used to be. No one today is expected to commit ritual suicide because of a personal failure. Mothers no longer tie pairs of chopsticks to their children's hands so that the utensils are held in the proper fashion. Yet it is still important to know how to bow, how to entertain, how to pay respect to senior officials, and other kata forms of behavior.

It is tempting to feel that "business is business" throughout the world and that cultural differences are secondary elements when conducting business. Nothing could be more mistaken, even when a business man or woman speaks English as a second language. Nihonteki keiei, or Japanese-style management, is different from the U.S. American- style of management. Japanese businessmen are aware of these cultural differences and they are willing to learn the culture-specific practices of their customers. A Japanese who was asked which was the most important business language answered, "My customer's." By the same token it is critical to learn how the Japanese conduct business and which cultural values influence their business- related behavior.

We do not recommend that U.S. Americans become Japanese. Such attempts will fail. Instead, we suggest learning the basic facts of Japanese etiquette. Businesspersons will feel more comfortable when they can better (though not completely) fit in with their Japanese colleagues. Following Japanese etiquette also has the benefit of being polite so that foreign guests do not accidentally insult or disgust their hosts. In addition, an awareness of Japanese customs allows better understanding of their behavior. Misunderstandings are less likely when Japanese behave in traditionally Japanese patterns, as when a Japanese negotiator says "yes" but actually means "no." Seeing things through others' eyes also offers a valued perspective for understanding the behavior of Japanese business colleagues.

This work presents information on Japanese culture and society as well as how to behave in a business context. Many business-oriented customs

are meaningless unless the actor understands their cultural contexts. It is easier to behave

appropriately when that behavior is understood in its cultural context.

This book stresses the importance of conforming to Japanese expectations. At a minimum, foreigners wishing to work with Japanese must know why the Japanese behave as they do; otherwise it will be impossible to understand their behavior. Michihiro Matsumoto, in his book The Unspoken Way, describes Japanese society as a natto society. Natto is a sticky paste-like composition of fermented soybeans. The soybeans are similar to each other as are the Japanese; there is great pressure to think collectively, to behave collectively, and to work collectively. More important, though, is the fact that the beans in a natto mass form a mass connected by viscid strings. If a string is broken and an individual soybean is separated from the mass, the bean quickly rots and begins to die. In the same way, this book discusses the many ways Japanese are connected to one another and how they have developed numerous mannerisms, strategies, and behaviors to link individuals to their groups. The saying "Seek shelter under the largest tree" suggests the importance of social relationships and the need to be part of the most powerful groups possible. Although Japanese folklore (and movies) contains numerous individual heroes, in practice, the belief is that individuals are too weak to stand alone. On the other hand, there is also the belief that individuals working together can achieve more than when acting alone. Soichiro Honda, founder of Honda Motor Corporation, Ltd., stressed the need of all workers cooperating together for the good of the group, in this case Honda Motor. He once

recommended (Schnapp, 1982):

There is a limit to what can be thought out by big shots sitting at their desks. Where 100 people think, there are 100 powers; if 1,000 people think, there are 1,000 powers.

Our focus is describing the behavior of middle-level and senior-level corporate workers. These white- collar workers, called sarariiman or salarymen (or less often, "worker bees"), are the Japanese foreigners are most likely to interact with, sit in meetings with, socialize and drink with, and negotiate contracts.

We offer the reader a number of concepts in Japanese; those wishing to deal with Japanese businessmen (many corporate Japanese dealing. with foreigners are males) can become familiar with them since they appear in both the literature and in business-related behaviors. Those who are uncomfortable with the Japanese forms of these words can ignore them. We also use the term "U.S. American" to denote businesspersons from the United States of America. The term "North American" is too imprecise and describes Canadians and Mexicans as well as those from the U.S. The term "American" properly characterizes anyone native to the multi-ethnic American continent and is too broad for our purposes.

The junior author has studied Japanese culture for over thirty-five years (including an inadequate year of Japanese language classes), and his admiration for Japan's cultural achievements (in business, arts, science, etc.) continues to grow. The senior author has spent over a decade studying both Japanese and U.S. American cultures. Both authors remain impressed by Japan's becoming the second largest

economy of the world during the 1960s through the 2000s.

Japan is now the world's third largest economy (no mean feat for a country with a population of 127 million (U.S.: 323 million), and we expect Japan to continue influencing the world in many cultural, artistic, scientific, environmental, and business-related areas. The need to understand Japanese culture and its business world continues, and will become even more important in the near future. This book describes and analyzes the kata of Japanese business. Both authors are professional sociologists, and our focus is the way (and why) Japanese salarymen behave the way they do. We offer practical advice to foreigners, westerners in particular, on how to successfully conduct business in all of its aspects, including etiquette and business-related social behavior.

1

JAPANESE CULTURE

1.1. The Significance of Belonging

The junior author once observed younger salarymen lined up outside an office building. It seems that part of that day was given to measuring recruits for business suits being tailored for them at subsidized prices. Recruits were taught the correct attire as members and representatives of their company while the company provided the appropriate clothing. They were also given haircuts that conformed to company's standards.

Japanese companies can be understood as large families or clans (extended families) in which members share a common identity. The fact that roughly thirty percent of all employees of large corporations found their jobs through personal contacts suggests the importance of personal recommendations and connections prior to employment. Often a company hires graduating students recommended by one specific professor or by a more senior employee. Khalid Mehtabdin

(1986) reports whoever introduced a potential recruit to recruiters almost always becomes that recruit's long-term mentor and is in turn responsible for his behavior throughout his working career. The senior mentor, along with higher-level managers, teaches the new employee to engage in "appropriate behavior," including how to dress, how to drink, and how to work well with others. When hired, the relationship is total and the person is trained to identify with the goals of his or her employer if he or she is to be successful. Even when not at work, an employee represents his company, and his behavior reflects on his employer. He is never "off-duty."

Recruits do not select an employer because of the attractiveness of a specific position. Recruits are asked to join the company as a whole and they are seldom hired for a specific set of responsibilities. The first years of employment are used to discover where the recruit can best be used and where he will be the most effective. Thus, recruits join a corporate "family." On-the-job training and frequent job rotations develop the recruit's talents and determine where he should be permanently placed in the company, although personal preferences and technical skills are also considered. The individual

learns to adapt to the group rather than the reverse. Masami Ishida, former president of the Idemitsu Petroleum Company, stressed this view of the corporation as family (cited in Alston, 1986: 242):

The philosophy of Idemitsu is, to put it briefly, application of the Japanese home life to enterprise. Since the first day of our business we have endeavored to bring our employees up men of good character, because they were

left to us by their parents... [E]ven during the very poor years after World War II we put the priority on the construction of dormitories and company-houses for them. Our company is our

home.

The more successful Japanese companies treat their employees as valued parts of a community. Traditionally and today, corporations ignore shareholders and their needs while focusing on developing and maintaining the loyalty of personnel at all levels. Japanese managers assume that loyal workers who feel part of the company will work harder to become more productive. As a result, Japanese managers see their major task as development of loyalty and workers' identification with the company. Only then, they believe, can workers-both manual and non-manual-achieve quality work in an efficient manner and take an interest in what they are producing to achieve the goals of the company.

This sense of loyalty is much more extensive than is found in the U.S. The Japanese word "marugakae" or "total embrace" refers to the notion that work-related relationships are total; companies expect total loyalty and dedication while the employee expects the company will provide a wide range of material and psychological resources. Japanese employees ask superiors for advice about. their personal problems while employers provide clothing, food, housing, athletic programs, and more for their employees. A bucho (team leader) may even suggest it is time for an employee to marry. The company would in this case help pay the cost of a matchmaker if necessary, pay in part for the wedding, and the bucho will most likely become

the godfather of the first child.

The core value in Japanese culture is a concern for group harmony. This cannot be achieved unless leaders concern themselves with the feelings of their groups' members. Members' feelings (ninjo) are given precedence over profit and short-term efficiency. In return for management's solicitude, subordinates offer loyalty and dedication to the goals of the corporation. One major aspect of concern for others' ninjo is avoiding criticism and talking as little as possible (De Mente, 2012: 32). Westerners often make the mistake of talking too much when dealing with Japanese colleagues. They also ignore the fact that much is left unsaid until rapport has been established. Often, westerners do not realize that Japanese feel uncomfortable openly criticizing others or saying "no." Concern for ninjo demands listening carefully to what is not being said as well as what is being said.

Forty-three percent of Japanese males aged 30-34 are unmarried, as compared to twenty-seven percent of U.S. American males of the same age. These figures suggest that Japanese males delay marriage until they have completed their education and worked for eight years or so. They are too busy proving themselves to their employers to marry. In addition, entry-level salaries are inadequate to support a family and rent an apartment in urban areas. Corporate employees are considered as apprentices for the first few years of employment. During this period, the new employee learns to identify closely with his work groups and his superiors until he has little sense of identity outside of work.

Rosalie Tung (1984) offers an extreme example of loyalty to one's employer. She summarizes an

earlier Japanese magazine account of a woman who died alone of pneumonia because her husband would not leave his office. The husband worked for a Japanese government ministry and was too busy meeting an important deadline to be at his wife's deathbed. The article noted positively on the husband's job loyalty and mentioned that similar examples were not unusual. Such behavior would be less frequent today, especially among younger workers, but the behavior would be respected and understood by older workers. In a similar manner, overworked employees have few alternatives except to endure or to commit suicide. Work- related suicides, called karo-jisatsu, have increased in numbers in recent years. In one instance, a 1997 suicide aged twenty-four had averaged thirty minutes to two hours of sleep a night and had not had a single day off in almost two years.

U.S. Americans tend to treat Japanese business acquaintances as individuals. This is a major error causing embarrassment to the Japanese involved. It is more productive and correct to treat Japanese as members of one group or another. For example, the offer, "Please write to me if you need more information" is too individualistic. A better way of making the same request is to say, "Please write to me on behalf of your company or your superior if we can supply you with more information." As discussed in Chapter 3, the most influential example of the importance of belonging is the practice of lifetime employment, stated as nenko (seniority) and shu-shin koyo (lifetime employment). Under this system, newly hired employees are told they now "belong" to the company much in the same way a person belongs to a family or nation. They are also likely to be given

promotions and more responsibilities as they age and gain more experience.

An additional example of the importance of "belonging" to a corporation is the company union. Labor unions in Japan form an integral part of the companies. Labor unions in Japan are enterprise unions in which each company establishes a union independent from all others. There are a few labor unions based on occupation such as those for teachers and railroad workers with members from more than one school or corporation. Junior managers, as well as manual workers, are expected to become active enterprise union members, and sixteen percent of all senior executives have served as union leaders during the first part of their careers (Kono and Clegg, 2001). It is almost obligatory for junior executives to be members of their companies' enterprise labor unions for a few years.

Since the enterprise labor union prospers or fails according to the fortunes of the company, union members feel responsible for the success of the corporation, and little adversarial antagonism exists between organized labor and management. In a real sense, enterprise unions and their members belong to the organization, and union leaders commonly contribute proposals resulting in greater productivity and long-term strategies. Managers on their part inform unions of any possible changes in policy and will ask for union input and agreement. Policies completely rejected by unions would not be adopted as this would threaten management-worker harmony.

Another policy Japanese companies promote in order to encourage a sense of belonging among their personnel is the bonus plan. Workers receive a bonus of up to one-third of their basic pay twice

a year at the end of the year and during the summer. The sizes of these bonuses are based on the profitability of the company. These bonuses, besides giving the Japanese economy a boost every six months, remind the employee that his/her fortune depends on his (and increasingly hers) and the company's performance. Pay is also based on seniority rather than individual performance, and workers receive pay raises based in large part on the length they have been (belonged to) members of the company (Alston, 1990). This system penalizes younger workers who may be more productive (especially in technical areas) than those more senior, but the former realizes that their loyalty, over time, will be rewarded if they remain part of the corporate community/family.

Merit pay based on individual performance has become more common in recent years. A study by the Japan Productivity Center for Socio-Economic Development of three-hundred and thirty-nine companies indicated that sixty percent of managers and thirty percent of non-managers were being paid at least in part according to merit. However, since merit pay demands people are rewarded in terms of individual performances, many Japanese corporations have not yet installed comprehensive standards measuring individual productivity. The increasing number of those whose pay is based partly on merit suggests that the policy of automatic pay raises according to seniority is becoming less prevalent than before, although it continues to be a major part of the average Japanese corporate worker's income.

Japanese work and socialize together and become familiar with one another; this reinforces the need for politeness since colleagues work together

until retirement. Making an enemy might result in working next to a disliked and hostile colleague for decades. It is safer to stay on good terms with every person one meets since the chances are that acquaintances are permanent. The result is a reliance on proper etiquette and social rituals to treat everyone in the correct, prescribed manner. While workers within a team develop strong friendships, this solidarity can be used to the workers' disadvantage. Managers can motivate workers by threatening to transfer individuals because leaving one's group and having to adjust to another group is a difficult experience for any Japanese; the threat of transfer is a strong motivator to work hard and fit harmoniously within the workgroup.

Then too, long-term associations result in knowing others and their personal characteristics thoroughly. Japanese business colleagues understand one another very well and there is often little need to use extensive verbalization to communicate. By contrast, U.S. Americans seem to Japanese to talk too much and lack the maturity to understand what is expressed through minimal communication. The Japanese motto "Hear one, understand ten" reflects this preference for using fewer words among those who understand one

another.

Japanese understand work as a team effort to such an extent that a worker does not want to be the first to leave work. Most try to leave as a group so no one feels ashamed of seeming to work less than the others. If a worker must leave before the others he is likely to say "Osakini shitsurei shimasu," which means, "Please allow me to leave earlier than you." This is the equivalence of "Goodbye." The

person's team workers respond by saying "Otsukare sama deshita," translated as "You have worked hard enough" or "You can call it a day." It is important to always show willingness to work hard and to not disappoint team members.

The downside of the emphasis on belonging is fear of not being accepted. Rejection by one's group members is a major form of punishment for a person guilty of nonconformity. Group members expect loyalty and total dedication to the group's goals. On the other hand, the group is expected to take care of loyal individual members. This reciprocity of benefits forges tightly knit groups which in turn can become highly productive and efficient. In the past nonconformist members of a village or urban neighborhood were shunned and ostracized by fellow members. This act, called mura hachibu, is found in contemporary Japan among those in workplaces. A co-worker who has make a serious error or shown a lack of loyalty is avoided by his peers; people will even hesitate to sit next to him during a meeting.

An example of the strength of mura hachibu is found in the experiences of Masabumi Hosono, who was on the Titanic when it sank in 1912

(Sato, Takei, and Alston, 2003). During the event, a number of male passengers gave their lifeboat seats to women and children. One male passenger said, "We've dressed our best and are prepared to die like gentlemen." This first-class passenger, Benjamin Guggenheim, and his manservant, changed into formal evening wear and prepared to drown rather than take women's lifeboat spaces. Masabumi Hosono was the only Japanese passenger on the Titanic. He managed to find space on a lifeboat and was later saved by a passing ship. Both the U.S.

American and Japanese presses accused Hosono of cowardice. It was felt in Japan that Hosono should have adhered to the "women and children first" standard as did other men. Hosono himself wrote in his diary "My decision [to jump into a lifeboat] saved my life-yet it brought me decades of shame in Japan." Because people believed that Hosono had pushed aside several passengers to get into a lifeboat, Japanese textbooks characterized him as a coward. He lost his civil servant position and lived a life of shame until his death.

Most large companies continue to maintain dormitories for recruits and subsidized cafeterias for workers and junior managers. Workers who join a company are assigned rooms in corporate dormitories for several years. Since housing is both scarce and expensive in Japan, this practice is considered a valuable perk for any young person. Dormitories are located near the workplace and new members save both money and commuting time by living in a corporate dormitory. Dormitory life promotes group orientation and a sense of belongingness. Former dormitory roommates and floormates remain friends for life and the dormitory experience gives workers a variety of friends throughout the company during their working years. Companies organize weekend tours and athletic contests for recruits, and a large part of a new employee's life is restricted by training and company-sponsored leisure activities. These future managers and executives, much like military recruits, eat, sleep, and socialize together. They are also given lessons on how to entertain clients, how to dress, etc.

Japanese corporations also provide cafeterias for their employees. These often offer a choice of

cuisines such as Chinese and western in addition to Japanese. Higher-level executives are expected to eat alongside their subordinates from time to time (though not necessarily at the same tables) to show solidarity. The foods in these company cafeterias are subsidized and served quickly so workers can eat cheaply or at no cost and then quickly go back to work. They are also open for workers' breaks and offer beverages and snacks throughout the day.

Many corporations maintain cemeteries of their own or else reserve sections of public cemeteries for deceased employees. Increasingly, fewer contemporary adults identify with a village or clan as was common in the past and no longer have access to centuries-old family cemeteries. In pre-modern times, extended families and villages maintained private cemeteries for their members, though such ties have almost disappeared in contemporary Japan. A consequence to this social isolation is cemeteries for deceased employees and the link between corporation and corporate members continues after death. In such cemeteries, the president of the company visits the corporate plots on an annual basis. He addresses the dead and tells them of the past year's successes and failures. The president apologizes if corporate goals were not met and he promises to work harder. Before he leaves, he thanks the deceased and offers them a bow, incense, flowers, and sake. The former employees become ancestors of the corporation and remain part of the corporate family after death.

New employees are shown the familial interests their superiors have in them during the first formal event they experience as members. New permanent employees attend a formal banquet during which they are formally recognized as members of

the corporate family (Rohlen, 1974). The former recruits sign a document stating that they pledge to obey the rules of the company and accept its expectations as their own. There are no specific lists of obligations or rules and the new members do not yet know where they will be assigned. The banquet continues with a speech by a representative of the parents of the new members. He tells them to work hard and obey their superiors. He essentially hands over the recruits to the company and tells them to obey the company as they obeyed their parents. There will be additional speeches by the president and other high-ranking members to welcome the new members, describe their new "family" in general terms, and urge them to work hard for the good of the company, its customers, and the Japanese nation. The new members are thoroughly placed into the corporate family and their transition from student/child to worker/adult is complete.

The Japanese accept that being members of a work group in Japan entails numerous costs. Members eat and drink together and give each other gifts upon promotion, a son's or daughter's entry into a prestigious university, weddings, and other occasions. Members of a group are expected to see each other frequently and they seldom develop close friendships outside of these groups. These social costs, called ko-sai hi, are the responsibility of the individual. These are necessary. to lubricate social interaction. The costs of dealing with outsiders are paid for by the corporation though there are always personal costs involved in entertaining, returning home, etc. that are not included in expense accounts. These are considered one's responsibility and as the cost of belonging.

Groups offer more than identity, welfare, and protection to their members. Groups also offer honor and respect, or kao. Kao includes one's social reputation and honor, as well as a person's sense of self-worth. Japanese have adopted numerous kata to avoid humiliating others. By contrast, causing someone to lose his kao (face) cannot easily be forgiven, if at all. The need for group harmony forces Japanese to work very hard to maintain their own and others' kao. One tactic that maintains group harmony is collective decisions since if a proposal fails there is no one individual to blame. U.S. Americans pinpoint the cause of a problem to blame an individual for a problem. The Japanese way is to avoid shaming individuals and change the system to make certain no one will make the same mistake again. Concern for others' face, social honor, reputation, or kao is paramount.

The importance placed on kao cannot be understated. Foreigners from more individualistic cultures should not blame Japanese individuals for work-related errors. Japanese know who is to blame for a mistake, but are reluctant to openly criticize an individual. The warning to a team, "We must try to come to work on time" allows the offender to maintain his kao while criticizing him indirectly.

U.S. Americans focus on the individual and

commonly praise an individual by saying, "I've heard good things about you." By contrast a Japanese is more likely to say, "Your company has a good reputation. It manufactures many excellent products." Japanese are impressed when someone shows knowledge and appreciation of the person's company, its products, and its history. People exist through their groups and one's self-identity in Japan is extremely group-oriented. When asked

what he (or she) does, an employee of Toyota is likely to state, "I am Toyota." He then may add that he is an engineer or an accountant but the first and major source of self-reference is one's employer. Because Japanese identify so strongly with their employers, praising a company is the same as praising its employees.

The family of salarymen takes second place to their employers and work groups. Japanese husbands and fathers are often absent family members. They leave for work early (often by seven a.m. because of long commutes), they come home late, and have little time for family activities except during two days a week. A supervisor may ask a salaryman to work for a period of time in another city (or country), perhaps for the experience, while his family stays where they are already located. Parents may not want to move because schools are inferior in the new location or the assignment is temporary. The term for husbands living apart from their families because of work demands is tanshinfunin, or "husbands without families."

1.2. The On Society

Japanese society revolves around the concept of social debt. There are many terms for a social debt but the most prevalent are on and giri. On is the feeling that an obligation exists when a person has performed a favor for someone else. Westerners easily understand this concept of the morality of repaying debts. However, Japanese values stress a deed entailing on cannot ever be completely repaid. The receiver becomes a permanent debtor to the donor. This awareness of unending debt makes Japanese hesitate to help strangers because such

acts tie persons closely together. The behavior that results in a feeling of on is not understood to be ordinary behavior as when a person lends a friend money for lunch or a drink. On is based on exceptional behavior. The first on Japanese individuals experience is the feeling toward parents. The parents have given life to a child and nurtured her during infancy and during later years. This behavior cannot be completely repaid since there are no equivalent deeds to the giving of birth and years of caring. As a result, the bond between Japanese and parents is especially close and stronger than the husband-wife bond. While less expected today than in the past, an adult male should ideally choose his parents' needs over his wife's. There are numerous traditional stories of the strength of the parent-child bonds over all others in Japanese mythology.

Deeds resulting in a feeling of on are those that are difficult to repay because they are in essence beyond price. Someone who helps a Japanese enroll in a university or finds him a scholarship places the father and student in his debt. An extreme example of an on debt is saving someone's life. It is important to try to help Japanese colleagues in the hopes that this behavior entails a feeling of on of one degree or another.

While on refers to the feeling that a favor entails a debt that cannot be easily repaid, giri refers to debts a person owes because of his position in society or group. A person owes giri to his employer or team supervisor solely because of the difference in rank. A similar belief exists in the west in the relationship of a soldier to his or her officers. This giri-type debt is discharged by working hard, by showing respect to superiors, and by being a cooperative co-

worker. Japanese merchandisers feel a giri type of debt to their customers; this is why Japanese sales clerks will welcome customers and thank them for their business. Clerks often give customers an omake, or gift, to thank them for being a customer. An omake is usually a small object given as a way of repaying a customer's debt for making a purchase. A man buying several shirts may be given several handkerchiefs as an omake. A woman buying vegetables may be given a few fruits or extra vegetables for the same reason.

Customers may also receive gifts when there have been disruptions in the buyer-seller relationships. Defective products will often result in each. customer being given a small gift as an apology. In such cases, the gift may be a small amount of gift certificates for their products. A faulty small appliance-a fan-was once sold during a sale. The sales staff, at the end of the work day, located all buyers and were sent to the clients' homes to replace the appliance and offer a small gift as an apology. It was expected the customers would. return to the store as a gesture of thanks for being treated courteously. Apologies and small gifts are part of a continual cycle of debts that need to be repaid.

In the past, Japanese women owed giri debts to parents and spouses and to all males in general. Their relative subordination demanded that they behave in subordinate kata to males. One way this giri was discharged was the development of a female form of speaking that was extremely submissive in form. Women used speech forms when speaking to males that were very polite and submissive. This tradition has been greatly weakened but many contemporary Japanese

women still hesitate to criticize males or show them anything less than complete respect, whether warranted or not.

1.3. The Japanese View of Time

Japanese businessmen are recognized for meeting delivery deadlines and for being on time for meetings. By contrast, Japanese are very slow in making decisions. This slowness stems from a number of sources. There is delay because Japanese want to learn as much as possible about an issue. Japanese are more holistic than U.S. Americans and believe more facts are associated together. While a westerner may be satisfied an issue involves only A, B, C, and D, the Japanese counterpart wants to also include E, F, G, and H in his analysis. This stems in part from the Japanese concern for details. Japanese are very detail-oriented and believe no issue has been thoroughly studied unless all relevant and quasi-relevant (to westerners except for Germans and Swedes) details have been included. Time spent on analysis is never wasted since any irrelevancies might be useful eventually. The Japanese educational system prepares students for concern for details. Education is in large part the memorization of facts and whoever knows more facts is considered the superior student. Be careful: Japanese remember facts and anything said during a meeting will be recorded and remembered!

Japanese also prefer to plan extensively to avoid any surprises, an attitude defined as uncertainty avoidance (see section 1.11 below). They tend to be pessimistic about the future and feel that if anything can go wrong, it will. People who have suffered centuries of civil war, earthquakes and

typhoons, fire-destroying cities, and hunger have learned to be both cautious and pessimistic. They prefer to avoid possible future crises through careful planning rather than meet unanticipated crises head on as do the more optimistic U.S. Americans. Then too, a crisis due to a lack of planning is seen as blameworthy and an indication of personal weakness or ineptitude. Many business crises, it is felt, can be avoided with foresight and planning. A crisis due to lack of planning may result in loss of face and a superior's confidence in the person responsible. These are reasons enough to encourage Japanese to plan ahead very, very carefully and in minute detail.

Anthropologists Edward T. Hall and Mildred Reed Hall (1990) note that cultures are divided into monochronic and polychronic cultures. People in monochronic cultures feel there is never enough time to accomplish what needs to be done. They frown on people who are late or "waste time," Such cultures are time-starved while moving fast, being efficient, and meeting schedules are major virtues. By contrast, polychronic cultures are those in which people feel there is plenty of time to accomplish whatever needs to be done, as reflected i in the Mexican proverb, "Whoever made time made plenty of it." People in polychronic cultures find it easy to work slowly and carefully while meeting schedules is not usually a virtue. Japanese businessmen are polychronic enough to accept the notion that there is plenty of time to plan and made careful decisions. In addition, Japanese do not feel that schedules are significant if they interfere with the flow of work, and they are very likely to continue a productive meeting even though the end of the workday is approaching. The wise foreigner

avoids scheduling meetings late in the afternoon if he wants the workday to end at the usual time. If a meeting is going well during a Friday afternoon, the Japanese negotiators may ask to continue the meeting on Saturday or into the evening. They may even ask a business associate to dine informally with them that evening. No business will be discussed during the dinner but the Japanese hosts may feel that the good feeling generated during the meeting should be cemented through socializing. A Japanese is quite able to invite someone to play golf because he has just learned of a foreign colleague's passion for golf and wants to socialize even if a day of negotiations must be re-scheduled for another day.

Such on-the-spot invitations should be accepted if at all possible. Japanese plan farther ahead than U.S. Americans and any disruptions of schedules demand considerable re-scheduling. There is a strong tendency for the Japanese to maintain a schedule in total detail. U.S. Americans also experience similar scheduling rigidity when being entertained. An invitation to go to a restaurant and to a bar for a few drinks means a schedule has been carefully drawn up and cannot be changed. By contrast, last-minute invitations disrupt schedules, but the Japanese are willing to re-schedule should they feel it is socially necessary. It is difficult to schedule many activities at the last minute, so such invitations are rare. The reason is an important one when a last-minute invitation is issued.

Being holistic, cautious, and polychronic, Japanese are very patient, plan long-term, and study all aspects of a problem no matter how long it takes. Such attitudes result in a major mistake when U.S. Americans deal with Japanese. The most

common mistake U.S. Americans make is being impatient. U.S. Americans expect leaders to make quick decisions; they also maintain strict schedules and act in "no nonsense" ways. In this cultural context, slow decision-making is seen as a character weakness or as inefficiency. By contrast, Japanese take time to thoroughly study a problem, slowly develop personal relationships with those they do business, and take as long as necessary to make decisions. Few actions more are counterproductive than trying to force Japanese to decide an issue quickly or showing impatience at the pace of negotiations.

In spite of the Japanese willingness to thoroughly study a problem and study potential business. partners, delivery dates are sacred and Japanese manufacturers enjoy the reputation of being strictly on time with their deliveries. By the same token, it is important to meet all agreed-upon deadlines because Japanese also expect delivery dates to be met. Though Japanese are flexible within a social context, they are inflexible when it comes to delivery deadlines.

For this reason, Japanese are nervous when dealing with U.S. Americans because the former believe US Americans do not plan thoroughly enough to meet all contingencies. It is common for Japanese partners to frequently ask about the progress of a contract to be reassured that all is going according to schedule. Robert Christopher (1986) suggests keeping Japanese partners very well informed as to the progress of a contract. He also notes that being on time for the first deliveries often will lead to more contracts because being on time is a final element in establishing trust for long- term business relationships. At times, Japanese will

agree to a small contract of little value to their company in order to test the business partners or in the hopes of increasing business dealings. The Japanese will later offer larger agreements when the first schedules are met and the foreign partner behaved in a trustworthy manner.

The longer-time horizon of Japanese businessmen causes misunderstandings to those with a shorter time horizon. A U.S. American working in a Japanese corporation was told he would get an increase in his salary "after a while." The Japanese understanding of this statement was that the employee would receive a pay raise in a few years; by contrast, the U.S. American thought the term referred to a few months. It is best for foreigners to ask for specifications when Japanese make forward- sounding statements such as "soon," "after a while," or "as soon as possible."

Part of this long-term time horizon mindset is based on several characteristics of Japanese society. Japan is a crowded nation and resources are often limited; Japanese must plan ahead because otherwise they will not gain the resources needed. A few years ago, Japanese banks developed the one- hundred-year home mortgage. Land is extremely expensive in Japan and most Japanese cannot afford to own their homes until they reach retirement age, if then. As a result, Japanese can now get a hundred-year mortgage that grandchildren will inherit. Japanese often must schedule vacations, golf tee times, tennis courts rentals, and restaurant reservations months ahead of time in order to attain reservations.

In addition, Japanese businessmen are patient and they do not insist that new ventures result in immediate profits. Japanese are polychronic in

that they believe that there is plenty of time for a venture to produce profits. A Japanese manager organizing a chain of sushi (see section 2.16. below) restaurants in Texas described his strategy to the junior author by saying that he expects a new sushi franchise to lose money for three years. The first three years of a sushi venture is seen as an investment for future long-term profits. This entrepreneur said that a business never fails after it attracts loyal customers. The franchise owners are expected to serve superior food even when operations run at a loss in order to build personal relationships with their employees and customers; loyal customers will then be attracted to the franchise and it will become a success. It is obvious that long-term planning is a core aspect of Japanese business strategy.

Japanese are polychronic when planning and making decisions. By contrast, they are extremely monochronic in terms of being on time. Since being late may be seen as an insulting act or as disinterest, meetings in Japan start on time (they may last longer than scheduled if there is a need or when making progress) and Japanese are likely to appear for a meeting or social appointment five to ten minutes ahead of time. Often, salarymen will wait outside a building and enter exactly at the scheduled time or a few minutes early. It is important to be punctual for any meeting or appointment. Expect business colleagues and guests to either be exactly on time or a little early. The fiscal year in Japan begins in the first of April. February and March are therefore very busy months for many Japanese as they close books, plan for the next year's budget, etc. It is best not to travel on business during February and March since many

Japanese are concerned with the end of the fiscal year. On the other hand, April and May are times when Japanese are optimistic and prefer to start

new ventures.

April 1 is also the day when all new hires for the year occur. These new graduates from high schools or universities have the same seniority, so all hires enjoy the same rank based on seniority. Indoctrination of new recruits takes place for three or four months, so many executives will be also busy mentoring new corporate members during the months of April through July.

1.4. The Search for Perfection (Kaizen)

Japanese are strongly motivated to achieve perfection, and failure is sometimes seen as achieving anything short of one hundred percent perfection. This attitude, known as the "zero defect" policy among Japanese manufacturers, is found in all business sectors, even in family-owned small businesses. The search for making a product or service better and cheaper is a core value. This attitude is similar to the Japanese fear of failure: an imperfect product or act is seen as at least a partial failure. This attitude makes Japanese manufacturers hesitant to offer products that are not as good as possible and they delay making them available to the public until their products perform and look as well as technically and visually possible ("Today is better than yesterday, and tomorrow better than today").

Japanese also assume any product is imperfect and can be improved if only to manufacture it more cheaply. Generally speaking, however, Japanese manufacturers have been able to increase

the quality of their products while at the same time decreasing their costs. Japanese customers on their part demand high quality products and "perfection." The preference for working toward product or service perfection (which includes design and packaging) is found throughout Japanese society and is reinforced by the demands of kata, or forms of behavior. U.S. Americans, on their part, are often satisfied with a product which is "as good as it needs to be" in terms of profitability or sales. They become impatient when Japanese delay decisions because the latter hope to make a specific product better.

This "zero tolerance" for defects of any type on the part of workers and managers reflects the value of komakai, or "every detail perfect." This practice includes everything being both as perfect as possible in quality and also more efficiently made or developed. By everything, we also mean all aspects of a product, including but not limited to the way a product is packaged. Products must both operate as advertised and appeal to customers ("the customer is king"). The products are to be "perfect," but those who make the product should act in the best possible way possible to ensure the success of their companies. An extreme example of this concern for perfect performance is a story, now part of Honda Motor mythology, of Soichiro Honda witnessing a worker tightening a bolt incorrectly (Sakita, 1982: 72). Honda picked up a wrench, re-tightened the bolt himself, and lightly hit the worker on the head saying, "You damned fool. This is how you're supposed to tighten bolts." This aggressive behavior would be impossible today, but the story illustrates the concern for quality performance.

The end result of kaizen is the search for of perfection through incremental improvements and is found throughout Japanese society. Schoolchildren are expected to memorize material in order to know a topic perfectly, products are expected to be wrapped in the correct manner; conforming to the kata of Japanese society reflects this cultural drive toward behaving in the right (i.e. perfectly). Inamori Kazuo (see section 3.11 below) summarized the emphasis on komakai to reach kaizen from manufacturing and manufacturing perspectives (cited in Azar, 2016:146):

Kyocera does not [accept] the notion that "very close is good enough" in management goals for order bookings, sales, production, hourly efficiency, or other areas. The statement "We didn't achieve 100%, but we did reach 99%, so let's go with that" is not acceptable. For production and sales targets, full accomplishment is required.

If it is agreed that workers need to stretch and perform light exercises before the workday begins, then a system of exercises will be developed. Honda Motor Company executives, with consultation with the company's workers' union, decided that many of the personnel worked too many hours and, as a result, spent too little time with their families, resulting in lower morale and work efficiency. Honda has now designated days, generally once a week, where work must stop early and everyone is expected to go home in time to share a meal with their families, even when there is work to complete. These days are called teiji no hi ("go home early"). This policy allows salarymen to go home at 5:40 p.m. or earlier instead of the usual several hours

later (Kriska, 2011: 193). Otherwise, some workers might work most or all night to complete a project!

A government panel has recently recommended a law limiting overtime to one hundred hours a month. Most salarymen work overtime without extra pay (they check out at the normal time and then continue working.). Someone who has worked until midnight or so would probably go to a hotel to sleep a few hours rather taking a long commute home. Commuter trains leave their home stations around midnight, depending on the line and route. While the average commute time in Tokyo is sixty minutes, half of commuters spend more than an hour commuting one way.

1.5. The Search for Harmony: Wa

Maintaining harmony (wa) among group members is so important that it dominates all business decisions. The term "wa" originally meant "circle" in that managers and lower-level workers must work together for the common good. In a circle, members of a corporation rely on one another for the good of the corporation. The term "circle" includes the expectation that workers will send management their suggestions for work improvement and that there is both bottom-up and top-down input. The term "wa" infers that bureaucracy and hierarchy are less important than cooperation and mutual support. An organization with high morale because of wa, it is believed, will always be more profitable than one with a lack of wa.

While wa is commonly defined as group morale, a near-equivalent term is the US. Marines' motto, "Gung Hof" translated as "work together," and "be enthusiastic." Note the motto is Asian in origin.

Soichiro Honda compared the differences in wa levels in Japanese and U.S. American factories in this manner (Schnapp, 1982):

The workshop should be a place where everybody finds a joy in working and earn his living. An organization that enforces monotonous labor and deprives the workers of the right to think may work well for a while but is bound to get decayed in the long-run. I noticed at auto factories in the U.S. that the working environment was bad. Decent people don't want to work at such places, and as a result the quality of labor at those workshops is poor.

Managers are promoted to important positions in large part because they promote wa among their team or division members. In such cases, technical expertise is less important than social skills. Japanese managers' main responsibility is to develop and maintain wa among colleagues and subordinates. Anyone who disturbs a group's wa is quickly shunned and will eventually be sent to another group. By contrast, anyone with the reputation of not supporting wa in a previous group will most likely be shunned by his new group. A reason why salarymen are polite, efficient and cooperative workers is that they do not wish to act in non-cooperative (ie, selfish) ways. Maintaining wa is central to understand the Japanese way of business.

It has been estimated that Japanese companies answer less than half of inquiries sent to them. It may be that an official does not understand the content of a letter and is too embarrassed to admit his ignorance. He sets aside the letter

and eventually "forgets" it. Since sending back a negative answer disturbs (wa), no answer is politer than a negative one from the Japanese perspective. Moreover, Japanese do not like to deal with strangers since no one knows what to expect or how to react to a letter from a stranger. It is more comfortable to say nothing than to say "no." Doing otherwise threatens wa even among strangers. More important, the start of the development of wa demands face-to-face interaction. If there is doubt that a business arrangement is wanted, the reaction among Japanese managers is generally to ignore requests from strangers by not answering their letters.

Maintaining wa demands praising the group rather than the individual. Japanese always praise the group even if only a single person was responsible for a success. The members of the group appreciate the one who was responsible and no one loses face when all members of the group are praised instead of one member. Rewarding an individual even with verbal praise embarrasses him, sets him apart, and threatens the group's wa because of jealousy. On the other hand, the reward for being a supporter of wa results in higher prestige. This value encourages higher productivity. Workers are discouraged to be competitive and "better" than other members of a work group. Workers who help their groups (and company) become successful while increasing their own prestige. They and their groups will be given more challenging responsibilities, and eventually be promoted to higher positions. No one will be promoted if not recognized as having the ability to enhance wa of his group.

The insistence of maintaining social harmony results in constantly demonstrating humility. Instead of declaring a direct "yes," a Japanese colleague says, "I think so," or "Maybe." These qualifiers are merely tokens of politeness and are not to be taken as hesitancy or uncertainty. In addition, direct questions are seen as too blunt and demanding. Often, Japanese colleagues avoid asking questions for fear they might seem too aggressive. By the same token, humility demands accepting partial responsibility when things have gone wrong. Apologizing makes the speaker appear honorable and group-oriented. Never criticize a subordinate in public; the group's leader is seen as responsible or else the system is at fault and must be changed.

It is best to assume Japanese speakers maintain harmony even when it is done indirectly. Any seeming directness or unusually blunt statements is most probably the result of a misunderstanding. as when a Japanese supervisor uses the phrase "Ganbatte kudasai" for "You must work harder." This is meant to convey the encouraging sentiment "Hang in there!" or "Persevere" rather than a reprimand.

An unfortunate result of the insistence for group harmony is the tendency to obey superiors when they are wrong. The group's leader is the symbol for the group and disagreement with a leader suggests disloyalty to the group. Japanese history contains many examples of feudal leaders testing their subordinates with impossible or irrational demands. In these cases, following orders was more important than criticizing official policy or rejecting inappropriate demands. On the other hand, accepting unreasonable demands from a

superior, such as unpaid overtime, excessive entertainment of potential clients, or even doing personal favors for a superior, places the superior and the company in the person's debt. That person will hopefully be rewarded eventually when evaluated as completely loyal and dependable.

The concern for wa begins when a person applies for work. Corporate recruiters believe that character, personality, and loyalty are the most important traits for recruits. Recruits are given a battery of tests to measure basic knowledge and personality traits; references are interviewed if thought to be necessary.
