However, knowledge and business skills are secondary since every Japanese corporation has extensive in-house training programs. In one survey, recruiters felt that personal interviews were central in importance. A majority of corporate officials wished to determine whether or not recruits would get along with coworkers and whether or not they would be loyal to work teams and corporation (Alston, 1986) rather than focusing on skills. The central issue was whether or not a recruit would help maintain the company's wa.

A rare exception to the above recruiting policy, was Soichiro Honda, founder of Honda Motor) Corporation, who broke with tradition and hired applicants based on their expertise and talents. He assumed that he could develop programs that would encourage new workers to develop group loyalty.

Maintaining wa is also the major responsibility of senior executives. The proverb "Management sits; it does not do" refers to the fact that consensus is the core responsibility of senior management as well as all supervisory personnel. Senior management does

direct and often initiates policy but an executive who does not maintain high morale among his personnel is considered a failure. Japanese developed a very harmonious society and the same is true of the workplace.

The way of achieving wa, from the Japanese perspective, is to encourage individuals to be concerned only for the well-being of the company and their work groups. Such persons are seen as unselfish and ideal members of their groups. They will be eventually rewarded by the group and company. The most common reward for unselfish behavior is the tradition of rewarding individuals according to seniority. Such persons will eventually be promoted to important positions or given total job security.

The concern for maintaining wa is also found in the practice of nemawashi ("root-binding"). Before a major decision is made, senior officials talk to their peers and subordinates on an individual basis to develop an informal consensus as to whether or not a proposal is acceptable. The senior official gets input and develops support for his plan. When everyone has indicated acceptance to the plan, a formal proposal is presented at a meeting and everyone present supports the proposed issue. By getting approval before a proposal is publicly discussed the senior official avoids public disagreements and maintains wa. If the initiator senses rejection or disapproval of his plan, he can either amend the plan to suit others or else discard the plan before resistance emerges. Nemawashi protects leaders from making decisions that might lower morale by causing internal disagreement.

This concern for corporate harmony can be further illustrated by the wage structure in

Japanese corporations. Wages are largely based on seniority, thereby decreasing the chances of competition among co-workers for higher pay. Promotion is also based on seniority until the mid-career stage. Corporate officials believe that basing wages on seniority decreases competition and avoids anti-group self-promotion. It is not until mid-career that promotions begin to be based largely on personal skill and experience.

Corporate white-collars receive equal pay until they reach their late thirties or early forties. At this time, some are promoted to become bucho, or division heads. Pay inequality now begins in earnest. Pay increases, now based on merit and performance, are larger from this moment, and these middle-age employees can now enjoy a more expensive lifestyle. One consequence of this system of pay raises is that many workers cannot pay off a home loan until just before or soon after retirement. An exception to this system of merit pay after age forty is the pay to those who are not promoted. Corporations are reluctant to dismiss inadequate but loyal employees. Though they are no longer promoted, they receive regular pay raises based on their seniority even when they are also given reduced workloads. Japanese follow a paternalistic attitude that older workers deserve higher incomes because of past loyalty and have greater financial needs than younger workers.

Many failing to reach the kacho or manager level by age fifty-five or so receive the title of "specialist" and are rotated from task to task as needed, or are given desks facing a window (to look out instead of working) and few duties until they retire. These practices are encouragements to retire without anyone losing face or disturbing the company's wa.

Those without major responsibilities or meaningful duties are said to be hiyameshi-gui or "cold rice eaters." Some may be asked to attend meetings or official social occasions to symbolically represent the company. They may also greet foreign guests who do not have impressive introductions (de Mente, 2012: 148). Other employees who have reached retirement age are allowed to continue in their duties, though they are not expected to be promoted. They continue to receive pay raises due to their seniority while many of these non- promoted employees are now encouraged to retire, or are actually dismissed (violating the principle of lifetime employment).

Others become managerial assistants or coordinators. Because of their age, they have the seniority to properly greet visitors and to represent the company during social events and conferences. They also represent their departments in negotiations so everyone continues to be aware of current events and potential changes in policy. They essentially keep everyone "in the loop."

Those who accept early (encouraged to do so or not) retirement are given two years' salary and other bonuses large enough to facilitate retirement for at least some employees. Unfortunately, government retiree benefits are not given until the official age of retirement is reached, forcing most early retirees to seek new employment to maintain their standards of living. This encourages employees to stay employed with their company for as long as possible.

The Japanese government has periodically raised the official age of retirement and expects to continue from the current sixty-two (decreed in 2016) to sixty-five by 2025. Companies are allowed

to establish their own retirement age, though employees generally stay on beyond the official limit if possible. Honda Motor Company has raised the retirement age of their employees to sixty- five, partly to maintain an adequate labor force. Japan's decades-old low birth rate is beginning to cause labor shortages of qualified workers (Slata Rodionova, March 3, 2016).

Managers realize workers develop antagonisms. It is obvious that some workers are more socially adept, harder working, and smarter than others. These are given more responsibility and more respect than other workers. In addition, even Japanese have boring jobs, become frustrated, or have irritating mannerisms. One solution to recover the harmony in an office is for the supervisor to organize an end-of-the-year party (bonenkai). These parties take place before the New Year and essentially are occasions to restore good relationships among team members. These parties are short in duration and held at the end of the workday. Each bonenkai begins with a formal speech by a senior official. This makes the gathering an official event and therefore obligatory (and the bill could be paid by the company.). The speaker thanks the workers for their hard work during the year, commends their loyalty to the company, and then asks for similar dedication and wa during the coming year. The leaders will stay for a few drinks and then leave to allow subordinates to enjoy themselves in a more relaxed manner for a couple of hours.

A similar technique for developing and maintaining wa is the gasshuku or weekend retreat (Schreiber, 1983). These take place for training purposes or because the supervisor feels morale

needs a boost. Japanese companies periodically send work teams on weekend retreats to Japanese- style inns near hot springs in the mountains. During the weekend, workers are given lectures on new techniques and work procedures in a relaxed environment. There are also athletic events, group calisthenics, walks in the woods, and outdoor grilling. There is at least one formal banquet during the gasshuku, with a high-level executive or respected outside expert as a keynote speaker. The banquet includes karaoke and numerous toasts accompanied by large amounts of drinking.

The importance of achieving and maintaining wa among co-workers is so central that outsiders seldom witness disagreements and conflicts within or between groups. While the existence of tension is accepted, overt disharmony and disagreements are not. Foreigners make the error of assuming that all Japanese workers are happy and satisfied because they do not complain. Maintaining wa demands that, at a minimum, everyone pretends harmony and high morale exist. Foreigners who express disagreements and anger in order to be open and honest with others are making a serious error when this seemingly anti-wa behavior is witnessed by Japanese. Another mistake made by those from more individualistic cultures is to use phrases such as "I think..." or "I wish to personally thank..." Avoiding the word "r" is important. It is better to replace "" with "we,"

1.6. The Place of Lawyers

There is an old story about a lost purse containing three gold coins illustrating the attitudes of Japanese toward litigation and the seeking of

redress in legal courts. A man lost a purse containing three gold coins. A second man found the purse, read the owner's name on the purse, searched for and found the owner, and presented him with the purse. The owner of the coins, embarrassed that he had lost such a large amount, refused to accept the purse or even acknowledge it was his. The second man insisted he had found the purse, that he wanted to give it to the owner, and that he deserved a reward for his honesty. The two parties decided to ask a judge to mediate their disagreement.

The judge listened to the arguments of both men, took a gold coin from his pocket, and added it to the three coins in question. The judge then gave two coins to each man. He pointed out that, because of this disagreement, the owner now has two coins instead of three; the finder also now has two coins when he could have kept three, and that he himself was one coin poorer. Because of the two men's lack of agreement, everyone was one coin poorer and the two fools had better not be seen in his court again. The lesson of the story, of course, is that people must settle their disagreements in an informal

manner.

Japanese avoid dealing with lawyers; they prefer to solve their conflicts outside of the courts because they see lawyers as decreasing the wa among associates and as very destructive to the maintaining of positive social relations. Business, conflicts are first mediated rather than taken to the courts. In the past, courts favored the wealthy and the upper class. Peasants and merchants rarely went to the courts to settle disagreements because they seldom received justice. In addition, there is the Japanese belief that disagreements are best

solved by compromise even if one party is in the right. Courts and lawyers are seen as imposing decisions on parties who should adjust their disagreements in personal ways. The result is that there are two lawyers per 10,000 persons in Japan as contrasted to forty lawyers per 10,000 persons in the U.S. The corresponding percentages for France and Germany are nine and twenty, respectively. Lawyers as such officially seldom take part in business dealings until an agreement is reached. William Chapman notes that there are thirty- five certified flower-arranging teachers for every Japanese lawyer, suggesting that the search for beauty is more important than overt legal conflicts (cited in Alston, 1986).

There are also practical reasons why there is so little litigation in Japan. Japanese use more paralegals in their daily affairs and companies use in-house employees who have legal training, often through the support of their employers. Japanese often seek free or relatively inexpensive legal advice from paralegals, Japanese Legal Support Center, bar associations, and municipalities in their daily affairs. Ordinary citizens find legal fees costly and seek private settlements instead. In addition, Japanese courts are notoriously slow and awarded settlements are small, encouraging citizens to avoid courts to redress their conflicts.

Becoming a lawyer in Japan is extremely difficult. Roughly twenty percent of applicants are accepted to the bar, while more than half are graduates from prestigious university law departments and law schools. Many lawyers find limited employment or numbers of clients, further making the legal profession unattractive. While many Japanese have legal training, few become licensed lawyers.

The public sees plaintiffs as selfish, greedy, and lacking in a sense of wa, and they receive little sympathy from their neighbors, the press, and the general public. Finally, Japanese judges are known for relying on the spirit of the law rather than on the strict letter of the law (For an example, see section 3.7. below). A plaintiff may have the law on his side and nevertheless have his suit rejected because he lacked the moral right. Ordinary persons see little advantage in seeking court-obtained sanctions since courts decisions are difficult to predict (Alston, 1986; Upham, 1981).

The 1655 legal code for Edo (now Tokyo) included the regulation that (Lu, 1987):

Parties engaged in public brawls and quarrels are punishable by death according to the law, irrespective of whichever party is in the right or at fault.

This article is no longer in effect, but it illustrates that there is a willingness to punish anyone who troubles the public peace, even if the complainant was provoked or an innocent victim. The best policy under such a value system is to avoid the courts as much as possible, since the innocent is as likely to be punished as the guilty. The fact that a person, seeks redress in the courts suggests that each party is at least in part guilty. Historically, citizens have distrusted the legal system; both private and business-related disagreements are often settled by participants in an informal manner to re-establish trust and wa. In the same manner, management views any public disagreement among co-workers as involving the guilt of all concerned.

Lawyers enter the negotiating picture during the last stage of the negotiations when the specific

wording of the contract needs to be agreed upon. The legal aspects involved in finalizing a contract legitimize agreement previously achieved. Using lawyers before this stage has been reached is seen as a sign of distrust. Members of the negotiating team who are lawyers behave as ordinary members of the team and do not insist on legalistic accuracy until a contract is being finalized.

1.7. Honne and Tatemae

No pair of concepts is as important as honne and tatemae, in part because they are major sources of frustration and cross-cultural misunderstanding to foreigners. Honne refers to one's true feelings and intentions. These are seldom expressed in Japanese society for fear of disturbing wa and others' good feelings and face. Honne is the truth as one sees it. It is an expression of one's real emotions and feelings as well as an expression of intent and state of mind. By contrast, tatemae are messages spoken to make others feel better, to maintain wa, and to avoid potential conflict caused by bad news, rejection, or criticism. Tatemae are social lies and verbal noise that are not meant to convey information but rather to maintain good feelings. The custom of using tatemae is the reason that Japanese have difficulty saying a direct "no" to someone. From a U.S. perspective, tatemae is hypocritical, though the Japanese do not understand tatemae in this fashion. Instead, they see these misrepresentations as important and laudable strategies to maintain

wa

Tatemae also refers to official corporate policy as presented to the general public. It becomes the official "truth" because it is an official statement,

though actual behavior and conditions differ. For example, leaders should be older than their subordinates. But what happens when younger persons are more familiar with new technologies and procedures? Should they be promoted over those who are older and cannot adjust to changes? The Japanese corporate solution, using tatemae, is to allow the younger employees to make decisions behind the scenes and let their older leaders take the official credit. In this way, respect for rank and seniority is maintained while the work is done by the best qualified. Everyone knows the honne of the situation is that the younger workers deserve full credit, but tatemae demands conformity to position and age rank. Tatemae demands the highest ranked- individual be treated as if he understands the technical details of a project.

Honne can be expressed during negotiations when the parties attend a drinking session together. A foreign negotiator may complain gently over drinks that the highest ranked Japanese does not ask very many technical questions. He will be told that the senior official is not familiar with the technical aspects of the issue since he was automatically promoted to his position due to his age. He relies on his more talented subordinates to deal with technical details. Tatemae demands

acting respectfully toward the senior official while realizing that, in terms of honne, decisions are made by subordinates.

1.8. Trust, Benevolence, Amae, and Makoto

Trust is the bedrock of any relationship among Japanese. If there is no trust, there is no reason

to establish long-lasting relationships when favors will not be returned and obligations not fulfilled. In traditional thinking, the foundation for any relationship is trust rather than a legal contract; without trust, there is no expectation of harmony and mutual support. Consequently, members from less group-oriented cultures feel Japanese spend too much time socializing and learning whether they can trust potential business partners before the initial stages of negotiation and discussion begin. The insistence of trust is reflected in the traditional Japanese contract: the keiyaku. It is vague and basically an agreement to conduct business. This type of agreement demands trust and is not agreed upon until all parties understand their respective responsibilities and expectations. The same keiyaku contract is found in the agreement offered to the new corporate workers when they are "adopted" into the corporation rather than hired for a specific position. Large Japanese corporations hire recruits for their complete careers with the expectation both parties will be loyal to one another. Recruits are not hired for specific positions but instead join the company "for better and for worse" in an agreement similar to the form of a western marriage ceremony. The relationships demand a large amount of trust because the two parties' responsibilities cannot be specified in detail beforehand. The details of the arrangement are developed after trust is established among potential business partners.

Benevolence is a major Asian value derived from Confucianism. Confucius lectured that those in power have the responsibility to protect the weak and help their subordinates. The ideal leader treats subordinates with kindness and with consideration

to their needs. The general rule is that the stronger helps the weaker. The supreme example of benevolence toward the weak is found in the concept of amae, which describes the relationship of a mother and her child. The child is helpless but the mother has the responsibility to protect and nurture the child because it is in fact helpless. In the same manner, those in power should help the weaker. Doing so otherwise or taking advantage of one's power is seen as selfish and antisocial. In an amae relationship, the dependent party expects the other's emotional and material support. In exchange, the weaker party offers loyalty, respect, and any aid he can offer.

A common tactic of Japanese business partners and associates is to insist they are weaker than the other party. Such humble protestations are flattering but they also set the stage for an implicit amae relationship in which the other party should grant favors because it is the stronger. Flattery is common among Japanese to be polite and maintain wa, but it is also used as a tactic to receive hopefully favors within an amae context.

The amae relationship has another characteristic. It is extremely difficult for outsiders to personally meet those making the final decisions in al corporation because Japanese subordinates work very hard to protect their superiors from meeting those they do not want to meet. The reason for this reluctance is that a superior is not responsible for anything involving people he has not met face- to-face. If a deal is rejected or a promise broken, the subordinate takes the responsibility and the company and superior are blameless. A decision- maker recognizes no amae relationship unless he has met personally with those concerned.

Telephone calls and letters do not fall in the category of amae relationships, and they are for the most part useless because it is necessary to first establish a face-to-face relationship to invoke an amae sense of responsibility. The best way to reach a decision-maker is to find a third party who has already established personal relations with someone one wishes to meet and convince him, through your own amae ties, to introduce you to the one who has the authority to make decisions. Anything else establishes business relations built

on sand.

While harmony and trust are the foundations of business relations, executives and team leaders at times place self-interest over harmony. Corporations demanding high amounts of overtime or taking advantage of workers, partly because secure jobs are currently scarce, are called burakku kigyo or "evil corporations." Michael Gerlach and Boyé de Mente (see section 1.18. below), both experts on Japanese business behavior, offer similar accounts of Japanese executives using their power to intimidate less powerful colleagues to their own advantage. They found that, while bonds of long- term friendships encourage a sense of personal obligation for fair dealings, Japanese executives have in the past seized opportunities to gain advantages over others. This may be more prevalent when Japanese deal with foreigners when absolute trust is less likely to exist; in addition, running roughshod over foreigners is more acceptable. There are strong traditions discouraging business partners from taking advantage of one another. Such actions may be legal but will not be considered

unethical.

Makoto, defined as sincerity, is related to amae.

Amae refers to the expectation that a person, hopefully a superior, will protect and support someone. The term "sincerity," however, means to Japanese that a person will be cooperative, avoid criticizing others, support the group's harmony, and follow the rules (kata) of behavior. Acting in a makoto manner means being concerned with others' face (honor) and well-being. Makoto demands that trust has already been established. Makoto does not demand total honesty if honesty means disturbing social relationships. Among Japanese, wa is more important than behavior that may lead to success while also resulting in disharmony.

1.9. Friendships

Japanese delay making friends with foreign coworkers until they are convinced the latter can become good group members and until a degree of trust has been established. In addition, Japanese do not easily make new friends outside of work, and they try seldom develop numerous friendship networks outside of work, as do U.S. Americans. Japanese develop extensive business networks but these are relationships based on expediency and they seldom include non-work-related activities. The Japanese have a saying "New friends are a burden" to indicate friendships entail obligations. Most Japanese are unwilling to develop friendships that might include the exchange of favors and demand too much time and responsibility. The consequence is that Japanese remain formal with acquaintances, even with neighbors and coworkers. In the same way, co-workers remain formal with one another and the relationship

is work-specific rather than extensive. Co-workers eat and drink together during the workweek but seldom invite one another to their homes.

Business-related friendships of any type seldom involve spouses. When Japanese are asked if they enjoy being expatriates in the U.S., a reason given for enjoying working in the US. is the opportunity to be with their spouses more often, including when entertaining and being entertained. The business world in Japan is male-oriented and Japanese seldom entertain business colleagues at

their homes.

1.10. The Hierarchical Society

The core of Japanese culture involves a series of hierarchical systems locating individuals in their places in society. One's place in society determines status and prestige, which are based in turn on such factors as the social standing of a family, the school one graduated from, age, occupation, employer (measured by the rank of companies worked for), and gender.

Over three-fourths of Japan's higher-level government officials and half of corporate presidents are graduates of Japan's most prestigious university, Tokyo University, founded in 1877 to educate Japan's future leaders. Becoming a Todai (Tokyo University's nickname) graduate demands nearly a life-long dedication. Attending the best (and most rigorous) grammar schools are necessary in order to be accepted to the best (and most rigorous) high schools. These high schools are ranked (their ranks are published in Japan's newspapers) in terms of how many of their graduates' pass Todai's entrance examinations.

Those who graduate from Todai are guaranteed being hired by the most respected civil-servant agencies and corporations, with promotions highly likely. School ties are important and Tokyo graduates form mutual-benefit ties that last throughout their lives. The prestige of Todai is as high or higher than elite-producing universities in other countries such as Harvard, Princeton, MIT, Oxford, or Cambridge Universities. In France, graduating from a Grande École leads to career paths to the highest-level elite positions (Alston et. al., 2003). These universities were originally established by Napoleon Bonaparte to prepare future French leaders for the modern era. Japan did the same long-term planning by establishing national policies of modernization during the second half of the nineteenth century.

Japanese corporations also exhibit a series of ranked groups/teams. The amount of prestige an employee receives is based on formal position in the bureaucracy, age, education, and gender-though minor other statuses exist such as city and province of origin- and his or her relations to higher-ranked persons. Honda Motor Corporation's U.S. American intern Laura Kriska (2011) received little respect at first because she was foreign, female, and started in a low-level position. She received more respect when she became a personal secretary to one of the company's directors. He also became her mentor (sempal) and took the time to give her language lessons and helped her in general, such as introducing her to important persons. In exchange, she found herself working harder to please her mentor, now also her sponsor.

Companies long ago established the sempai-köhai mentoring system. This system reflects both the

hierarchical aspect of their society as well as the familial forms of relationships encouraged in Japanese companies. A superior, the sempai, "adopts" a subordinate, the kohai, and becomes the latter's mentor. He sees that the subordinate receives proper training and work experience, recommends him for promotion and high visibility projects, and protects him should the köhai make mistakes. Usually the two have previously- established personal ties such as graduating from the same university or academic department, originating from the same city or province, and the like. Originally, the term "köhai" was "kogai" and referred to a servant or pet one takes care of and nurtures. Today, however, the term refers to a person who is taken care of by a superior or a mentor. Just as a pet should be devoted to its owner, the kogai/kohai should be devoted to his mentor, the sempai.

The sempai-köhal relationship is extensive. The sempai teaches his kohai proper etiquette, how to dress properly, introduces him to valuable contacts, and gives him career-related advice such as when to ask for a special assignment. He introduces him to his favorite bar hostesses so that the kohai becomes recognized and welcomed in a number of bars and nightclubs. The sempai becomes the köhai's role model and advisor.

A similar position in Japanese companies is the oyabun. The oyabun is a person who is known to be empathetic and sympathetic. He is the person a worker is most likely to seek out first for a solution of a personal problem. Should the worker have a work-related problem, he is more likely to talk to his sempai. The oyabun is asked for advice dealing with personal problems. The oyabun at times hosts

parties for the employees he manages, gives them presents at the appropriate times, and listens to their personal problems. He offers advice when a subordinate should marry, and at the same time, may offer the name of several eligible young ladies or the name of a respected marriage-broker. The oyabun are the hearts of the company.

The oyabun protects subordinates when they make mistakes. The Japanese have a strong tradition of apprenticeship whereby new workers learn by observing more experienced senior workers. They gradually are given more and more responsibility as they become capable of fulfilling more complicated duties. The ideal oyabun watches his charges closely but allows them to make minor mistakes in order to learn. A vice-president of Matsushita Electric summarized this oyabun-kōhai relationship by declaring (Gould, 1970: 79):

We believe people can be trusted, and should be allowed to go on their own as much as possible. But we closely supervise in case of ruinous mistakes... Everything depends on the innate capability of the individual executive. But we can also speed up his performance.

This close supervision of newer workers, of course, demands familiarity with the subordinates and knowledge of their personalities and talents.

While there exists a high level of economic equality in Japan, there also exist powerful groups dominating the decision-making processes; Japanese citizens are fairly equal in terms of wealth and incomes, but a number of very small minorities decide all major public policy decisions in Japan. Each of Japan's largest corporations is grouped with other companies and with at least

one bank and with an insurance company. These groupings, called keiretsu, support their members by offering them favorable contracts and special discounts. Keiretsu companies hold stock in one another's companies and banks making unfriendly takeovers impossible. The group's bank may own as much as thirty percent of the company's' stock. A keiretsu may contain hundreds of companies, each making decisions with the others' interests in mind. For example, Toshiba has roughly two hundred related companies and a third-tier set of six hundred "grandchild companies" of suppliers and subcontractors. Many of these companies employ personnel who have retired from the parent company, making the social ties among these companies strong and constantly maintained.

Keiretsu senior executives meet on a monthly basis to determine policy, divide contracts among themselves, and allocate resources. The members of these "presidents' clubs" maintain close ties with members of powerful government ministries such as the Ministry of Finance. These persons greatly influence economic decisions made by government officials. Keiretsu leaders also meet with government officials to influence economic decisions made on the national level. These corporate leaders are members of the keidanren, a private club made up of roughly 1,000 members. No major economic decision can be made in Japan without the support of the keidanren.

At the other end of the power spectrum are those who have part-time jobs or no jobs at all. An estimated seventy percent of Japan's labor force enjoys lifetime employment (often called "regular employment"). The rest hold part time work, earning an average of U.S. $1,800 a month

(Semuels, 2017). The number of workers not covered by lifetime employment has increased since the early 1990s, when Japan's "Great Depression" began and the government issued regulations to increase corporations' ability to dismiss workers and hire cheaper, temporary labor. Many Japanese do not find their ideal jobs. Some become "free-ta," or those who flit from one part. time job to another in the hope of finding secure, well-paid positions. Since there are now only eight job openings for every ten applicants and the economy has been in a recession for the last fifteen years, the chances of obtaining a secure future are low. Recent reports indicate an increase in the number of free -ta in the labor force. Japanese corporations are now more willing to hire workers on a temporary and short-time basis.

Another detrimental consequence of the Japanese stress on the respect given to hierarchy and rank is the existence of positions for senior workers that have little authority or purpose except to give prestige to their holders. Many Japanese white- collars working in large corporations have the goal of achieving promotions into middle management (chukan kanrishoku) levels by age forty-five or so, and the best are promoted based on their aptitude and past work. There are other positions (relatively low in number) given to older workers that are honorific rather than functional. Typical examples are acting branch managers in Japanese banks, who are placed between section chiefs and subsection chiefs. In many cases, these positions are rewards for past successes and long tenure instead of performance because of the need to maintain wa

Such positions are often given to workers who continue to fulfill the same (or less) duties as

before, but now have a higher rank and pay. They may be given a desk or office and an expense account for entertaining clients, government officials, etc. These elder statesmen probably have ties with those in other companies and with long- term customers, giving them additional influence. However, such persons must be respected and not ignored, since they enjoy great personal influence. They often become senior advisors and provide the benefit of their experience. The fact that such persons enjoy wide social networks throughout their companies makes them respected and gives them influence not found among less senior workers.

The problem with this custom of honorific positions is that holders continue to have influence in the company, although they are in the minority. Having senior (though honorific) positions, seniority, and being older give such persons influence and respect, called insei. Insei is authority based on status and age rather than what one does. These features give them the power to influence policy and their approval is needed for any major changes of policy. They become elder statesmen and form very conservative forces in their organizations. This situation is described as rougai, or the control of elderly persons over the young (and is found throughout Japanese. society). Toshiba Corporation and Mitsubishi Heavy Industries, Ltd., for example, have experienced poor business performance due in large part to their over-conservative policies keeping them from adapting to market changes quickly enough. Over time, the presence of older workers with insei can result in too-conservative mindsets,

1.11. Uncertainty Avoidance

The core values of Japanese culture have been compared to other cultures by Geert Hofstede (2001) and colleagues (2010). A key to understand how to conduct business with salarymen is Hofstede's concept of uncertainty avoidance (UA), defined as "the extent to which members of a culture feel threatened by ambiguous or unknown situations" (Hofstede et al., 2010: 191). Below are the rankings for selected national samples in terms of the UA index (Hofstede, 2001: 151):

Greece France

1.0 (highest) Japan

10.5

Italy 23.0 Germany

USA

43.0

7.0

S. Korea 29.0

16.5

Singapore 53.0 (lowest)

It is clear from the above table that Japan, as seventh in rank, is one of the highest-ranking nations i in terms of the uncertainty avoidance index. Uncertainty avoidance is not to be understood as fear, which is focused on a specific situation (Hofstede, et al., 2010: 197). The concept of UA deals with the unknown. Once a danger is known, Japanese easily focus their energies to solve the problem.

The major characteristic of UA is that members of high uncertainty cultures do not handle ambiguities well. An example of this aversion to uncertainty, high UA individuals prefer games of skills and strategy while low UA players prefer games of chance (Hofstede, 2001: 180). When a situation is understood, however, high UA cultures then focus on solutions to problems that are now clearly recognized. However, such cultures do not

handle ambiguous situations well, and they form rules (kata) to manage these ambiguities. Once a problem is defined, high UA societies can mobilize to solve the problem. The concept of kaizen (see section 1.4 above) fits well into Hofstede's framework, since kaizen is the slow, careful,

strategy for change.

The UA index measures the degree to which members of a society accept or reject unstructured, ambiguous situations. Cultures scoring high on UA are most likely to contain large numbers of rules (kata), even when some are not followed, as found among the French (Alston et al., 2005) and Italians. Often, high UA cultures have so many formal and informal rules that some conflict with others, and members have to decide which rules to follow and which to ignore. Conformity to rules is preferred to nonconformity ("what is different is dangerous"). One could also add "what is new is dangerous." In fact, high UA cultures discourage entrepreneurial activities, and have less startup companies than in low UA cultures (Smit, 2015).

There are many exceptions in Japan to this discouragement of entrepreneurs, as shown by the following corporate founders: Soichiro Honda (Honda Motor Corporation), Kazuo Inamori (see section 3.11 below), Fusajiro Yamauchi (Nintendo Company Limited), Jujiro Matsuda (Mazda Motor Corporation), Kiichiro Toyoda (Toyota Motor Corporation), and others. Many of Japan's most successful innovators emerged during the 1950s and 1960s, during a time of high economic growth and a national sense that Japan needed to expand and internationalize its economy (Alston, 1986).

In high UA level schools, memorizing is stressed over allowing students more independence in how

and what they learn. Teachers are expected to know what students need to learn (Dellner, 2009), and there will be more uniformity from one school to another. In low UA cultures, teaching is more open- ended and more focused in the development of individual abilities and interests. Low UA schools will use a variety of textbooks rather than following one set of teaching materials established by a national, centralized, organization.

At work, high level UA employees are expected to follow the rules rather than innovating when necessary. There will also be less inter-organization mobility, as workers will be reluctant to change employers to work in new situations. In general, strict schedules are preferred, and people are expected to be on time for events and activities, and to follow work protocols. Seniority is stressed for promotion rather than merit. There will be suspicion of foreign managers as they symbolize the unknown (there will be a rejection of foreigners in general, and immigration of foreigners discouraged). Managerial style will be consultative rather than competitive. Group decisions are preferred to individual initiative (Hofstede, 2001: 160-1).

High levels of uncertainty avoidance encourage long-term planning in order to control possible changes as much as possible. It is also better to work slowly than to make mistakes. High levels of UA in a culture discourage acceptance of rapid changes. The result is two-fold. There is a high interest in long-term planning, and once a plan has been established, workers are expected to follow the plan and established protocols. In fact, Japanese score high in terms of a preference for long-term planning as compared to other national

samples. Hofstede's fifth dimension of core cultural values is the measure for long-term orientation. Japan ranked fourth among twenty-three national samples (China was first), while U.S. respondents ranked seventeenth (Hofstede et al., 2010: 240).

Second, although societies with high levels of UA tend to be conservative and traditional, change is possible once a danger is recognized and clearly defined.

Japan itself has experienced drastic changes during the modern era, including the 1950s-70s. After the economic stagnation during the 1990s until after 2010, Japanese companies have begun once again to exhibit high levels of innovation, especially in the electronic product areas, including organic electroluminescent (OEL) television, audio instruments, and other consumer electronics. Many Japanese companies are now highly innovative. In 2014, Japan was ranked third in the world in terms of the number of patent applications (325,986); China was ranked first with 928,177 and the U.S. ranked second with 578,802. Part of this success was due to incremental change, in which new products were not only developed, but were gradually improved in both quality and lower cost. Change is made more palatable by being planned for and made in small steps (kaizen).

By contrast, cultures scoring low on the UA index encourage individual initiative and originality. They have fewer rules and laws while nonconformity is more accepted, while members are more pragmatic rather than conforming to rules. Change in general is more welcomed and new products have more appeal. Less planning is done and there is more optimism than a fear of future problems. People will use common sense when they

feel rules are not adequate. Low UA persons are not as likely to see the need to plan for the future; they would rather let the future happen and then react to any emerging problems at that time.

As important, low UA members are more receptive to new situations, including dealing with strangers. The OECD found that, in 2014, the time needed to start a business in Japan was twice that needed in the U.S. In addition, the OECD reported in 2009 that Japan ranked twelfth in terms of "ease of doing business." The corresponding world ranking for the U.S. was third. Microsoft, Apple, and Tesla, Inc. are examples of U.S. American companies that foster low levels of uncertainty avoidance.

Low UA westerners dealing with Japanese are often irritated with the slow pace of doing business during the early stages of negotiations. Japanese feel uncomfortable when dealing with strangers, and they prefer to know their foreign colleagues well before negotiating in earnest or when completing contracts. Members from high UA cultures (Japan and Asian cultures in general) do not deal comfortably with strangers. Doing business with salarymen is less problematic and faster when westerners become part of the formers' social networks. In addition, high UA cultures discourage rapid social changes and proposals should emphasize they promote the continuation of past policy rather than breaks with tradition.

1.12. Ageism at Work

Another major source of inequality in Japan is age. Age was highly respected during Japan's pre- modern period and age remains a significant aspect of prestige. Younger workers are relatively

underpaid compared to older workers because seniority and age are the most important determinants of pay. Younger persons need to stay with one company for several decades before they are well-rewarded. Since older persons hold the positions of authority, younger persons wait decades before they are given the authority to make significant decisions. New ideas are slow to develop in Japan's corporate hierarchies unless a superior is willing to listen to younger colleagues.

It is unthinkable that someone younger would have formal authority over someone more senior since promotions in corporations are largely based on age and seniority (which are almost the same) until reaching the head of division level. Many corporate titles are empty titles because some older white collars are promoted based on their seniority. Younger workers often are the real initiators of policy in a division but they are expected to defer to their superiors. Often, Japanese workers have little formal influence until they reach their middle forties and become department heads or respected

"elders."

Pay raises upon promotion become larger and those in managerial positions begin to make unilateral decisions (after consultation). Younger I workers are forced to bide their time before gaining significantly higher salaries and decision- making opportunities. U.S. Americans often make the mistake of sending to Japan negotiators who are relatively younger. They may be technically superior but they will not receive much respect, and older Japanese with the authority to make decisions will find it difficult to be honest with them or even entertain them as if they were equal. A younger representative may do most of the talking during a

meeting but a senior person will be present to show the company is serious about doing business. Hiroki and Joan S. Kato (1992) recommend that anyone sent to Japan with the authority to make important decisions be forty years old or more.

1.13. The Equal Society

In spite of strict social hierarchies with different levels of prestige and decision-making capability, the Japanese society itself is remarkably equal. There is little extreme poverty and most Japanese consider themselves to be middle class. There are few minorities in Japan, primarily Koreans, Burakumin (outcasts) and Ainu (indigenous ethnic group); the rest of the population is extremely homogeneous in terms of genetic background. Japanese automobile drivers' licenses do not include information on hair and eye color since over ninety- five percent of Japanese have the same hair and eye color. There is also a high level of income equality in Japan. Income inequality in Japan is the lowest among the world's industrial nations. Average annual CEO compensation in the U.S. is US$12.26 million and US$2.35 million in Japan (Roberto A. Ferdman: work blog: September 25, 2014). Measured by the Gini coefficient where zero indicates total equality and one indicates total inequality, Japan had a 2009 income equality index of 339 (US: 499) before taxes (Pew Research Center, December 10, 2013). A similar measure of wealth inequality is the wage difference between CEO and average worker in 2014. In Japan, the average CEO earned sixty-seven times the income of average workers (US: 354). The society also has excellent public transportation, educational,

and medical systems, and public peace is well- maintained. The life expectancy in 2014 of the Japanese population was the highest in the world: 83.7 years (U.S.: 79.3 years).

Equality is found in all areas of life in Japan because conformity is so central to the Japanese personality. The saying "The tall nail gets hammered down" indicates being different violates the Japanese sense of wa. Even business competitors copy one another and Japanese companies are driven by yokonarabi, or the drive to imitate competitors. If a company introduces a new product, then its competitors are certain to develop a similar product even if it does not fit their product lines or long-range plans. The pressure exists to be as alike as possible only, ideally, a little more successful. In fact, the concern here is not to win over one's competitors but rather not to lose in the competition. This results in copying competitors as much as possible. Yoshimura and Anderson (1997) explain that Japanese companies are capable of developing a product that is doomed to failure just because a competitor has introduced a similar product. Instead of going with their strengths, Japanese companies have a tendency of copying competitors' products to maintain conformity. The two authors mention how, after Sony bought Columbia Pictures in 1989, Matsushita followed suit and bought Universal Studios a year later even though there was no rational economic reason to do

so.

Foreigners can take advantage of the yokonarabi spirit by suggesting that a competitor will soon use your material or one of your products. The drive to conform to competitors' actions may give you a more careful hearing. If you can say that

a competitor is using a product or developing a selected research line, corporate decision-makers will listen if doing so helps them conform to their competitors.

Salarymen, including senior executives, dress alike in similar color suits and eat the same foods when together. In the same manner, school children wear uniforms and are expected to memorize the same material. The Japanese word "chigau" means "different," but it also means "wrong." Japanese are careful not to ignore the many kata that structure Japanese behavior. To do otherwise may result in social isolation and rejection. Japanese children who have lived abroad are often bullied because they do not behave like "normal" children. The Japanese have established schools for returning expatriate school-age children to better familiarize them with how to behave. Those who have lived in more individualistic societies such as the U.S. have an especially difficult time adjusting to the more conformist Japanese society.

1.14. The Importance of Education

Education in Japan determines the future of graduates since the most respected corporations hire only those who graduate from the schools with the highest prestige. Graduates from Tokyo University and Kyoto University, for example, automatically join the civil service or the most highly-ranked corporations and eventually become leaders in government or in the corporate world. Essentially all of Japan's elites have graduated from the ten or so highest-ranked universities. Similar to the U.S. American university rankings published by the US News & World Report, Japanese newspapers

also annually rank universities and high schools. High schools are ranked according to the number of their graduates accepted by the more respected universities.

High school education is taken seriously by those who are occupationally ambitious. Educational programs such as mathematics break down each unit into its simplest part and students are expected to understand each section before going to the next. The result is an emphasis on both quality and detail. Learning is memorizing facts then proceeding to the next level. Students learn to value perfection by focusing on details. Japan's educational system does not encourage original thinking or creativity among its students and the main task of students is to learn what is presented. Students wear uniforms, do housework in their classrooms, and learn how to subordinate their needs to the group. The results are high school graduates who are intellectually homogeneous, conformist, used to working hard, and well-grounded in basic knowledge.

The educational system is also highly competitive. Students attend high schools that will accept them rather than those located closest to their homes; as a result, many high school students living in Tokyo and in Japan's largest cities commute long hours to their preferred schools (The senior author of this book commuted standing up in commuter trains two hours each way to and from his high school.). Each school holds competitions for entry, including the highest- ranked kindergartens. Middle-class parents also pay large sums for after-hours "cram" educational programs, called juku (see Appendix A), that prepare students for the entrance examinations of the better high schools and universities. Roughly three-

fourths of high school students attend these cram schools (Feiler, 2004: 182-83) in order to prepare for university levels entrance examinations. Schools also offer a wide range of after-school club activities. Many school-age children attend private lessons called kyoshitsu ("class") specializing in developing non-academic skills such as calligraphy, piano lessons, swimming, gymnastics, abacus, and other activities.

An estimated half of high school graduating students fail the examinations of their first university choice and some of them spend the next year preparing for the next examinations. At one time, roughly 200,000 high school graduates were studying at home and in private cram schools (juku) to re-take university entrance examinations (Rohlen, 1983) called "examination hell" by students and parents alike. Three-quarters of applicants to Tokyo University fail at least once before being accepted, some three or four times (see Feiler, 2004).

One-third of all students applying to the highest-prestige universities and highest-prestige medical and dental schools re-take their entrance examinations at least twice. Public universities require scores from two different examination systems: one- or two-days general examinations dealing with subjects taught in high schools, and a series of second examinations prepared by each university. The ten highest-ranked universities accept one tenth of those who take their examinations. The next tier of universities accepts only one in five applicants. The preparations for these examinations are so demanding that there is the adage "more than four hours sleep a night means failure. It is proper to send a gift to a

business colleague's child who has been accepted to the university of his choice to celebrate the

occasion.

Rohlen found at the same time that some high schools did not develop high-quality graduates. Many of these students attend industrial high schools that prepare students for lower-level jobs; they emphasize practical learning rather than preparation to enter universities. Most high school graduates, if they attend universities, are forced to matriculate into the lower-ranked universities. Entering any of the fifteen highest- ranked universities is very competitive because the best job positions are seldom offered to those who graduate from any other universities. Japan is truly an educational meritocracy, resulting in a strict hierarchy.

Those who refuse to study for long hours or cannot enter a higher-ranked high school or university are allowed to study as much - or less -as they wish. Schools do not hold back students when they do not pass their subjects. Everyone is promoted to the next class irrespective of grades. The only requirement to graduate is to attend at least half of one's classes. The legal code states that school attendance over the tenth grade is not obligatory, and truancy is not illegal for those attending high school (Nathan, 2004: 39).

1.15. The Importance of Work in Japanese Culture

The importance of work in Japan is illustrated by the emergence of a newly-defined fatal disease of death from overwork. The victims die from suicide, heart failure, cerebral hemorrhage, or other similar

symptoms. The most likely candidate is a white- collar corporate worker of either gender, though fatalities have occurred in all occupations. The estimated number of such deaths has increased in recent years because large-scale layoffs during the last decades resulted in fewer workers performing more duties and working harder. A 2016 government-sponsored white paper reported that one fifth of companies in their survey said some employees worked more than eighty hours or more of overtime a month. Nation-wide, twenty-one percent of Japanese workers logged (officially) forty- nine hours or more hours each week (U.S.: 16%). Many workers at all levels and occupations drink supplementary energy drinks to help them recover from fatigue and lack of sleep.

Japanese workers define loyalty to superiors and employers as including working long hours. Long hours at one's desk or entertaining clients and colleagues are necessary to show one's loyalty to groups and corporations. That is the reason Japanese workers take only half of the holidays they are entitled to by law. Many workers suspect being on vacation will be defined by others as selfishness and might as a result lower one's reputation, evaluations, and eventually the chance for meaningful promotions.

This pattern of overwork involves workers at all levels. A division manager (bucho) stays at his desk after the workday is finished to show his own superiors and subordinates his dedication. This manager's subordinates now also must stay at their desks to similarly show their devotion to working and respect for their superior. Official statistics of hours worked are understated because conscientious employees do not report the extra

hours at their desks or elsewhere as overtime or "real" work. As a result, fifty or more hours of work- related behavior a week is normal for many white- collar workers. Since most salarymen commute one or more hour a trip, workers seldom enjoy a full eight hours sleep. The Japanese term of overtime is "zangyo," and the meaning includes the notion of unpaid work.

The commonly used verb gambaru, defined as "hang in there," or "keep trying" is used to encourage workers to work hard and persevere. The long-term view held by the Japanese allows them to work for long periods of time until gaining success or, as in the case of companies, profit. The term above is also used when great energy and perseverance are needed to achieve a plan. No Japanese wants to let down the group or team by not working hard or long enough.

As expected, work is the major topic of discussion among Japanese employees. They talk about their superiors, the meaning of any ambiguous messages they may have received, and the unofficial prestige of those who are doing well. Alan Goldman (1994) recommends that anyone planning to talk to corporate Japanese read in-house publications such as corporate newsletters and recent speeches by higher-level officials. The expense of translating such documents into English is well worth the cost since the reader is now able to quote the leaders and converse on local gossip. At the very least, the reader becomes familiar with topics of interests such as recently achieved contracts and is able to talk about issues of interest to Japanese counterparts. Being familiar with the CEO's latest speech, awards, or with corporate events indicates respect for the company and careful preparation, both attributes

highly respected.

The Leisure Development Center, a national agency, was established to measure uses of free time and attitudes toward leisure. A tipping point occurred in 1995 when thirty-five percent (as against 34%) of respondent in a survey stated that leisure was more important than work (Norbury, 2008: 77). Before, a plurality had stated work was more important. While surveys in Japan are unreliable, the latest surveys indicate a change in attitudes toward work.

1.16. Language

Anthropologists Edward T. and Mildred Hall

(1990) divide language usage into two types: low context and high context. Low-context language use, found in English-speaking nations, Germany, Holland, and Sweden as well as other societies, is direct in that the information of a message is contained in the message. No other information is needed to understand the message itself. Scientific concepts, which are extremely low context, aim at clarity in which messages, such as mathematical formulas, are understood through universally shared definitions. Such messages are concise and low context speakers are impatient with rhetoric and vagueness. The demand in the US. to "Say what you mean and mean what you say" indicates a preference for low context communications.

are

High-context messages, by contrast, multidimensional in which the message is not limited to the words themselves. High-context messages include tone of voice, body language, and shared experience. High-context messages also contain multiple and symbolic meanings. Foods

often offer a complex set of high context messages. A birthday cake is more than a dessert or an item containing a specific number of calories. Champagne, of course, is more than a thirst quencher. All cultures offer special foods paying respect to guests; rejecting such foods suggests an unwillingness to be a guest or a friend. In the same high-context manner, a nation's flag is more than a piece of colored cloth.

Most intercultural problems are caused by assuming a message is low-context when it is in fact high-context. Imagine a situation when a Japanese suggests playing golf the next day instead of discussing business. The homesick U.S. American is probably in a hurry to return home because he finds Tokyo hot, humid, and foreign and needs to return to his office where other work is piling up. He instead suggests they meet as usual to achieve an agreement in as short a time as possible. Unfortunately, this person does not realize that an invitation to play golf is a high-context message. The host had already reserved a tee-off time, made reservations for lunch and dinner, hired a car and chauffer, and invited several business colleagues. The negotiations were going badly and the Japanese host felt that a day playing golf would allow him to discuss sensitive issues in an informal environment. Post-game drinks allow the players to get to know one another better. The invitation was a high-context message that participants needed to talk informally.

Japanese executives enjoy playing golf and golf courses are often used as sites to become acquainted and to indicate respect to clients and visiting dignitaries. In November 2017, Prime Minister Shinzo Abe hosted U.S. President Donald J.

Trump at a golf course in Saitama. They enjoyed a hamburger meal at a club house and then played golf together the next day. The two national leaders had already played golf in Florida the previous February.

Japanese language usage is high-context. It is indirect and vague and many messages can only be understood by knowing the speaker, by observing body language, and by inferring from tone and past conversations what is being said. Japanese find it difficult to speak directly and they are more comfortable in discussing sensitive matters in informal surroundings such as bars or golf courses. Often, communication among Japanese lacks much verbal content. The traditional non-verbal communication is called ishin-denshin, described by Japanese as "What the mind thinks, the heart transmits." Older Japanese are especially likely to express their feelings in nonverbal ways. The extensive entertaining taking place before business negotiation begins helps develop knowledge of how a person feels and thinks. Younger Japanese are less likely to communicate in the traditional ishin-denshin manner but most still read nonverbal messages better than the more verbal U.S. Americans.

Michihiro Matsumoto (see section 1.19. below) offers an example of indirect use of language by a Japanese. A Japanese asks his mentor (oyabun) for a promotion. The mentor responds by saying, "The sun and moon are different." Does the response suggest a "no" or a "yes?" Is the mentor hinting the subordinate should wait before asking for a promotion? Or is he changing the subject to avoid answering directly?

A common fault on the part of U.S. Americans is assuming a request for information involves only the specific information being asked. Being low- context in their language usage, US. Americans are likely to give only the requested information. By contrast, being high-context, Japanese expect any and all information related to a specific request. As a consequence, U.S. Americans tend to feel Japanese give too much information that they define as irrelevant. The Japanese, on the other hand, feel that U.S. Americans seldom give enough information. The latter gives out information on a "need to know" basis; Japanese give out information on a "nice to know" basis.

Dealing with Japanese demands an indirect manner. Direct demands or requests should not be made unless they are couched in very polite, indirect ways, often with an implied apology. Typical forms of requests are:

.

.

I am not certain of my information, but...

I hope this request is not too demanding, but...

I apologize for my ignorance, but....

If we can be of any help... (instead of "Call me...")

Other indirect forms of communication include the aizuchi language pattern. The term refers to responses to whatever the other person is saying in order to show one is listening. These include such interruptions as, "Yes," "I see," "Of course," and "Really?" Also, part of aizuchi is the constant nodding of the head as if in agreement. These verbal forms do not indicate agreement but are indications of listening. Japanese are also likely to

make comments in the form of questions as, "The weather is hot, isn't?" These are comments asking for support and agreement while indirectly making a statement. The aizuchi-type statement "The food here is expensive, isn't it" makes a statement that suggests a restaurant's prices are too high and the speaker wants to look at another restaurant's price list of available dishes (Japanese restaurants display models and prices of their dishes so that pedestrians can judge the price and the quality of the food before entering a restaurant.). Comments in the form of questions also draw others into the conversation and avoid being too direct.

Another characteristic of communication common with Japanese is the hesitancy to ask a direct question. Japanese often explain why they are asking a direct question to avoid being too direct. Sometimes these explanations are longer than the questions themselves; the speaker wishes to be polite, does not wish to appear ignorant, or does not want to seem to be too aggressive. Japanese seldom "get to the point" even when explaining why they are asking questions.

It is possible to suggest that further discussions be done openly (honne) after a relationship has been firmly established. The term in English for i open discussions is to "talk stomach to stomach." It is also possible in a letter to begin with a social greeting and then the writer suggests that he or she will "omit formalities." This allows the writer to state his concerns openly without fear of insulting the reader. There are other ways to infer the next message will be more open by the use of transitional phrases such as "By the way," "Some people say," or "In any event" to indicate a honne type message may follow.

A source of confusion caused by westerners when talking to Japanese is the use of idioms and slang. This is especially true of U.S. Americans since they wish to be informal with those they are speaking. However, most English-speaking Japanese do not get exposed to everyday slang and idioms. U.S. Americans commonly use sports idioms in their spoken language ("a slam dunk," "let's go for a home run," "he's a great relief player") that few Japanese learn in their language courses. U.S. Americans also are apt to use military terms ("Let's get bombed") to make points ("All nine yards") completely lost by Japanese listeners.

It is also best to avoid humor when talking in English to a Japanese audience. Tragedy crosses cultural borders well as the many translations of Shakespeare's tragedies attest. However, humor travels less easily from one culture to another. U.S. American humor is laced with irony and sarcasm as well as plays on words, and these seldom translate well. Sarcasm, based on saying one thing but meaning another, may be taken literally and the humor lost altogether.

Japanese respect slogans and Japanese businesses post numerous slogans on walls and bulletin boards in offices and work areas. Resembling one-line haiku poems (see section 1.17. below), slogans are used to motivate workers. U.S. Americans generally distrust slogans and are suspicious of them, yet they should not be ridiculed because slogans are often shorthand messages to describe an event or goal. Those paying attention to slogans in a Japanese workplace can gain insights on what is considered important or problematic. In the fifties, a common slogan hung in factories was "export or die" to encourage workers to dedicate themselves to

manufacture export items.

Another unique aspect of the Japanese form of communication is the use of seals to stamp documents instead of using handwritten signatures. Those planning to stay in Japan for any length of time should develop and register a personal seal, inkan or hanko, to use along with their signatures. Japanese business and government leaders did not sign their names in the past and seldom do so today. Instead, they stamped their seals on documents. Even today it is difficult to conduct basic activities such as signing a lease or opening a local bank account without using a registered seal. A seal next to a signature also indicates the document is an important as well as an official statement. Important documents include a first page containing a matrix of squares in which the readers' seals are stamped. U.S. Americans using personal seals when signing a contract or agreement show their willingness to adopt local practices.

Japanese do not use the telephone, faxes, or other impersonal forms of communication for personal purposes. These are used for business communications. Personal communications are limited to personally written letters and personal visits. A personal note can be added to more technical information but the best way to maintain a relationship is through face-to-face visits. 1.17. The Haiku Society

Haiku is a form of poetry extremely popular in Japan, and its popularity illustrates the Japanese approach to language. A haiku is a three- line, seventeen syllables poem. Becoming popular

during the seventeenth century, haiku poems contain high-context, multiple meanings and images. The Emperor of Japan annually composes a haiku poem as a New Year's gift to his subjects. In seventeen syllables, the Emperor projects his wishes for the coming year or makes comments on trends in the Japanese society. Thus, the Emperor of Japan delivers a seventeen-syllable "state of the union" message; the equivalent presidential message in the U.S. may take an hour or more to deliver. At the same time, the Imperial Household sponsors a contest in which anyone can submit an original haiku based on a pre-determined theme; thousands of amateur poets annually take part in the competition.

Many of the most celebrated haiku poets were Buddhist monks. Their poems contain messages lamenting how short life is and admiring the beauty of nature. The most revered haiku poet is Matsuo Bashō (1644-94). His poems are deceptively simple yet they offer sudden Zen-like illuminations of life (Miyamori, 2002: 89):

There is no sign in the cicada's cry That they are just about to die.

Cicadas, butterflies, and flowers are popular motifs in haiku poems. Cicadas have short lives yet they fill the night's air with wonderful sounds illustrating that life is short but is also full of beauty; this is a very Buddhist view of the transitory character of life. A typical haiku poem praising minimum communication was written by Oshima Ryōta, who died in 1787 (Yuzuru Miura, 1991:74):

Silent communion

Between the guest,

The host, and the white chrysanthemum.

U.S. Americans should speak more in haiku form by using fewer words. While they do no need to become poets, they show humility by speaking less. The Japanese saying "The most mature rice stalk bows its head the lowest" suggests that leaders are most respected when they "sit and do not do." This behavior is called teishisei, or "low profile management style."

1.18. Happiness Levels in Japan

Japan in general is not a happiness-driven society. There is no general value supporting "the pursuit of happiness." The focuses in the Japanese value system are conformity and group loyalty. Individuals are offered social identification and a sense of belonging/ security from their group memberships which will lead to personal satisfaction, though the latter is a secondary goal. Satisfaction results from the success of one's group, not from individual successes. Personal happiness is seldom emphasized as a goal, either on the individual or organizational level. In fact, individuals who seek satisfaction through nonconformity (often the only strategy) are seen as suspect individuals. Older Japanese overwhelmingly disapprove of younger persons seeking personal satisfaction rather than conforming to societal demands.

Morale-development and maintenance programs/ practice sponsored by corporations, including drinking sessions, lifetime employment, etc., exist to improve the morale and to develop feelings of wa among workers (Sullivan, 1992) rather than

to improve the personal satisfaction of personnel. This failure to focus on individual satisfaction is reflected in the fact that majorities of (56%) Japanese workers are unhappy with their lives and with some aspects of their workplace (74%). Occupational training focuses on workers' willingness to work hard, not to become personally happy.

Justine Underhill notes in Yahoo Finance (August 20, 2015) that one-hundred-hour workweeks are common in Japanese corporations. She describes a Japanese IT worker who often worked until three a.m., went home for a few hours' sleep, then returned to her office by seven the next morning. She later took a three-month leave after experiencing a mental breakdown. Twenty-two percent of Japanese workers work fifty or more hours a week, as compared to eleven percent in the U.S. In a celebrated legal case, a man hanged himself after working almost two hundred hours of overtime a month for seven months. His case became known to the general public because the man's wife sued her husband's employer for his death. She became shunned by her neighbors while the mass media and the general public criticized her because of her non-conforming behavior. One response to such cases of overwork is for companies to turn off lights at 7:30 p.m. to force office worker to leave their offices.

The 2016 World Happiness Report, sponsored by the United Nations, ranked nations according to their average happiness levels. Japan was ranked fifty-third (U.S.: thirteenth). Other surveys similarly indicate low levels of satisfaction with their lives among Japanese. In spite of relatively higher rates of unhappiness as compared to other

nationalities, the pressure for maintaining wa has resulted in an almost non-existence of labor strikes since the seventies. For the six-year average ending in 2000, Japan experienced one strike per 100,000 workers (U.S.: 60). Although workers are allowed to strike for better working conditions and wages by the Trade Union Act, the act of striking is seen as a disturbance of wa in relation to employers, managers, and customers. Labor unions seldom resort to strikes to demand better pay and conditions. Avoiding personal shame and disturbing wa are more important than seeking personal gains in a way that create disturbances in society or in organizations. A transportation union ordered a strike during the 1970s but only during the hours of five and seven in the morning so commuters would not be inconvenienced. When a strike has been announced, workers will often continue to work; the announcement of a strike is enough to alarm (shame) management.

Japanese traditional values encourage stoic responses to personal hardships. The saying "good medicine tastes bitter" reflects the general attitude that hardships are necessary for a good life. John Nathan (2004: 68) quotes a forty-eight-year-old general manager reflecting a sense of existential unease and dissatisfaction: "All my life I've struggled to get ahead without any doubts about what I was doing." He feels unhappy about his life in spite of his prosperity.

A consequence of this general unhappiness is the fact that the highest rate of suicide in Japan is found among males aged thirty-five to fifty-five years old. Males are more than twice as likely to commit suicide than women, suggesting social pressures on men to conform is greater or less likely to be

alleviated through social support, etc. Depressed Japanese seldom seek help or inform others of their mental states for fear of being embarrassed or defined as a non-conformist and complainer. Though Japan's suicide rates have decreased during the last two decades, suicide remains the highest cause of death among those aged fifteen to thirty- nine. OECD data for 2013 indicate the suicide rate for Japan was 18.7 per 100,000 per capita (U.S.: 13.1) and one of the highest among the advanced industrialized societies.

Younger Japanese often do not know how to enjoy themselves without guidance from a group. A worrisome phenomenon is the contemporary pattern of late teens and young adults becoming self-incarcerators (hikikomori or NEET: Not in Education, Employment or Training). Hikikomori are defined by the government as those who are not in the labor force, housewives, students, unpaid workers in family businesses, or not looking for work. Hikikomori includes (mostly) young males who have stayed in their homes for six months or more. They stay in their rooms except for meals and have a large suicide rate. Such hikikomori have totally given up being members of the general society, in large part because of their dissatisfaction with the educational system and lack of employment prospects.

When asked to list their favorite leisure activities and hobbies, Japanese tend to list trivial things such as listening to music, playing video games, reading, and spending their free time sleeping or shopping. Most leisure activities involve group activities such as a weekend visiting mountain areas. The major source of personal happiness among salarymen is achieving wa with their occupational groups.

They are usually too busy fulfilling their work responsibilities to engage in personally-selected activities.

A major reason for this lack of individually- preferred leisure activities is that most Japanese, young or old, have very little free time. Students are expected to spend most of their time studying at school or in "cram schools" (see section 11.4 above) or in school-sponsored club activities. The happiest group in Japan is made up of single working women aged 18-25 years old. Most live with their parents to save money for their marriage and spend a portion of their pay on consumer goods for themselves and for leisure activities.

Long working hours are common for both students and workers at all levels. Leslie Buck
