(2017) became an apprentice to a Kyoto master gardener. Crew members regularly worked ten to twelve hours a day, six days a week, irrespective of the weather (at times in freezing temperatures). Buck learned to be humble and to accept her subordinate positions of being younger, female, foreign, and an apprentice even when her mentor. shouted at her and ridiculed her lack of skill (understood as showing interest in an apprentice with potential). Buck nevertheless gradually became skilled in a step-by-step process (kaizen) guided by her mentor/supervisor. Her work allowed her very little, if any, free time except for one day each week.

1.19. Suggested Readings

De Mente, Boyé Lafayette (1997) The Japanese Have a Word for It: The Complete Guide to Japanese Thought and Culture. Lincolnwood, Illinois:

Passport Books.

Donahue, Ray T. editor (2002) Exploring Japaneseness: On Japanese Enactments of Culture and Consciousness. Westport, Connecticut: Ablex Publishing

Gerlach, Michael L. (1992) Alliance Capitalism: The Social Organization of Japanese Business. Berkeley, California: University of California Press.

Matsumoto, Michihiro (1988) The Unspoken Way. Haragei: Silence in Japanese Business and Society. Tokyo: Kodansha International.

Mitsubishi Corporation (1988) Tatemae and Honne: Distinguishing Between Good Form and Real Intention in Japanese Business Culture. New York: The Free Press.

Palumbo, Frederick A. and Paul A. Herbig (1974) "Salaryman Sudden Death Syndrome," Employee Relations 16(1): 54-61.

Schmidt, Klaus D. (1978) Doing Business in Japan. Palo Alto, California: Stanford Research Institute International.

2

JAPANESE SOCIAL

ETIQUETTE

2.1. The Japanese Introduction

The first step in establishing a personal relationship is the introduction. Japanese seldom introduce themselves because it is seen as too aggressive. Nor do they like to deal with strangers. A self- introduction does not begin the trust process and the best way to meet people in Japan is for someone to introduce the two parties. Salarymen often enlist the help of professional introducers or shokai-nin. These develop wide networks of acquaintances and are very useful in connecting potential business partners. Trading companies (sogo shosha) and banks also provide introducers for their customers. Trading companies dominate the export-import business and they once had a nearly complete monopoly on imports and exports. Their personnel

are very internationally-oriented and they develop and maintain extensive private and government contacts. After an agreement to conduct business is achieved, the shokai-nin is also used to deliver bad news or to explain why ill feelings exist. He is the one the Japanese partner is most likely to call to transmit complaints and to settle any future disputes that arise.

The responsibility of the shokai-nin is so great that he might refuse to take on the role of introducer if he thinks one party will not act honorably. The other party will blame the introducer for the dishonesty and consequently loses face and reputation. There are a number of western expatriates living in Japan who serve as introducers. These consultants develop extensive networks and may be respected by the Japanese corporate officials one wishes to meet.

The Japan External Trade Organization (JETRO) was established in 2003 to promote Japanese international trade. JETRO officials often serve as introducers when the issue is the export of Japanese goods. JETRO members also help foreigners establish offices in Japan and provide them with limited legal advice. JETRO personnel offer advice and provide shokai-nin services if the matter is important for Japan's economy.

The Japanese Chamber of Commerce, the Industry, Organization for Small and Medium Enterprises and Regional Innovation (SME Support, Japan), the American Chamber of Commerce, and other international organizations are additional sources of introducers. Virtually all Japanese with managerial positions, including those living in the U.S. are members of business-related groups and can become shokai-nin to help fellow members.

There are other organizations that are possible sources of introducers. Many Japanese cities and universities have sister counterparts in the U.S. and throughout the world. These offer opportunities to meet Japanese officials and perhaps available as introducers.

Making the proper introductions is necessary because first impressions are very important to Japanese. The first impression they make of someone is often the lasting one. This is especially true when the first meeting or introduction gives an impression of whether a person looks and acts as if he were trustworthy and capable of becoming a friend. First impressions include appearance, who is the go-between, and behavior. A high-prestige introducer gives face to both persons involved because it is important that both parties respect the person making the introduction. A high-prestige introducer ensures respect at least during the first introductory meeting. There are now numerous consultants fluent in Japanese (or English) who can facilitate first introductions (see section 5.18 below).

2.2. The Japanese Bow and Handshake

The Japanese culture is not a touching culture and even relatives seldom touch each other in public. Instead of shaking hands, Japanese bow to each other as a greeting. The bow is also used to indicate respect of others. It is difficult for strangers to meet in Japan because they do not know how much respect they should show strangers by the depth of their bows and their use of respectful titles and vocabulary. The general rule is that the higher prestige person bows less deeply than the one with

lower prestige or rank.

It is important to bow to all persons, especially those who are older and therefore holding senior ranks, even if the foreigner is ignorant of the fine points of the bowing etiquette. A simple nod of the head is the minimum while the lowered head and a small bow of a few degrees with the upper torso is politer. Not bowing may be understood as an unfriendly or proud attitude. Japanese constantly bow to one another even when talking on the telephone. It is amusing to see men and women using cell phones in public constantly bowing to the distant recipients.

Jack Seward (see section 2.21. below), whose books on Japanese culture we highly recommend, summarizes a study on the extent Japanese bow during the day. A typical manager bows over a hundred times a day and an elevator girl greeting customers as they use a department store's escalator may bow over two thousand times a day. Bowing is a normal part of greeting and showing respect, and foreigners in Japan should adopt the practice of bowing to colleagues, subordinates, and superiors. Japanese corporations teach employees how to bow correctly to colleagues and customers. Department stores use machines to train sales personnel as to the proper way to bow.

Men bow with their arms held straight down with hands flat on their thighs; women bow with folded hands in front. People bow looking slightly to the left to notice from the corner of their eyes how deep is the other's bow; if the other's bow was deeper,

the person will repeat his bow again, this time deeper to compensate for the other's politeness. Bowing slightly to the right avoids bumping heads with another person. There are times when

older Japanese might bow several times to show

additional respect.

There are four levels of bows based on how deep the bow is made: equals bow with the upper torso bent at fifteen degrees; one's superior or customer receives a thirty-degree bow; high-ranking VIPS receive 45-degree bows; finally, apologies demand a 90-degree bow, such as when a husband comes home late from bar-hopping with his business colleagues and greets his wife.

Many Japanese now shake hands with westerners though they themselves do not like to do so. If a Japanese holds out his hand, grasp it gently and share a quick handshake while making a small bow; no handshake should be hard and crushing since Japanese handshakes tend to be "limp fish" in character. Some older Japanese may not know how to end a handshake for fear of seeming impolite, as if too short a handshake may be interpreted as not polite enough. It is best in such instances to make the handshake fairly short and take the initiative to end the shake. Introductions and shaking hands are accompanied with lowered eyes because prolonged eye contact is seen as impolite. Do not feel insulted with others' lack of eye contact. They are merely showing respect by avoiding too much eye contact.

2.3. Apologies

Foreigners dealing with Japanese experience frequent apologies; it is said that Japanese apologize more than any other people. The origin of such extensive apologies lies partly in the Confucian ethos emphasizing that people respect and subordinate themselves to superiors. In addition,

strict obedience to social superiors during Japan's feudal period was expected of everyone. Even members of the warrior class, the samurai, were expected to completely obey their superiors. Failing executing an order from a feudal lord could result in the person committing seppuku, or ritual suicide. Ordinary Japanese were also expected to follow strict rules of conduct or could be summarily executed. The result was the development of rigid etiquette systems for each social class and it became safer to continuously apologize even when a fault had not occurred or one was innocent. In addition, it would have been impossible as well as impractical to punish everyone for minor infractions of etiquette. The compromise was to expect people to apologize; the superior could then show benevolence by forgiving the culprit and place him in the leader's debt.

Contemporary Japanese continue the custom of frequent apologies. There are two common forms of business-related apologies in letter form: wabi jo and shimatsu sho. The first is sent to customers when apologies are in order. When any serious problem or error occurs, such as a missing or late shipment, or faulty products, it is customary to first personally telephone a customer to apologize, then mail a wahi jo apology. After the apology is mailed, the custom demands that a representative of the offending company be sent to personally apologize to the customer for the mishap. A serious error demands a personal visit by the head of the division or department.

The second form of apology, shimatsu sho, is an in-house letter in which the person in charge of the group where a mistake occurred sends a letter to his superior apologizing for the mistake and accepting

responsibility. He describes the department's error, indicates regrets, promises the event will never happen again, and concludes with a plan to improve protocols. If the error was serious in nature, the letter may be followed by some punitive action. An apology to friends and colleagues might be vague to save face. The fact that an apology has been offered is more important than its content or specificity. The issue is closed after an apology has been offered, and it is a breach of etiquette not to accept an apology. Foreigners not used to the frequency of apologies should be prepared to both accept apologies and issue them in turn. Even innocent persons apologize. Someone who is a victim of an auto accident will apologize to the police and to the guilty person.

Among Japanese an apology is often the equivalent to thanks and in fact Japanese say, "I apologize" (sumimasen) in situations when a U.S. American would say, "Thank you." The meaning of the word is vague and does not refer to anything specific and it is used to cover all possible past, present, and future situations. In addition, "sumimasen" acknowledges that one has not yet reciprocated when a favor has been done. Thus, "sumimasen" also tells the listener, "Thank you; 1 will repay you and I apologize that I have not done so yet." Japanese assume that any favor implies effort and "sumimasen" also means both thanks and an apology for bothering someone. Sumimasen is also used to get someone's attention as when calling a server. In such cases, the term means both "Excuse me" and "Thank you." The latter is a show of appreciation before the request is made.

Apologies are frequently repeated a number of times. A younger speaker may repeat, "I apologize"

several times if the situation is tense or the offense is serious. It is common among speakers to repeat words to stress their importance and apologies are customarily exaggerated to indicate serious concern. This can be accomplished by overstating possible consequences of an awkward situation. A Japanese may apologize by saying, "I am sorry you will be late for your next appointment" when arriving late to a meeting. Apologies may include excuses, though this is not commonly done. The best apologies indicate humility since being humble is a major value in Japanese society. While a westerner may inform someone, he does not feel well, a Japanese would apologize for having a cold!

It is possible to apologize and indicate innocence at the same time ("The slow traffic made me late for this meeting and I apologize for being late"). It is important to show regret even when forces beyond control caused the situation. The ideal apology includes mention of the speaker's emotional state. It is also best to include in an apology a reference to the other person's feelings. Doing so shows empathy and an understanding of the other person's perspective. Other apologies indicate there is no possible remedy ("There is no excuse for my behavior and my being late has disturbed you. Thank you for being so patient.").

Jeremiah L. Sullivan (1992) describes the instance of a Japanese manager giving his U.S. American subordinate an order to ship to another division all lots smaller than twenty-five units. A month later the manager became enraged when his own superior told him there remained numerous lots of twenty-five units in the warehouse. The American subordinate said she had shipped out all lots smaller than twenty-five as ordered. It became

obvious that the Japanese manager has given the wrong order due to a faulty knowledge of English. Unfortunately, the American subordinate did not understand correct etiquette procedures (kata) for this situation and instead insisted that she had been given the wrong order. What she should have done is to accept the blame and immediately ship the required lots. Instead, she blamed the superior. Her best strategy in this case would have been to accept the blame and apologize, thereby maintaining good relations with her superior. The misunderstanding would have been cleared up later, perhaps when the two were drinking together. Japanese are forgiving of mistakes but they are less forgiving of stubbornness and an unwillingness to maintain group harmony and respect. Mistakes can be tolerated as learning experiences as long as they are not repeated!

2.4. Polite Restraint

A Japanese asked if he would like a drink will respond with, "No, thank you" This response is part of the custom of enryo, meaning "reserve" or "hesitation." Enryo means not being greedy or demanding and "no" is always the first response to any offer. When offering tea to a guest it is better to hand her a cup of tea rather than accepting a denial at face value. Politeness demands one to three rejections of any offer although rejections are social noises not to be taken literally. It is better to state, "Please take some tea" than asking, "Will you have tea?" The latter question begs for a polite refusal. It is difficult to reject any offer made by Japanese since they will assume rejection is merely politeness. In such a situation, it is best to accept

any invitation or offer after refusing it several times to show humility.

Polite restraint is especially evident in the manner Japanese speak. There is a reluctance to say too much, to seem to dominate a conversation, or to seem to make demands. The Japanese saying "Hear one, understand ten" encourages the speaker to use as few words as possible. Japanese audiences do not appreciate elegant speeches and ornate rhetoric. High-prestige speakers instead are cryptic. Speakers assume their audiences understand their nonverbal messages.

Recommendations are stated in a general form. A Japanese may note that, "Japan has a good train system" and mean that the listener should ride the train from Tokyo to Osaka rather than fly. Many statements also start with the words "maybe" and "I think" that are polite but are not meant to convey uncertainty. A Japanese who says, "Perhaps you are tired" is suggesting that the meeting has lasted long enough and the visitor should call for an adjournment and go to his hotel. The statement "Perhaps you are hungry" is a suggestion that it is time to end a meeting for lunch. The speaker is being modest and indirect; he is not being indecisive. Similarly, it is important to pay attention to indirect suggestions in order to anticipate the wishes of others. Often, a Japanese asks, "Would you like some tea?" when he himself wants tea. A colleague may ask, "Do you want to eat now?" as a hint that he is hungry but does not want to be too direct. Japanese hesitate making direct requests as they believe doing so threatens solidarity with other members of a group.

Japanese use silence as a means to communicate and use fewer words than other nationalities. A

Japanese manager in France compared French and Japanese patterns of communication in this way:

When the French want to say 100 things, they will verbalize 150 things. When Japanese say 70 things, they are trying to get the other person to understand 100.

It is therefore important to use restraint when communicating with Japanese colleagues.

Restraint should also take place when receiving compliments. Success is understood to be the result of a group or team, especially within a business context, and someone who receives compliments should not take full credit even when he or she is fully responsible. The term usually used for "thank you" is okagesama de. The term, however, is more literally translated as "Because of being in your great shadow," or more simply as "Thanks to you (or everyone). The proper response to "Congratulations on your completing the project" demands a response indicating that many persons are also to be congratulated, or okagesama de. A mere "thank you" does not recognize a team's effort. U.S. Americans must learn not to take personal credit when complimented. In addition, including the other person(s) as part of the praise highlights that a social connection exists in that your victory is also another's. Acknowledging a compliment without giving partial credit to others may also give the impression that you are better than others, and public humility is necessary to become accepted.

Japanese find the body language of U.S. Americans both too aggressive and too informal. Typically, U.S. businessmen gesticulate with their arms too much from the Japanese perspective. They also wave their hands too much, pound on the table, or close their

fists when making a point. U.S. Americans also take too much space when sitting down at a table. Japanese at a meeting place their papers on the chair next to them or in neat piles on the table in front of them. They do not lounge or appear to be comfortable in the U.S. American fashion. Nor do they show much emotion during a meeting; they do not frown, raise their voices, or use body language indicating anger or disagreement. Even crossing one's legs is considered a bad mannerism, especially when higher-ranking persons are present.

2.5. Never Say "No"

It is best not to ask a Japanese a question demanding either a "yes" or "no" answer. Because Japanese do not like to say "no," such a question is answered either by a "yes" or by a vague statement that does not obligate the speaker. Saying "no" bluntly is considered impolite and aggressive. A blunt, honest answer is seldom given in public since is possibly injurious to social relationships. As a result, Japanese conversations are circuitous and ambiguous.

it

The reluctance to stating a "no" often results in generalized responses that indirectly offer information why a "no" is appropriate without being negative. The process for not saying "no" involves a general discussion hinting that a request is impossible or difficult to achieve. A U.S. American might ask a Japanese colleague if a report can be completed in two weeks. Instead of simply saying, "No, the report needs more work," a Japanese might mention he has a number of meetings during the next two weeks and that an important customer is visiting the office, and he wants to discuss problems

with the shipment of products. He will not say, "No" directly but expects the American to infer from the answer that a two-week deadline is impossible. A common response to avoid being negative is "chotto muzukashii," or "It will be difficult."

.

.

.

.

.

MESSAGES THAT MIGHT MEAN "NO"

We will be glad to consider this later.

We need to study this proposal (or point) more closely.

Yes, your plan is very interesting.

It may be difficult to do as you suggest.

Yes, we understand.

We are not certain about this.

We should consider this during the next meeting.

We will try our best.

We need to consider your proposal.

We must think about it.

While U.S. Americans take pride in being blunt and direct in their conversations, this behavior is counterproductive among Japanese. Generally speaking, a silence or a vague answer can often be translated as a negative response. Often a "no" is conveyed by a vague answer such as, "It is not possible for our section alone to decide on that point." A request for more information might suggest a negative response such as, "We would like you to give us more details on your pricing policy." If the answer to the question "When can we expect a decision on our price offer?" is silence

or an avoidance of a direct, factual answer, then the hidden answer is some unhappiness about price. Silence is also used between sentences to slow the pace of the conversation and to allow the speaker to think. Shinomiya and Szepkouski (2002) recommend waiting ten seconds or so before responding to a question and as occasional pauses between sentences.

There are also a number of gestures that indicate displeasure or negativity. Sucking air loudly through the teeth signals displeasure or unhappiness. In the same way, placing hands on the back of the neck suggests disinterest, confusion, and rejection to what is being discussed, though some Japanese may do the same to relax. Frowning as well as pinching one's eyebrow are ways of indicating disagreement. By contrast, someone who listens with his eyes shut is indicating high consideration and concentration.

2.6. Conversational Feedbacks

Conversations include messages from active listeners through both speech and body language. Japanese listeners encourage others to speak by nodding, and by saying "Yes," and "I see." A common form of feedback is "hai" (yes). In this case, however, a nod or a "yes" does not indicate agreement. Instead, these messages indicate the listener is listening and paying attention. Even the response "I see" does not automatically indicate understanding. The listener is merely indicating he is listening; whether he agrees or understands is another matter.

Silence during a conversation may at times indicate disagreement or confusion. Such behaviors

are preferred to overt disagreements, and the participants may not know how to react politely. The correct response in such cases is to copy the other person and accept a pause in the conversation; additional words or explanations merely add to the problem rather than solve it. If the situation is caused by confusion, the Japanese colleague may merely be taking time to think seriously how to answer or react properly. It is best not to demand a response but rather wait until a response is eventually given. Not pushing the pace of conversation eases the tension that may exist and allows for easier communication.

2.7. The Japanese Business Card

Exchanging business cards (meishi) is an important ritual and taken seriously. The exchange of cards is considered the first part of introductions. How this exchange is conducted is a major element in the evaluation of persons since first impressions are very important. A business card website reported that the average Japanese salaryman exchanges about 2,500 business cards annually, for a remarkable total of over eight billion in Japan. Those who do not have business cards available can write down their names, telephone numbers, and their employers' names on pieces of paper, though business cards are much more appropriate.

Those going to Japan on business normally take at least ten times the number of business cards usually needed elsewhere. Bilingual cards can be ordered online and delivered quickly in Japan, though it is wise to have someone knowledgeable in both languages to check the translation. International-class hotels have the facilities to

quickly translate and print cards in-house. Not exchanging cards is seen as reluctance or inability to establish a personal relationship. Similarly, running out of business cards is seen as an act of inefficiency. A lack of business cards also suggests the person is not important enough to have a business card or that he has no organizational authority. There are occasions when every member of a large welcoming group receives a business card, and leaving someone out suggests inattention to details, bad preparation and planning, or as a personal insult. Expect to exchange many more cards than is customary in the U.S. Japanese do not like to discuss business with strangers until business cards have been exchanged. Do not suggest postponing an exchange of cards until after a meeting because of an eagerness to begin business discussions.

Just as introducing oneself is seen as rude, asking a Japanese for his business card is also a violation of business protocols (kata). The correct procedure is to offer a business card first and expect one in return. The gesture of not exchanging a business card after one has been offered suggests rejection and an unwillingness to do business, though that will seldom, if ever, occur. Usually, presidents of companies do not exchange business cards because cards are included in the first correspondences and because it is assumed that high-ranking members of different organizations already know each other. even when meeting for the first time. There should have been a number of written exchanges before the first meeting. At times, the introducer gives each party the others' cards before they meet.

It is also important to carry business cards at all times while in Japan. Japanese businessmen

exchange cards in non-business environments,

when playing golf, or when meeting someone at a restaurant. Cards should be handy and easily available, and fumbling for a card makes the person look foolish and unprepared.

Business cards in Japan are made of good quality paper since they reflect on their owners and they are central to providing a positive first impression. They should also be bilingual with Japanese script on one side and English on the other. Bilingual business cards provide Japanese with a pronunciation guide of foreign names. There are four sounds in the English language that do not exist in Japanese, and a card with the U.S. American's name in the phonetic katakana script helps those who are not skilled in English (Kato and Kato, 1992). The title should be as specific as possible on the Japanese side since there are many empty and symbolic work titles in Japan. A vague title suggests low prestige in the company or a position that is more honorific than functional.

One offers the business card with two hands and a small bow. Using two hands to offer and receive business cards is very important. A one-handed offering is impolite and indicates inattention or disinterest. In Japan, all objects are offered and received with two hands to show respect unless those involved are close friends. Japanese accustomed to dealing with U.S. Americans will likely offer to shake hands at this time and the process can be confusing for those who are not prepared to both bow and shake hands. Two hands again grasp the card should a handshake occur after a bow. The card is handed with the Japanese side on top and right side up so that the receiver does not have to flip the card one hundred and eighty

degrees to read the information. State your name, title, and employer or company as the card is offered to another person. Do not make joking comments about your card, another's card, or someone's name. Receiving cards is also done with both hands to show respect for the other person. The card is then studied carefully for a few moments to indicate interest. If possible, it is customary to refer to the card after perusal by making relevant comments such as, "I have great respect for your company." The reader should also look impressed, even a little surprised, when looking at the other's card at the other's high rank or prestigious employer. The card is then placed in a card case. For first meetings, the business cards of others are placed on the table to use as a reference if needed. They are placed in a card case after the end of a meeting.

Business cards are never casually placed in a pocket. Card cases should be of good quality to show respect for one's own and others' cards. Japanese notice the quality of a card's paper, typing, and case. Nor should the card case be placed in a back pocket as doing so indicates lack of respect and of "sitting on someone" because that person is not considered to be very important. Cards stored in a back pocket and taken out in public are seen as "coming from one's behind" and form insulting gestures. Cards are never folded or defaced in any way. Another person's card is never written on in his presence. It is better to make notes on note paper rather than someone else's card.

Japanese business cards offer information in reverse order to that of western cards though the English translation will be in the western order. The name of the company comes first, then title, and then name (reading columns from right to

left). This order reflects the importance of group membership and the relative lack of individualism in Japanese society. Japanese initially evaluate strangers more on the companies they work for rather than on personal achievements.

Business cards can be accompanied with letters of introduction called shokaijo. This refers to the practice of sending a letter along with one's business card to recommend the receiver to a third party. These should be used infrequently since an introduction in any form makes you responsible for the person recommended. Since Japanese are suspicious of strangers, using a business card as an introduction (shokaijo) creates a relationship of potential trust among three persons. In turn, any failure on the part of one member can destroy the trust between the other two. All involved lose face and the relationships are damaged.

Japanese businesspersons keep business cards in special files and use them for future reference. If the gift exchange stage is achieved, Japanese businessmen note in their files of gifts given and received from each of the cards' donors. This is also the place to note a person's personal hobbies and interests for future reference. The importance of business cards among Japanese is illustrated by the habit of former Prime Minister Yasuhiro Nakasone studying before he went to sleep all the meishi he had received during the day. Since an important government official may receive hundreds of business cards each day, Minister Nakasone thought cards were important enough for a closer analysis at the end of each day. Stationery stores offer meishi albums, making storage easier and providing easier reference of gifts given and received.

2.8. The First Meeting

First meetings, called "aisatsu," are primarily social events allowing the participants to develop familiarity and trust. The term "aisatsu" means "greeting" and indicates that the purpose of the first meeting is to develop personal networks with those who plan to conduct business together. No business is discussed during this first meeting. If business is discussed, most likely during the last few moments of the meeting, the conversation is abstract and general. The Japanese are not prepared to "get down to business" at this point. This is the opportunity when participants indicate a willingness to conduct business together and that, hopefully, the participants want a long-term relationship.

Japanese belong to a gift-giving culture and those attending an aisatsu usually exchange small gifts. These are tokens of appreciation and need not be expensive because they are merely symbols indicating friendship and sociability. Unusually expensive gifts are likely to be seen as potential bribes or as an inappropriate eagerness to establish business ties too quickly. Gifts to avoid are those that cut or packed in fours or in nines. Cutting objects symbolize the cutting of a relationship and the numbers four and nine are unlucky. The most typical gift is candies or other sweets individually wrapped so the receiver can share it with his/ her colleagues. A wide variety of baked goods and Japanese confectionery that are nicely (and appropriately) wrapped as well as other suitable gifts are easily available at department stores, international-class hotels, and shopping malls at major train stations. Office-related articles with company logos are acceptable, including desktop

items, photo stands, and the like. These gifts are symbolic and their values are unimportant. Better gifts are exchanged when a contract has been signed.

The shokai-nin, or introducer, if one was used to introduce both parties to one another, is present if possible. Often the shokai-nin is the one who arranged the first meeting and is considered to be the symbolic host. If he does not attend the first meeting he will have discussed the characteristics of the personnel and companies involved and praised both parties. He offers a letter of introduction along with his business card to each leader. The best scenario, of course, is for the shokai- nin to be present to make the formal introductions and lead the conversation, if necessary. He may even suggest when the next meeting should take place. The host pays for the expenses of the first meeting

Contrary to the U.S. American custom, the lowest ranked person is introduced first, and the process continues until the last person introduced has the highest prestige or highest corporate position of those present. A retired official may occasionally be present. He has no official position in the company but maintains his high prestige after retirement through the force of his personality, seniority, and social networks. His presence indicates the hosts want to show respect to the guests. This retiree is called an insei and wields personal rather than organizational power. It is worth noting again that higher-ranked Japanese officials talk less during a meeting than their subordinates. Especially during first meetings, the presence alone of a higher- ranked official is sufficient to indicate approval of the meeting and he is not expected to say

very much. The most senior official present may give a short welcoming speech and some neutral concluding statements, but he is not expected to take an active part in the early discussions.

It is common in the U.S. to describe oneself fully during a first meeting. Since first impressions are important it follows that one should give out as much information as possible about one's qualities and characteristics. The Japanese manner is to say less and let personal information be given by a third party or by company literature. U.S. Americans meeting Japanese for the first time should also say as little as possible about themselves. Answers to questions should be short and avoid any boasting. The Japanese customarily discover the details of someone they are interested in before they decide on further business dealings. Third parties are allowed to praise an individual since such praise is part of one's social reputation or face. It is acceptable to promote someone else by saying, "I wish you to meet my most talented engineer."

If the aisatsu goes well the Japanese leader may ask the foreign party out to supper. The invitation is considered an extension of the aisatsu and no business is discussed during the evening. There may be an additional invitation to go to a nightclub or bar after the meal. Foreign visitors can claim fatigue because of jet lag if they wish to decline, though the invitation should nevertheless be accepted if possible. Japanese often deliver good news at the end of a social event and the Japanese leader may suggest when the next meeting should take place as the guests leave the club to catch their

taxis.

2.9. Pointing and Other Gestures

Pointing with a finger is seen as insulting by Japanese. Pointing is done with the open hand rather than with an outstretched first finger if it must be done at all. To indicate oneself, Japanese commonly place the first finger on the nose. Use the open palm to indicate a figure, chart, or object.

Japanese use few arm and hands movements. The best strategy is to move body parts as little as possible when talking to Japanese since gestures are highly culture-bound and some may offend in one culture but not in another. Raising one's thumb can mean either "boyfriend/male" (depending on the context) or "good/well done" as it does in the U.S. Similarly, raising one's little finger means "girlfriend/female." Among Japanese, a circle made with thumb and index finger means "money" and does not indicate pleasure or "OK." Tapping oneself lightly with an open palm means, "I'll do it," or "leave it to me." The gestures of crossing one's finger for luck and air quotes make no sense to Japanese. There are other gestures and body movements that have specific meanings among Japanese. The sound of air being sucked through teeth indicates disagreement or displeasure. Tapping oneself lightly with an open palm means "I'll do it" or "Leave it to me." Waving the vertically-held right hand up and down stands for "Excuse me." This is usually accompanied with a bow and used in public among strangers as when someone steps on a foot while standing in a crowded commuter train. Waving an open hand held horizontally means "Come here."

Many gestures are specific to companies, and those wishing to deal with Japanese must quickly learn the customs (kata) that are company-specific. At YKK, the world's largest manufacturers of

zippers, the president dons a company cap and white jacket to walk around an office or factory floor to observe employees at work. When he wears cap and white jacket, any worker is allowed to ask Otsuka Yoshiharu a question or speak to him informally on any topic. When he wears a dark business suit, however, workers are not allowed to talk to him unless he talks to them first (Lu, 1986).

2.10. Laughter, Smiles, and Politeness

Laughter expresses different emotions in Japan in addition to amusement. The Japanese phrase "aiso- warai" is translated as "polite laughter" and as "false laughter." The term refers to a diplomatic reaction to a tense situation when a person does not know how to react. Polite laughter occurs when a Japanese does not understand what is being said, often when the message is spoken in a non-Japanese language. Laughter is also used to express surprise and when the person does not know how to respond. It is also used to cover any embarrassment that might otherwise demand an apology or correction. Finally, a laugh may indicate an unwillingness to say something negative. It becomes a message that the listener does not agree with the speaker but does not want to express criticism directly. It is best to ignore what may seem like inappropriate laughter when speaking in English to Japanese colleagues. Repeat what has just been said in a different manner or ignore the situation completely and return to the point later.

In the same way, smiles at times do not always indicate pleasure in Japan. A smile may be given when the listener does not know how to answer the speaker or does not want to answer directly.

An embarrassed Japanese also smiles rather than saying something he does not want to express openly.

In the same way, being polite is not always a sign of respect or welcome. Japanese do not want to offend and disturb wa, even with foreigners. The result is an automatic behavior in order not to offend. Workers dealing with the public are expected to be polite to customers; stores position staff, usually attractive women, at doors to bow and welcome customers as they enter. Being polite is part of dealing with customers. The term reigi ("politeness makes perfect") stresses that being polite to others is part of expected behavior.

2.11. Titles and Names

Japanese follow the practice of writing their name with the family name first then their personal name. They may reverse this order when dealing with westerners so attention must be made of the correct sequencing of names. Introductions always use only the family name as in "I wish to present to you Director Matsunaga-san" when being introduced to Hiroshi Matsunaga (order in the western manner). The third party will never introduce someone as "Matsunaga Hiroshi." Surnames are often accompanied with honorific suffixes with "san" being the most common, as in "Matsunga-san" or "Director Matsunaga-san."

Strangers call each other by family names while personal names are seldom used except among close friends and relatives. Even close friends call one another by their family name with a more informal suffix such as "Hello, Watanabe-chan," or "Hello, Watanabe-kun." Close friends often give one

another nicknames based on family names: "Hello, Nabe-chan." Close friends also call one another by their first names after being shortened, as in "Sabu- chan" when the given name is Saburo. Family members often call children "chan." Close friends and family members may also use that honorific when the child becomes an adult. This usage is restricted to family members and is not used by outsiders. Childhood friends also give each other nicknames based on personal attributes, much as is done in the west. Teachers and schoolmates add the honorific "kun" to students' family names, as in "Fukurai-kun." Outsiders avoid using this honorific even when co-workers and former schoolmates use the term. These distinctions between given and family names are still rigidly maintained by older Japanese and given names are never used at work. People also call each other by honorific. titles. Present and former students always call their teachers sensei. The president of a company is invariably addressed as shacho by employees and by outsiders who are younger or lower in rank. People with high rank are called by their titles. The term okyaku ("honorable guest") is used when foreigners, customers, and guests are involved. Professional greeters in department stores and clerks add the honorific "sama" to the previous word to indicate extreme respect as in "Welcome, okyaku-sama." The first form of address used is seldom changed during additional meetings. A teacher, for example, is addressed as "sensel" for the rest of the person's life. It is therefore important to use others' correct names and titles when being introduced for the first time. There is also no instant familiarity in terms of calling others by first names as is common in the U.S. No one in Japan is on a first-name basis except

family members, childhood friends, and former

schoolmates.

Many Japanese who customarily deal with westerners adopt western-style first names to make it easier for foreign English-speakers to remember their names and to follow the U.S. American practice of quickly getting on a first-name basis. A Japanese may say, "Call me George" to follow the U.S. American custom. This first name, however, should not be used when other Japanese are present since you will be the only one to use his first name.

2.12. Japanese-Style Email

The Japanese kata of communication requires frequent messages of respect toward the recipient, including face-to-face and other forms such as emails. The contents of emails should appear friendly, personal and humble. Normally straightforward and businesslike, the normal U.S. American way of writing emails will be interpreted by Japanese as too direct, impersonal, and even unfriendly. Emails, like letters, use a more formal form of communication with a number of traditional protocols.

The title of the message should be clear and describe the reason for the message, as in "On the schedule for our next meeting." Otherwise, the letter may be defined as spam and will not reach the intended addressee. Japanese paragraphs are shorter than US. American messages. Be prepared to divide the contents of the email (or letter) into smaller paragraphs, some in two or three lines. Japanese-style protocols for emails are similar to those found in letters and are depicted by the sandwich method of paragraph writing. The

business matter is placed between the greeting and apology. The use of honorific titles (Chairman, etc.) is always included at the start, no matter the closeness of the business relationship. The start of the email should not use the first name of the addressee. Use instead surname and title ("Vice President Abe"). First names alone are not used in business communications.

The first paragraph contains personal messages such as "I hope this email will find you well," "I appreciate all you and your company have done for us," "Allow me to thank you for the other day's business meeting (golf day, gift, etc.)," or "I am very grateful to you for your help." These messages set the tone of the communication.

The first sentence should identity the respondent ifs he is not well known to the addressee, such as "I am Georgia Allen in charge of product development of Imperial Industries." The second sentence in an email should contain an apology: "I apologize for bothering you, since I know you are busy." A personal message or a reference to the season is the equivalence to a bow. If possible, the first paragraph should also mention the season ("I hope you are not suffering from the summer's heat.") and something personal about the reader ("I apologize for this request for information since you must be very busy."). There is always time, according to Japanese etiquette, to be polite. Not all statements need to be business-related in any form of communication.

The second part of the message deals with the business issue. A comment of appreciation is appropriate as the first line of the second part, such as "I realize you are busy, but could we change the date of our next meeting?" The contents should be as comprehensive and detailed as possible when the

recipient is not familiar with the English language. When making a suggestion for a change, it is best to use interrogative sentences starting with "Could (Can) we..." or "Would (Will) it be possible if ..." so as not to be seen as giving direct orders. Responses for information can be direct in this paragraph.

The third part of the email (or letter) returns to polite protocols, and ends with a closing section showing appreciation for the relationship, such as "I look forward to hearing from you," and "Should you have any questions, do not hesitate to contact us." Right before signing "Best Regards," it is better to end with an apology: "Sorry to bother you while you are so busy, but please tell me if a change in scheduling is acceptable to you."

Email writing should stay formal even after both parties are on good business terms and slang should, as usual, be avoided. Large amounts of technical details should be sent by post or fax to keep the email from seeming to be too long or

redundant.

2.13. General Appearance

Japanese business dress requires a formal and neat appearance. Even taxi drivers (who also wear white gloves), convenience store clerks, secretaries, and those with service jobs in general wear formal clothes or wear uniforms of some type. White shirts are mandatory for those in non-manual occupations. Those who do not wear suits wear uniform-like jackets and windbreakers, often with ties. Suit colors are limited to dark or navy blue, and gray, though younger Japanese now wear less conservative attire. Businessmen do not wear

long hair or beards, nor do they wear accessories

such as bracelets, caps, and earrings. Tattoos among Japanese are regarded as lower class and as indicating gang memberships since organized gang members, the yakuza, sport tattoos. A major purpose of business fashion in Japan is to blend in and resemble everyone else. In factories, all personnel, including management and top officials, are likely to wear company uniforms (often a windbreaker or jacket) to reinforce conformity as well as following safety demands. Accessories indicating wealth such as expensive watches or jewelry are kept to a minimum.

Those who do not wear formal business attire are judged as not being serious and possibly not reliable enough to become business partners. Visitors to a Japanese office during Japan's hot and humid summers should not take off their jackets unless asked to do so. Office temperatures during the summer are kept higher to conform to the Ministry of the Environment's recommendation to save energy; warmer temperature also reduces corporate electric costs. Summer dress is now more relaxed among Japanese workers than winter attire but formal business attire is still expected irrespective of the season. Not bringing a coat to a summer meeting may give the impression you are not serious about work or the meeting. Remember, though, businesses in Japan now keep offices warmer during the summer months than in the past. This is a practice established by the government to conserve energy. More informal attire is allowed during the warmer months and all workers, including executives, are expected to wear more casual attire during daily activities. More formal occasions such as first meetings or the start of negotiations demand more formal attire. Formal

posture at all times is also expected, and business associates do not slouch, chew gum, place their feet on a table, or otherwise show disrespect to host or guest.

Part of one's general appearance includes the talking distance between two persons. Japanese stand farther apart than U.S. Americans. U.S. Americans find it difficult to talk comfortably with Japanese because they maintain less physical distance when talking to another person than do Japanese. The unconscious reaction for a U.S. American talking to a Japanese is to slowly decrease the distance between the two while the latter slowly inches backwards. U.S. Americans should be alert to this cultural difference and maintain the same social distance as the Japanese. Doing otherwise makes the Japanese uncomfortable and creates a negative impression.

In addition, Japanese culture is not a touching culture and adults seldom touch each other, even close friends. Business associates, no matter the quality or length of their friendship, never tap each other on the arm to emphasize a point, hug each other upon greeting, or slap one another's back as a sign of friendship and informality. Too much touching creates a negative impression.

2.14. General Gift Giving

Japanese show respect and a willingness to develop and maintain friendships through the frequent exchange of gifts. Gifts create a sense of debt suggesting the donor trusts the receiver to reciprocate in the future. If the receiver wants to develop the implied relationship then a gift of the same approximate value is offered in turn.

Offering gifts is one way to indicate a wish to develop relationships in a culture that has difficulty in speaking forthrightly. Gift giving is central for the creation and maintenance of friendships in business relationships and during other occasions. Omiyage is a gift given as a souvenir when someone returns from a vacation. Sashiire and osusowake are small portions of food and drink given to neighbors, friends, or colleagues working late. Offer a gift of food or alcohol whenever visiting a Japanese home. Japanese expect gifts to be appropriately wrapped and the wrapping and cord knots should correctly reflect the occasion. However, many Japanese are no longer familiar with the complex rituals involving wrapping of formal gifts, and department stores provide staff to correctly wrap articles for customers. A traditional custom is to bring a gift covered with an additional wrapping cloth called a furoshiki. The furoshiki is a cloth used to keep a gift in good state. It shows greater respect of the receiver, but the cloth is not included as part of the gift and is kept by the donor. Today, stores include attractive bags to contain gifts, and these bags are acceptable substitutes for the furoshiki.

Gifts are often given in pairs to show completeness, as in the pairs of male-female, yin- yang, heaven-earth, and his-and-her objects. Gifts do not come in fours or nines. The word for four, shi, sounds like "death" while nine (ku) sounds like the words "suffering" and "choking." Other unwelcome gifts include handkerchiefs, objects that cut (knives, scissors, letter openers) since they symbolize a wish to end (cut) the relationship, and objects associated with money such as money clips and wallets. In addition, Japanese prefer receiving objects in selected odd numbers. Gifts that are given in

threes, fives, and sevens suggest the asymmetrical beauty of nature and also reflect the Zen preference for rough, seemingly unfinished and spontaneous works of art. Gifts of cash are traditional, though they should be in odd-numbered amounts as in 5,000 yen (US$ 50), 30,000 yen, etc.

It is customary to refuse an offered gift several times to appear humble and appreciative of the gesture. The donor is expected to ignore this symbolic gesture of refusal and insist on giving the gift. By the same token, it is customary to practice humility when giving a gift with such comments as: "This gift is just a small sign of my appreciation," "Here is a little something for you," or "I hope you like it." Those who receive gifts thank the donors at this time though gifts are not usually opened in the presence of the donor or in public. Open the gift in private and send a note thanking the donor as soon as possible.

Japan has two major gift-giving times: ochugen at the end of June and early July, and oseibo at the end of November through the middle of December. There are many suitable gifts for ochugen and oseibo including tea, coffee, wine, brandy, whiskey, sake, candy, cookies, meat, dried fish, fruit, soy sauce, soaps, and detergent. In the past, most gifts were objects to be eaten or practical household items. Gift exchanges are considered so important that many companies provide appropriate gifts for their employees to give to customers, prospective clients, and business associates. Department store personnel also offer advice on what to give and how to package and wrap the appropriate gifts in the appropriate manner.

Most Japanese are interested in foreign countries and appropriate gifts include scenic books of non-

Japanese landscapes and tourist attractions. Local and handmade handicrafts are also gratefully received. Many Japanese enjoy drinking coffee and tea, making gourmet coffees or teas appropriate gifts. Gifts of liquors are welcomed since foreign alcohol is heavily taxed. The Japanese have developed a taste for European sweets and another welcomed gift is candy, especially gourmet chocolates. Such gurume (gourmet) gifts are appreciated. A recently-developed custom consists of female workers giving their male bosses and colleague boxes of chocolates to celebrate the U.S. American holiday of Valentine Day. There is now a celebration of White Day in March during which men give women gifts in return. The ideal gift is one that the receiver can share with others. Boxes of candy and cookies are ideal since their contents can be shared with coworkers and superiors, as are boxes of chocolates and mixed nuts.

Gifts can also indicate a personal knowledge in another's tastes and hobbies. Giving a set of golf balls to a colleague who plays golf shows personal interest as well as friendliness. More informal gifts include hats with logos of American sports teams or universities. Other important gifts are invitations to play golf, visiting a baseball game, or hosting other activities based on a guest's interests. If a Japanese has been invited to visit the U.S., a good gesture is to discover the leader's personal hobbies and interests and then organize outings to make the visit more enjoyable. Other gifts might be those that show some aspects of local culture. Officials at Texas A&M University habitually host barbecues for foreign guests. Each of the visiting dignitaries receives a pair of cowboy boots, a western-style hat, and a book on the history of the university and the

area.

Japanese appreciate quality goods with well- known brand names. Items made in Europe rather than China or other Asian countries are better

received. Gifts should be small because Japanese houses are relatively small from a U.S. American perspective and seldom have much storage space. A gift often overlooked by U.S. Americans is a series of photographs commemorating an event or visit. Group photographs are popular among Japanese and they often celebrate major events with photo sessions. Group photographs should be given to each member of a visiting party.

Children of business colleagues can be given objects featuring any currently popular cartoons or movie figures. Wives of business colleagues, should you feel obligated to offer them gifts, appreciate thick bathroom towels, cosmetics, seasonings, and body-care products. Gourmet foods are also

welcomed.

There is an etiquette associated with gift exchanges. When each member of a group receives a gift, its price or value differs according to rank; the senior guest receives the most expensive gift, the second most senior person receives a slightly less expensive gift, etc. If gifts are given to a group as a whole, the most convenient type of gift is one that contains individually-wrapped items for ease of sharing. Personal gifts are not opened while the donor is present unless the donor insists. Companies often give each other gifts and these may be opened during a banquet or other formal ceremony. The recipient often visits the donor a day or so after a gift has been offered to personally thank the donor. Such "thank-you" visits are short, and they allow the receiver to show his appreciation

after unwrapping a gift in private.

Japanese are now in the habit of exchanging greeting cards. They are even beginning to celebrate the year-end Christmas season by sending greeting cards. It is common to send business associates New Year's greeting cards although personal letters are also acceptable. One rule of thumb is that colleagues send New Year greetings to anyone they have recently exchanged business cards, especially if they expect to conduct business together in the future (Goldman, 1995). Such cards reinforce earlier meetings no matter how casual they might have been. If a greeting card is used, it is proper to mention the previous meeting. This suggests the meeting was important and has not been forgotten. A few added lines in a card make it more personal. Cards should be received during the first three days of January. The ideal time for a card to arrive is on January first.

An important period of business-related gift giving occurs after the official New Year's holidays. This period involves greeting all business associates, including customers, clients, and suppliers. This behavior, called aisatsu-mawari ("going around to pay respects"). At this time, courtesy calls (with gifts) are common. It is also appropriate at this time to send gifts to show appreciation for the past year's business. Important persons should be given gift commensurate with their rank. These gifts are sent to the receiver's office.

Following the kata of gift giving is important. We recommend that lists be kept of gifts received and given. This record avoids duplication of gifts and giving something less expensive or more expensive than gifts received. It is bad form to give the same

gift twice, as when a package of golf balls to a golf fan is given during two separate occasions. The second gift can be golf-themed but different, perhaps a new driver.

2.15. Expressing Complaints

Expressing complaints is done extremely indirectly or not at all except through body language, increased silence, or a third party. Complaints are best expressed by an intermediary. A complaint expressed through an intermediary lessens the sting and shows concern for maintaining the face (social honor; reputation) of others. The one who originally introduced the two parties makes an ideal intermediary because both parties trust him or her. Social occasions are also ideal for gently expressing complaints.

No matter how complaints are expressed, detailed reasons for the complaints should be emphasized since Japanese wish to understand the background of any issue. Within the context of a complaint, the reason for the complaint should include why the situation is not to the speaker's liking

It is also best to find an impersonal excuse for a complaint so that the other party does not feel embarrassed. For example, instead of bluntly saying, "Too many units are arriving damaged" one could start by saying, "How difficult it is to pack units so that there is no damage in transit." This comment alerts the listener that there may be problems with packing and shipping. He in turn may suggest, "We hope to decrease the breakage amount." This is not the time to argue whether or not the shipper is at fault. Remember, maintaining wa is paramount and that good feelings are

more important than being factual. Expressing complaints should be done very gently so that no feelings are hurt.

2.16. Japanese Eating Etiquette

Japanese prefer to eat lightly for the noonday meal; the evening meal is more formal and takes longer. Japanese believe breakfast is "gold" and is a more complete meal than its U.S. American counterpart. Japanese also do not like to entertain business colleagues during lunch, and business lunches are rare. Salarymen prefer to eat a quick meal of noodle soup, a bowl of rice with various meats or fish on top, or the contents of a bento box (a box containing rice, various meat toppings, and condiments). It is common for co-workers to eat their lunches at their desks while discussing business. Japanese also enjoy snacking throughout the day and when drinking

The central element of all meals is rice; the term "gohan" means both "meal" and "rice." A bowl of rice is always put on the left whereas a bowl of soup sits on the right, due to the Japanese tradition of placing important objects on the left side. It is bad manners to pour soy sauce or condiments on rice as it is eaten by itself. Nevertheless, some Japanese may enjoy a raw egg on rice seasoned with soy sauce in private settings. It is customary for a guest to say, "Ita daki masu" ("I now eat with gratitude") at the start of a meal. At the end of the meal the diner shows appreciation by saying, "Gochiso sama deshita" ("It has been a feast"). Meals usually end with a serving of green tea believed to help the digestion. Japanese meals seldom include desert though some fruit may be served as a last course. Guests invited for a meal

at someone's home leave shortly after the meal though the host and hostess will insist on the guest staying later.

Business-related guests are given formal meals and entertainments. A common business practice is hosting foreign guests to a settai, a ceremonial meal, banquet, or formal reception during the first week of arriving in Japan. The settai is held at a restaurant (resutoran), sushi restaurant or other dining places offering the party some privacy, often at an international-class hotel. The most typical place for a settai is a traditional Japanese-style restaurant called a ryotei. A ryotei offers geishas playing traditional musical instruments and singing songs in a traditional form of the Japanese language, and a garden for the guests to visit. Kappou is another type of restaurant offering formal Japanese cuisine where chefs cook in front of customers.

The mood during a settai begins in a formal matter and stays formal even though there will be numerous toasts and declarations of welcome, friendship, and appreciation. A settai often includes a professional toastmaster to deliver short messages of respect and welcome at the beginning and end of the evening.

A settai may also involve sightseeing or other leisure activities such as playing golf, theater- going, attending a baseball game, or any other event thought to be of interest to the senior guest. Mostly men attend settai since family members and spouses are seldom invited to any business functions of all types. There are no discussions of business issues because the purpose of a settai is to show hospitality and to learn more about the personal attributes of the guests.

Food portions in Japan are smaller though

Japanese consume larger amounts of rice than U.S. Americans. A typical Japanese meal is built around meat and vegetables that support rice. U.S. Americans should be prepared to eat less and be satisfied with smaller portions. Perhaps that is why only six percent of Japanese men are considered overweight as compared to thirty percent of U.S. Americans, according to a 2008 World Health Organization survey.

Sushi bars are also used to entertain business associates. Sushi bars in Japan are usually small, independently owned facilities. A novelty for foreigners is eating at a kaiten sushi (conveyor belt sushi). At these places, the chef's area is surrounded by a moat of slowly flowing water while various dishes of sushi are floated for the customers to grab what appeals to them. The bill is added up according to how many dishes a customer has at his place at the end of the meal. Nowadays, "kaiten sushi" also indicate sushi restaurants in general. Noodle shops are very popular and are found in neighborhoods and major train stations. Soba or buckwheat noodles, and udon or wheat noodles, are popular for light meals.

A convenient traveler's aid is the fact that restaurants, except the most exclusive, display models of their dishes where passersby can look at the selections offered inside. Dishes are often numbered so even a non-speaking foreigner can show the number of a desired dish to a server. The junior author has asked servers to go with him to the sidewalk to point out the wanted dishes or has copied the numbers of desired dishes to show to a server. Some dishes may be a little misleading to strangers. The junior author once ordered what looked to him to be skewers of chicken and found

he had ordered skewered squid. They look the same if you do not notice that one set of "chicken" strips has suckers. Japanese prefer to know what a restaurant has to offer and they look at the offerings of a number of restaurants before deciding where to eat. Similarly, menus often are accompanied with photos so even non-Japanese can have a good idea as to what they want to order. Japanese believe food must look attractive in addition to the taste. Models and photos are used by potential customers to evaluate the offerings of restaurants.

Japan has its own fast food traditions, although U.S. American-style quick foods, including McDonald's (almost 2,900 outlets in 2016) and Kentucky Fried Chicken (1,100 outlets in 2016), are also popular. KFC has convinced over three million Japanese that fried chicken is an appropriate Christmas meal (Barton, 2016). Many family. owned groceries include sections offering grilled meats on skewers, filled buns, dumplings, and other selections to passing pedestrians. Teriyaki, consisting of thinly cut strips of beef, chicken, or fish (usually a slice of yellowtail), is served with its own special sauce. Popular during the summer months are zaru soba noodles made with buckwheat and served cold with a unique sauce. There is a specialty of soba-ya restaurants that offer bowls of hot noodles containing various meats, and vegetables.

Many food kiosks also offer hiyashi chuka (cold noodles topped with egg, roast pork, bean sprouts, tomato, and cucumber), endamame (fresh green soybeans boiled in salt water), and hiyayakko (cold tofu). Japanese customarily eat grilled eels believed to recover energy and stamina and alleviate suffering from the summer heat on the day known

as the Midsummer Day of the Ox occurring around July 20%. In the winter, Japanese enjoy oden, a soup with many ingredients including soy sauce, sugar, and sake. Japanese household enjoy nabe or hot-pot cooking

U.S. Americans will find okonomiyaki familiar. Okonomiyaki consists of omelets fried like pancakes containing pork and vegetables. Probably copied from the Dutch, tempura-style ingredients are dipped in a special batter and deep-fried. Tempura contains seafood and vegetables. A similar technique, tonkatsu, involves meat chunks that are breaded and deep-fried.

Japanese often limit their conversations while eating. The foreign guest should not become alarmed if conversations decrease during a meal. In addition, the host initiates communication. There is a strict sense of hierarchy among Japanese forcing lower-ranking persons to defer to senior officials in terms of speaking, asking questions, and responding to what a senior person says.

Eating protocols are simple. In a Japanese-style meal, all dishes are usually served at the same time with each selection served in small dishes. The custom is to sample each dish in turn rather than finishing one dish and going to another. The diner eats rice by bringing the bowl to the mouth and shoveling the rice into the mouth with chopsticks. One never sticks chopsticks into a bowl of rice since that is the protocol when offering rice to ancestors during memorial services. The first course is miso soup made of fermented soybean paste and a broth. Miso soups contain a number of ingredients such as fish, meat, vegetables, seaweed, or tofu. Miso is so popular and pervasive that Japanese say, "That is the miso (charm, beauty] of it" when admiring

something.

2.17. Japanese Drinking Etiquette

The business culture is a drinking culture and alcohol plays a central part in business and in being sociable. The use of alcohol supports the belief that those who have had a few alcoholic drinks are more likely to be honest in what they are saying. Consequently, most business invitations include drinking sessions to allow for more open, relaxed, discussions. A relatively modern word is nominikeshon, which is coined from the Japanese word nomi (drinking) and the last section of the English word "communication." This word, now usually pronounced "nomincation" (see section 3.11 below) translates as "communication through drinking" and reflects the need to be slightly drunk as an enabler to frank conversations. While drinking together is important, the goal of drinking is to communicate more freely in a society bound by many kata and concern for one's and the others' face. While some Japanese drink to excess, the ideal drinker does not lose control nor does he drink so much that he cannot act socially correctly. According to a 2015 World Health Organization report of national consumption levels, Japanese per capita of alcohol was 7.5 liters (U.S.: 9.0).

The most common drinks are tea, beer, and sake. These are the beverages drunk during ordinary and formal Japanese meals. The most common beverage, tea, is usually green tea, but there exist many other available types of tea. The most formal tea drinking is done as the central part of the Japanese tea ceremony when tea is prepared and drunk in a very ritualistic manner. Japanese also

enjoy drinking coffee and there are many coffee shops in Japan's larger urban areas. Tea, however, is more traditional and it is drunk in a formal manner even in informal settings. Japanese are also avid consumers of imported wines while domestic wines are becoming more popular.

Sake is distilled rice wine. It is served in small cups that can be drunk in one large gulp though most drink sake using small sips. Sake is classified into four types in descending order of quality: ginjo- shu, junmai-shu, honjozo-shu, and futsu-shu. The best grade of sake has an admired bouquet and taste. Although sipping from small cups gives the impression that little alcohol is being consumed, the many toasts that accompany drinking sake result in large amounts being consumed during an evening; sake hangovers are painful. Sake is served warm or cold depending on the type and the season. Growing in popularity, Japanese also drink domestic shochu, distilled from rice, barley, sweet potatoes, or soybeans. Shochu is potent and drunk diluted with warm or cold water, ice, tea, or soft drinks.

Japan has a tradition of beer-brewing, and beer is often drunk during meals when co-workers eat together. Japanese commonly drink beer and sake when socializing with colleagues and friends. Umeshu, or plum liquor, is popular among Japanese women. There are now various types of made- in-Japan whiskies and wines. Imported spirits are heavily taxed and expensive when served in bars. The considerate guest avoids imported spirits and beers and asks for local beverages. Because of their expense and scarcity (especially liqueurs), imported spirits are appreciated gifts. The experienced

traveler buys duty-free alcohol at the airport before

leaving for Japan.

Since the main goal of a drinking session is to encourage the honne (honest) type of conversation, it is important to act relaxed and slightly drunk; otherwise your fellow-revelers may not believe you are talking in an honest fashion. It is, of course, important not to drink so much that rational conversation becomes impossible. There is a Japanese saying that warns of the dangers of drinking too much sake (Galef, 1987):

First the man takes a drink, then the drink takes a drink, then the drink takes the man.

Politeness demands ordering whatever others are drinking to show solidarity and conformity. Japanese beer bottles are larger than their western counterparts (while beer glasses are smaller) because it is assumed that more than one person will share a bottle. This encourages the drinker to take a few sips per glass and then let someone else pour additional beverage. Small glasses are a blessing when the party becomes more informal and raucous when the toasts call for "bottoms up" or "100%," meaning to drink a glass empty.

Drinking etiquette is simple. A person never pours for himself but pours instead for others. An attentive host constantly pours for guests to maintains full glasses. A guest pours for the person on his right. It is also customary for the main guest to show courtesy by pouring for his host in return. Pouring is done with two hands. In response, the drinker presents a cup (when drinking sake) or glass with two hands i.e., the left hand is placed underneath the cup or bottle while the right hand

steadies the cup or bottle. The cup or glass is always raised when a glass or cup is being filled. Japanese enjoy eating when drinking and drinking sessions always include snacks and light meals.

When a drinker has had enough he can leave sake in the cup or make side motions with his hand over the cup or glass to indicate enough. If the host is insistent, the custom is to stop drinking and ignore a full glass. The purpose of drinking together is sociability and bonding rather than becoming inebriated. Nevertheless, the downtown entertainment areas of Tokyo in the late evenings include reeling businessmen trying to reach their commuter trains before the last one leaves the station. It is important, though, to behave relaxed and as if slightly tipsy. Drinking sessions often are accompanied with bar games and karaoke singing. This behavior is mandatory to show conformity to the group.

A common and informal way of drinking is to spend several hours in an akachochin. The term akachochin ("red paper lanterns restaurant") refers to small taverns or bars (similar to British neighborhood pubs). They are generally small, family-owned, and serve non-expensive meals and beverages. These establishments provide seats at the bar and tables large enough to contain fifteen to twenty persons placed in nooks closed off by curtains. Larger tables crowd customers together to make drinking sessions friendlier since patrons sit elbow-to-elbow. The privacy afforded by these drinking nooks also allows for more relaxed and riotous behaviors. The normally staid public behavior of Japanese disappears in the privacy of these drinking nooks. These are not places to take high-ranking officials and executives but

rather one's friends and colleagues. These bars also provide light meals such as rice balls, beef intestines stew, roasted fish, and sashimi (thinly sliced raw fish). Akachochin also serve small dishes that compliment sake and beer such as edamame and pickled vegetables. A common dish is baked chicken (yakitori) with sweet or salty sauces. Another common dish is oden. Oden includes a variety of cooked foods dipped in savory soups containing thickly sliced Japanese radishes, deep fried tofu, egg, fish cake, dumplings made of fish paste, and more. Many drinking sessions involve three steps: a dinner party, pub drinking, and a late-night visit to a nightclub. The first step of this "three-step ladder" (hashigo) celebration consists of dinner at a restaurant. The second step (nijikai) involves going to a bar. These are often establishments where the participants, especially the hosts, are recognized by the staff, welcomed by name, and automatically delivered their favorite drinks. In such places, hosts commonly have their bills sent to their companies for payment.

Contemporary businessmen also spend the second stage of the evening in taverns called izakaya. Izakaya are popular and represent friendliness among businessmen and politicians. Japanese Prime Minister Koizumi hosted former U.S. President George Bush at a famous Tokyo izakaya during Bush's Pacific Rim tour during the last year of his presidency. Izakaya are considered more reputable and have higher prestige than akachochin. In the summer, roof beer gardens serving beer and snacks, located close to train stations, are also popular with late-night drinkers. Many members of the group will leave at the end of the nijikai by giving fatigue or the need to catch

their commuter trains as an excuse.

Others will conclude the last part of the three- step evening by going to a club offering karaoke singing. This third stage has been preceded with eating and drinking, and the participants should now be relaxed and in good humor. Some business discussion might take place during this stage. Being invited to this stage is seen as an honor and as a sign that the process of trust and sociability has reached an advanced stage.
