Foreigners often decline invitations to continue the evening in a nijikai or sanjikai stage because they feel it is a waste of time to do so. This is a major error and such invitations should be accepted if at all possible (De Mente, 1987: 281-82). Being jetlagged is a valid excuse but accepting such invitations is important. The senior member who has issued the invitation is the host to the three-step hashigo and it is his duty to pay the bills.

Japanese also enjoy large-scale business parties that might at times become loud. Such parties, called enkai, almost always include karaoke singing. The main guests are expected to sing solo to the accompanied music. It is best to be prepared and familiar with the words of a few songs. Do not be surprised if your Japanese party partners regain their formal attitudes the next day. Offices are not places for frivolity and Japanese are formal at work. They are also unwilling to discuss a previous night's behavior at the office. What happened during a drinking spree is not discussed at the office. This silence avoids embarrassing those who drank too much the night before or were not present.

Co-workers usually celebrate the completion of a project by holding an uchi-age drinking party. This type of party is the formal closing of something

difficult or important, such as completing a project, signing a contract, gaining an important client, etc. While some businessmen may organize an uchi- age for any reason, it is usually organized for the completion of significant projects and goals. Participants in an uchi-age are allowed excessive drinking and outrageous behavior to let off steam though no quarrels or complaints are allowed. The evening's entertainment is also seen as developing enthusiasm for the next day and the next project. More ominous is the invitation by a superior to, "Let's have a drink," or, "Chotto ippai." Colleagues may use the term as an invitation to get together for an hour or so after work. In this case chotto ippai is an informal invitation. However, a superior may invite a subordinate to a chotto ippai in order to speak privately about a serious matter. The bucho may want to criticize a subordinate, point out weaknesses in his performance, or suggest he could be a better team player. In this case, the chotto ippai invitation is a formal affair involving serious issues and cannot be refused. On the other hand, the boss may want to praise a subordinate, ask if he is interested in a special project, whether he wants to be sent abroad, or ask other questions of a delicate matter (including marriage). The informal setting is conducive to discussions more difficult to carry out in more formal surroundings.

The chotto ipai, or informal drinking invitation, often has as its purpose an invitation to communicate in an informal setting. Most drinking sessions of all types have purposes other than a desire to drink alcohol and flirt with bar hostesses. Attendance is required to develop and maintain wa, though such social settings are one of the few places salarymen can relax.

Attendees often use the occasion to show off one's ohako or personal skill or exceptional talent. Those drinking together may ask someone to display his ohako if appropriate to the setting, including knowledge of folk songs and a good voice, bar tricks, or storytelling. A person may be able to tell amusing stories (including ghost stories), recite well-known poems, and the like. It is an advantage to be known to have a talent enjoyed by others. Ohako may involve other talents such as being a good golf putter; if so, the person will be the focus of bets on his performance when on the golf course. One's talent, sometimes called kakushi-gei or "hidden art," reflects the necessity of practicing one's performance in secret until it is presentable. It is possible to hire a small room with karaoke equipment for several hours in order to practice a number of songs. Foreigners going to Japan should consider developing a "talent" before going to Japan. Karaoke singing is always a possibility.

2.18. Your Five Best "Friends" in Japan

There are five best "friends" for those visiting Japan to negotiate or meet with Japanese business colleagues. The first is the local embassy or consulate. Embassies contain commercial bureaus whose personnel provide information on local companies, trade laws, and the like. They also provide valuable introductions. Many U.S. State Department personnel develop extensive contacts, know the local legal structure, and are able to help with transportation, taxes, and other problems. Many can also help thread through national and corporate bureaucracies. Such technical information is important but most useful

is contacts and introductions made through the embassy. Embassies keep lists of physicians and pharmacies with foreign language expertise.

The second "friend" is the local representative of a company's bank and accounting firm. International banks provide valuable services for their customers including letters of credit and financial advice. It is best to make connections with the foreign branches of local banking and accounting firms before leaving for Japan. Since the national government officials, especially those in the Ministry of Finance (MOF), are involved in any financial matters dealing with foreigners, the chances are high that bank representatives can provide introductions to higher-level government and corporate officials.

The third best "friend" is the hotel. Most

foreigners in Japan for business stay at international-class hotels located near Tokyo's business centers where nearly all Japanese large corporations have their main offices or branches. Many of the personnel of international-level hotels speak English and other languages and can help with local transportation, hosting of parties, and other services. These hotels are popular and well-known meeting locations for both national and foreign businesspersons because they are also the settings for business banquets, meetings, conferences, and weddings. They also provide valuable services for business travelers. There are faxing facilities, international telephones, duplicating, limousine buses, and other resources. Many of these hotels offer translating services and general secretarial personnel. They can also print bilingual business cards. International-class hotels also maintain lists of English-speaking doctors on

call.

Ideal hotels for foreign business travelers are located in the center of Tokyo's business section and are easily reached by Japanese guests or hosts. Some hotels are conveniently situated near the Tokyo Station, a central rail line with extensive stores in its mall. Sample hotels known for their services are: Four Seasons Hotel, Imperial Hotel, The Peninsula Hotel, and the Dai-ichi Hotel.

These hotels also include arcades devoted to shopping and general consumer services. Many stores and services are found on the premises, such as clothing in foreigners' sizes and styles, barber shops, and stores selling products used by travelers in general. Tourist goods are also available for those needing souvenirs and gifts. The Imperial Hotel includes an excellent bookstore featuring foreign language books and Asian art. Each hotel also has a number of restaurants. There is always a restaurant serving classical Japanese cuisine in addition to continental and Chinese restaurants.

International businesspersons' hotels can make the difference between business failure and success if only because they maintain personal morale. They help avoid culture shock of foreign visitors by providing comfortable and familiar environments. Foreign visitors to Japan often complain about the difficulty in communicating with Japanese in English, limited Wi-Fi spots, ATMs, and stores accepting foreign credit cards. Hotel staff can provide help in obtaining debit cards for local use and provide names of medical personnel speaking English if needed. The hotel staff can call ahead when a foreign guest leaves for a meeting. It is customary to phone a host when a guest is leaving the hotel for a meeting.

All these services, and more, are located in international-class hotels. Those not wishing to shop outside their hotels can find a wide range of available items including personal toiletries, medicine, computer accessories and peripherals, fitness facilities, and even massages. All have on the premises small drug stores, Internet services, and post offices with personnel used to dealing with foreign postage, international parcel delivery services, exchange counters, and ATMs. The hotel staff can inform taxi drivers where guests want to go; the hotel's business card can be shown to a taxi driver for the return trip.

A note of caution. Inviting guests to one's hotel room is not done by salarymen. There is always the hotel's reception lobby, where drinks and snacks are available. If a lobby is too public or crowded (or there are not enough comfortable chairs), a nearby coffee shop is acceptable.

The fourth "friend" in Japan is the police stationed in neighborhood koban or police box. Koban are located in every neighborhood and many busy street corners. They provide a visible presence of the police who settle minor domestic arguments and maintain the civic peace. Outsiders find koban valuable because the police provide directions for those who do not know how to find local addresses. Westerners can also stop and ask for directions. While most local police stationed in the koban do not speak English or other foreign languages, they are willing to point out directions or telephone a colleague who speaks the language needed to give directions to local residences, local museums, stores, etc. Having an inadequate map of Tokyo, the junior author once showed a koban officer the address of one of Japan's most famous department

stores. An officer personally led my wife and I several blocks until we had reached the store in question.

Japanese frequently ask directions of police because most Japanese streets have no names. Since alleys and winding streets may look like all others to strangers visiting a neighborhood, it is often necessary to ask someone for directions. The address system in Japanese cities has little to do with the location of a building. Addresses are based on areas rather than streets. In metropolitan areas, a building's address starts with the city, then the ku or ward, then a smaller district called a cho, then a banchi or lot number. Numbers are then assigned to buildings based on where they are located on a lot, the smallest defined space. Building numbers are based on when a building was constructed rather than its location. Koban have local maps posted nearby which are used by both pedestrians and taxi drivers. Since local police are required to visit every household in the koban's area once a year and check on the household's registration, koban officers are familiar with the neighborhood and they can show visitors the way.

Japanese are very honest and most lost items are either returned to the owner if possible or taken to the nearest koban. If you lose a wallet or other article while walking in the streets of any of Japan's large cities the chances are that the object will be taken to a koban's lost-and-found section. Keeping a hotel's card in your wallet guarantees it will be returned to that hotel if found by a Japanese. The fifth "friend," perhaps the most important, is one's consultant. Consultants often provide introductions, help avoid errors, and make a business visit much less stressful. There now

exist numerous consultants who are familiar with Japanese culture and language. Some consultants are both bilingual and bicultural and familiar with business protocols in both Japan and the U.S. The World Bank ranks countries as to their relative ease in starting and operating company, and relative ease in doing business in general. Japan's rank is thirty-four (U.S.: 80) among one hundred- and-ninety national economies. Japan's regulatory environment is complex and unique in many ways, and a knowledgeable consultant is a necessary resource.

As examples of the usefulness of consultants, Article 20 of the Commercial Registration Act states that every company in Japan must register its official seal (hanko), which is often used to sign contracts. A consultant is able to inform a foreign client whether an official seal is needed, or when one is required to use it for business agreements. In addition, contracts in Japan generally include, should the signatory parties disagree, the phrase that disputes...shall be determined by mutual negotiation." How and when this clause should be enacted is a subtle issue best decided by a consultant.

There are other sources of help when considering doing business with Japanese or when in Japan. The Japan Chamber of Commerce and Industry has 1.26 million members worldwide and is active in promoting Japanese products, often those that are innovative and world-class. U.S. Americans in Japan should also consider becoming involved with the American Chamber of Commerce in Japan, which has roughly 3,500 members.

2.19. Who to Send to Japan

Everyone experienced with Japanese-style business practices and asked who should be sent to Japan answers, "Someone who is patient." Japanese will not be rushed except under very unusual circumstances. Japanese will not be pressured unless there is a crisis, rapid price changes, and the like. Japanese are hard workers but the bottom line is achieving and maintaining good relations rather than an immediate profit. Developing the correct social relations takes time because few Japanese will rush if doing so might result in damaged relationships. Alison R. Lanier (1962) notes that maintaining harmony and morale are paramount among Japanese.

A corporate representative to Japan must also be sociable and able to entertain and be entertained to a much greater degree than is the custom in the U.S. This includes being able to drink alcoholic beverages throughout the evening and join in what might seem to be silly bar games, singing, and frequent toasting. Part of the socializing has the aim of creating a feeling of thankfulness or social debt (giri and on). The ideal corporate representatives should also be willing to perform favors in order to create a sense of debt that might be repaid within a business context.

A third needed characteristic is being able to stay humble. Dealing with Japanese demands low levels of self-promotion while at the same time praising others. U.S. Americans are taught to promote themselves in order to stand out and be noticed while modesty is a major virtue among Japanese. Even description of one's products or services is done in a manner that avoids overt boasting and self-promotion. In the same manner, the ideal representatives should be able to control their

emotions, especially anger and frustration. They should not express frustration or disappointment, nor show too much eagerness.

A representative should make the effort to learn at least some of the Japanese colleagues' language. Japanese make a great effort to learn the languages of their customers and U.S. Americans should do likewise. Those sent to Japan on extended assignments can better avoid culture shock and a sense of alienation when able to communicate to some extent. Being able to do so avoids many of the everyday problems experienced by expatriates. Finally, dealing with Japanese demands great diplomatic skills.

2.20. Enjoying Japan

While everyone faces the threat of culture shock when in a different culture for a period of time, experiencing Japan outside of business can be exhilarating. It is a matter of accepting differences and finding ways to cope with the unknown. Smart phones and tablet-type devices brought to Japan can be used once international roaming has been set up by one's telephone company. KDDI sells sims cards for those wishing to make international calls while keeping established cell numbers.

Wi-Fi access has become more common in recent years. Hotels rooms provide Wi-Fi access, as do Starbucks and 7-Eleven outlets as well as other chains. Personal checks are seldom accepted because Japanese do not have the custom of using personal checks, though most stores accept travelers' checks. ATMs are located everywhere, though most still do not accept foreign credit cards. Nevertheless, all of the 17,000 7-Eleven stores in

Tokyo have ATMs accepting foreign credit cards. Some major train terminals have English-speaking staff to help travelers, as do many of the larger

stores.

2.21. Suggested Readings

Brannen, Christalyn (1991) Going to Japan on Business: A Quick Guide to Protocol, Travel, & Language. Berkeley, California: Stone Bridge Press. Goldman, Alan (1994) Doing Business with the Japanese: A Guide to Successful Communication, Management, and Diplomacy. Albany, New York: State University of New York Press.

Japan Culture Institute (1988) Discover Japan: Words, Customs and Concepts. New York: Harper &

Row.

Seward, Jack (1988) More About the Japanese (Revised Edition). Tokyo: Yohan Publications. Shimizu, Ikkö (1996) The Dark Side of Japanese Business. New York: M.E. Sharpe.

Takada, Noriko and Rita L. Lampkin (1997) The Japanese Way: Aspects of Behavior, Attitudes, and Customs of the Japanese. Lincolnwood, Illinois: Passport Books.

3

WORK IN JAPAN

3.1. Work Is Life

Japanese culture is a "live-to-work" culture in which work is the central aspect of each person's identity. The Japanese realized during the 1950s they would have to compete in international markets or else face unrelenting poverty. Since Japan lacked the natural resources (petroleum, iron, etc.) to develop and maintain a modern economy, the Japanese economy would have to be based on selling products throughout the world- especially to the US-in order to have the currency to import needed resources to make products to export, etc. The Japanese also realized that their very culture was threatened unless Japan maintained a high- level import-export cycle. As a result, the people became unified in working hard to save Japan. Senyu koraku is defined as "work first; enjoy later." It reflects the traditional Japanese toward work and its importance, including students preparing for high school and university entrance examinations.

This value is changing, as younger workers increasingly demand more leisure time. Younger Japanese husbands are more family-oriented than in previous generations, and, due to increasing labor shortages, are able to demand more free time. More salarymen even wish to take all of vacation time allowed, a practice unknown among previous generations!

Work in Japan also has a religious dimension. Japanese are not very religious in the Christian sense since they do not attend services frequently, they seldom pray, or believe that religion is an important aspect of their identity. However, religious feelings exist and few Japanese families miss visiting shrines and temples during New Year's ceremonies and other major holidays. Work in Japan is seen as an activity that adds meaning to life. This positive view of work is reinforced by the Buddhist belief that work is a spiritual discipline leading to the achievement of Nirvana. Zen Buddhism continues to strongly influence business-related values partly because managers use these values to encourage workers to work harder and become more loyal. Japanese Buddhism stresses that work activity is good and idleness is bad; i.e., to work is necessary if one wants to be a good person and be considered a moral person.

Work is so central for Japanese that descriptions of work and salarymen are found in television dramas, manga (adult comic books), newspapers, and magazines. An example of a comic book series with a work theme is Salary Man Kintaro. Kintaro was a rebellious punk before being hired by a large construction company. Short-tempered but impassioned, Kintaro now fights against bureaucratic absurdities and arrogant managers.

The series is a comment on the irrational, authoritarian, and stressful aspects of Japanese business as well as the recognition that not all managers promote wa among subordinates. Business-oriented literature is very popular with the average Japanese salaryman who commutes one to two hours to and from work. He reads business-oriented manga and magazines while "floating" (standing up) in the crowded trains.

There are also numerous novels with business themes where plots deal with the main character's problems at work. These short novels at times contain practical information about the business world although the emphasis is on the psychological problems Japanese workers experience at work (see Prindle, 1989 for examples of the business novel). The popularity of these novels and other business-related literature suggests that Japanese workers experience many problems and stresses related to work. Employment offers security and social identity in Japan but many individuals are not happy. Louis Harris conducted a survey during 1992 of almost four thousand middle-level managers in fifteen countries (reported in Fernandez, 1993). The results showed that U.S. managers were more satisfied than their Japanese counterparts.
