The survey was conducted before the 2008 "bubble burst" and before the practice of lifetime employment declined. Although Japanese workers work hard and are totally immersed in their work, they remain relatively unsatisfied.

3.2. Work Is War

Japanese businessmen and government officials view work as a battle for cultural survival. The result of work should be more than profit, which is a secondary concern. The real reward for working hard is the survival of the company and the Japanese nation. A common slogan found in many factories during the post-war period illustrated this concept of work and survival: "Export or Die." This slogan meant that Japanese workers were expected to work hard so that their nation and culture could survive and prosper. At the same time, workers were identified as heroic modern-day warriors, or samurai, fighting to preserve their nation. This belief in the warrior-worker encouraged workers to adopt the samurai war-like spirit and it also enhanced morale: workers, especially exporters, were in the front line defending their nation. Essentially, "the marketplace is a battlefield," as another slogan stated. Wages in Japan were kept low during the 1950s and 1960s so companies could re-invest more of their profits. Although

wages have risen dramatically since the postwar period, wages today are still relatively low compared to those in other advanced industrialized economies while the quality of life is lower. Increasingly, younger Japanese believe they should receive a greater share of Japan's economic success and complain, "Why are the Japanese so poor while Japan is so rich?"

Given this orientation toward work, it is no surprise that contemporary businessmen study the classic Chinese and Japanese treatises on military strategy. One of the greatest Chinese strategists, Sun Tzu, wrote during the sixth century what contemporary Asians consider the greatest work on strategic thinking His The Art of War has been required reading by salarymen and managers for decades, and it is still popular today. The work is studied and discussed in training seminars sponsored by contemporary Japanese corporations. The Art of War stresses the use of one's strong points against an enemy's weak points. The Japanese automobile strategy of first selling their cheapest models to U.S. American consumers was based on the fact that Detroit ignored the demand for smaller and cheaper cars and instead manufactured more expensive models with their higher profit margins. Once Japanese automobiles dominated the low end of the market the Japanese auto manufacturers began to build intermediate and later higher-priced automobiles. Japanese automobile makers had first focused on the U.S. American automobile industry's weakest point.

Sun Tzu also recommends emphasizing the larger and long-term picture. Min Chen (1995) recounts the famous story "Top Horse, Middle Horse, and Weak Horse" to illustrate the strategic thinking of

Sun Tzu. Sun Bin, a strategist living during China's Warring States Period (476-221 BC), was an advisor of General Tian of the Qi Kingdom. General Tian was a dedicated horse racer and wagered large sums of cash on his horses. At that time, the custom was to hold three races with the first race entering only the best horses. The second race included only average horses and the third race pitted the slowest horses.

Sun Bin advised his superior to place his worse horse in the first race with his opponent's best horse, his best horse in the second race against the average horse and his second-best horse in the third race against the worse horse. As a result, the General lost the first race but won the other two. Sun Bin understood that the goal should be to win the most races, not any one. In the same way, one can lose temporarily in business and in war by not gaining a profit for a period of time or by losing a specific market but win greater prizes in the long run. In business, Japanese companies accept short term losses when selling a new product until either the price goes down to make the product more attractive to consumers or competitors leave the

market.

Sun Tzu also stressed thorough planning as the key for success. This includes knowing one's enemy as well as knowing oneself. He also taught that direct confrontations are to be avoided at all costs and that forming alliances was a major component of victory. Sun Tzu also emphasized the importance of morale. To succeed, leaders must gain the complete support of their subordinates; this expectation demands treating them as part of the leaders' (or corporate) family. This makes possible the principle of "fighting as one man," by

which Sun Tzu meant that general and soldiers shared the same values and goals (Chen, 1995). Anyone wishing to understand Asian corporate strategy will find Sun Tzu's The Art of War invaluable. All Japanese corporate employers have studied the work carefully and the work can even be found in comic book form for ease of reading while commuting or relaxing at home. Companies sponsor seminars to familiarize workers with the work.

Another classic read by Japanese businessmen is the seventeenth century work The Book of Five Rings by Miyamoto Musashi (1584-1645). There are very few Japanese salarymen who are not familiar with the works of both Sun Tzu and Miyamoto Musashi. Musashi was a famous samurai known for his battle skills. By age thirty, Musashi had killed sixty opponents in face-to-face combat. He was also a skilled ink-brush artist. Musashi recommends total dedication and alertness. Salarymen are encouraged to adopt Musashi's strategies and translate them into the business context. Below are a few examples of strategic advice Musashi offers easily translated into business-related behavior (Musashi, 1974):

In all forms of strategy, it is necessary to maintain the combat stance in everyday life and to make your everyday stance your combat stance.

You must practice diligently in order to understand how to win.

. Any man who wants to master the essence of my strategy must research diligently, training morning and evening. Thus, he can polish his skill, become free from self, and realize

extraordinary ability. He will come to possess miraculous power.

.... people are always under the impression that the enemy is strong, and so tend to become cautious. But if you have good soldiers, and if you understand the principles of strategy... there is nothing to worry about.

....you can win by relaxing your body and spirit and then, catching on to the moment the enemy relaxes, attack strongly and quickly... The Japanese world of business contains a large vocabulary devoted to military terms. A term of respect is "konjó," which can be translated as "fighting spirit," "courage," "perseverance," and "willpower." The worker who has konjo is one who never gives up and is a model for other workers. He embodies the best qualities of the ancient samurai warriors. Another military term found in the corporate vocabulary is "nukegake." The term refers to a warrior who sneaks out of camp before a battle and attacks the enemy on his own initiative. Such a personal raid may be successful and result in personal honor and a reputation for bravery, but nukegake also violates military discipline and disrupts a superior's own plan of attack. In contemporary business nukegake refers to behavior that ignores policy or the orders of a superior. Such glory-seeking causes anger and jealousy among colleagues and embarrasses superiors. A nonconformist labeled as a nukegake will not be trusted to follow kata and will eventually be punished for his too-individualistic behavior, even if his independent action results in success.

Another military term used by Japanese workers is "hayameshi" (eat quickly). In classical times soldiers in the field were expected to eat their

lunches quickly to avoid being surprised by an enemy while resting and eating. Modern Japanese workers also eat their lunches quickly for fear of wasting valuable time. It is ordinarily impolite to eat quickly in large part because eating together is a social activity. Eating quickly would indicate a dislike of being with the other diners and indicate a rejection of friendship. However, co-workers eating lunch together at their desks would hayameshi and eat fast to indicate seriousness and a willingness to go back to work as soon as possible.

3.3. The Will to Work

Japanese, including supervisors and educators, believe that everyone has about the same aptitudes and talents as anyone else. The difference among persons is what Japanese call "kiai" and "seishin," or "will" Everyone can do whatever is expected through a strong will and determination. Even less- than-average students can learn the difficult art of writing and reading the Japanese scripts if they are willing to work hard and refuse to quit. At work, supervisors assume anyone can learn to be better- skilled through perseverance and determination. Those who do not work hard, learn diverse job skills, or even work long hours are assumed to lack will and loyalty to the group. Often the president of a corporation that failed to meet its profit/sales goals will apologize to the workers, promise to work harder himself, and vow to show more dedication and kiai.

A common failure among novice Buddhist priests is falling asleep when learning how to meditate. The meditation teacher walks among the novice priests and hits anyone falling asleep with a stick

to wake him up. Smaller replicas of these "pouring spirit sticks" are available for sale in souvenir stores located near temples. Such sticks are often found in offices to remind workers to stay alert and work hard. These sticks also remind workers to concentrate on their work. Many workers in the manufacturing sector believe that production mistakes (such as letting pass a defective part) and accidents are the result of loss of concentration; the stick reminds them, like Buddhist monks, to focus their minds on their duties. Such concentration (syu-chu) is so important that workers seldom have any personal objects in their offices or workstations that might distract them such as music, family photographs, and personal posters.

Keeping one's mind focused while determined to work hard and accomplish what is expected are highly encouraged. Corporations often send new employees to Buddhist "boot" camps where they learn to concentrate, meditate, and be enthusiastic when working. Workers with more seniority are also periodically sent to these sessions to invigorate them and renew their enthusiasm. Supervisors commonly urge workers with the term "Gambaru," translated as "Hang in there," "Keep trying," or "Persevere."

The term "persevere" is commonly used to encourage workers. Although Soichiro Honda (1906-1991) was a nonconformist in many ways, he stressed that hard work and persevering were central values. He once said:

Success can be achieved only through repeated failure and introspection. In fact, success represents 1 percent of your work which results only from the 99 percent that is called failure.

A unique characteristic of Japanese corporations is that there are relatively few job prescriptions attached to various positions. It is assumed that not only are most workers similar in talents, but they are also willing to perform any task expected of them. As a result, while teams and divisions have specific responsibilities, individual members do not. In addition, workers are expected to help one another and to learn from peers. These expectations further blur individual responsibilities. Those with seniority may carry out more attractive duties or those that demand more experience, but it is assumed that junior team members will learn to complete all duties by watching and imitating more senior workers.

The "do your best" is a common message in the Japanese workplace. Workers should want to work hard and carefully, as well as having the desire (will) to devote themselves to work and the group's goals. An example of such a message is found in the Matsushita Corporation anthem sung daily before work starts (Alston 1990:33):

world

For

the building of a new Japan,

Let's put our strength and minds together, Doing our best to promote production, Sending our goods to the people of the

Endlessly and continuously,

Like water gushing from a fountain.

3.4. The Five Ss and the Search for Quality

Toyohiro Kono and Stewart Clegg (2001) state that a large part of Japan's post-war success can be summarized by a set of slogans called the Five Ss often found on walls and bulletins boards in

Japanese factories. These five principles encourage workers to respect ever-improving quality work. The first principle, "putting in order" (seiri), stresses keeping one's work area clean and orderly. In many Japanese plants, workers are expected to spend the last minutes of a shift cleaning their machines, tools, and work areas. In the same manner, Japanese school children sweep and clean classrooms and schoolyards and help serve meals; advanced students are also expected to help those who need help understand the class material. Westerners visiting Japanese factories are always amazed how clean they are.

This stress on cooperation in schools prepare students for their work-related responsibilities when adults. "Arranging properly" (seiton) reminds workers to organize their tools and materials in the proper manner. A minor example of the seiton value is that desks in Japanese offices are clean of clutter and all materials are kept out of sight or are neatly stacked. Personal desks do not contain personal items, such as family photos and vacation

souvenirs.

last

The three principles are "cleanliness" (seiketsu), "cleaning" (seiso), and "good behavior" (shitsuke). All of these principles impose on factory floor and office workers the responsibility for cleanliness and order as well as accepting the production goals of the organization. Good behavior in Japan refers to acceptance of the group's standards and goals as well as maintaining

wa.

Quality performance is a society-wide value. Soichiro Honda, whose sayings are still popular after over twenty-five years after his death, once

said, "If you make a superior product, people will buy it."

3.5. Decision Making

Decision-making in a Japanese corporation is always a cooperative activity. Leaders have the authority to make unilateral decisions, but doing so is seldom done. Leaders propose and suggest but they seek above all for total agreement before decisions are made public. A unilateral decision on the part of a leader is called "one screech of the crane" and suggests that the group should make decisions. The most common way decisions are made in Japanese corporations is through the ringi system, which is somewhat similar to a suggestion made by a worker in a U.S. American factory and placed in a suggestion box. A ringisho, or written proposal, is submitted horizontally to one's peers within middle management levels, within one's department, and across divisional and departmental lines. The document is a proposal for action, it details the background of a problem, and describes how to solve the problem. The ringisho does not contain a series of possible scenarios but instead presents one possible plan of action. The ringisho is shown to anyone whose work might be involved in the plan of action. Each relevant person studies the plan and accepts or rejects it to one degree or another. Disagreements are expressed in subtle terms, probably during a meal or drinking session. If a disagreement is suggested, the originator of the ringisho enacts a nemawashi (see section 3.15.3. below) type of discussion to seek support. A ringisho is amended and shown again and again until either agreement is reached,

the author senses too much rejection, or too many managers "forget" to sign the proposal and the ringisho quietly disappears.

If accepted, the ringisho is then presented to the originator's superior and the document travels up the chain of command until total agreement has been reached. Each person stamps his hanko, or personal stamp, on the document to indicate assent. The document is then sent back to the originator for implementation.

The ringi system has a number of both positive and negative features. It allows middle-level managers to show initiative. Change and reform are introduced in a way that involves a large number of persons during the planning stage. The ringisho is also used as a training tool. The originator deals with a large number of colleagues outside of his division and extends his social circle. He also learns how to compromise with others. His behavior and progress are closely watched by his superior and by his mentor so that the ringisho also becomes a performance test. More important, the ringi system allows for consensus as well as keeping colleagues i aware of whatever is being considered.

Upper-level managers or immediate supervisors at times "suggest" that someone develop and take charge of a ringisho. This allows leaders to remain in the background while a proposal is being evaluated. They can also judge the reaction of a plan as it passes from hand to hand. If a ringisho suggested by a leader is eventually accepted, he has involved others in the process and followed the dictate that "leaders sit, they do not do."

Some ringisho are little more than suggestions, though they may have significant consequences. The junior author once talked to a team of factory

workers who had proposed a plan to increase their productivity. They had suggested placing three machines closer together so that coordination among the operators would be easier and faster. The workers had also suggested that the material to be machined by the three machines be placed closer to the first machine. Though it took over a month for the workers to get permission (they may have exaggerated the speed of the manager's decision to me; the machines were massive and work was stopped while the relocation was going on), the final decision accelerated the team's production by twenty percent. The foreman of the team hosted the members to a supper as a reward and their group photograph of the occasion was proudly posted on the team's bulletin board.

It is obvious that the ringi system of making decisions is slow. There is also the likelihood of snowballing or groupthink where the acceptance of a ringisho becomes more likely as the readers see how many have already indicated their acceptance. In this case, the ringisho is more a process of "collective indecision" and does not live up to its promise. It is also easy for persons to gently indicate disagreement and so water down the plan of action until the document reinforces the status quo rather than propose change. With so many persons expected to evaluate and eventually accept the document, the ringisho also becomes a practice for the avoiding responsibility because no one person can be blamed if the plan is not successful.

Many companies have attempted to streamline the process, though no one wishes to eliminate the system completely. Some companies have attempted to limit the number of signatures needed for a ringisho to become official policy. Other

companies have limited the use of the ringisho system to medium- and long-range plans. Toyota Motor Company limits the system to projects involving large amounts of money. Since all Japanese companies have suggestion boxes or their equivalence, ideas of smaller scope can always be placed in a suggestion box or mentioned to a higher- level mentor to pass ideas to other managers.

Although the collective nature of the ringisho keeps relevant managers informed on future policy, its collective nature allows higher-level managers to avoid blame for their policy-related errors. Should a ringisho results in harming customers (due to defects in products, semi-legal activities, or financial loss), legal prosecution or punishing those responsible becomes extremely difficult. Those who sign off to a ringisho that ultimately failed remain in their positions of influence. The ringisho also allows older, more conservative managers to reject whatever offends their traditional values by refusing to agree to a ringisho. The consequence is a very conservative organization resistant to new ideas and change.

There are other negative aspects to the ringi collective decision-making. When a problem exists, U.S. Americans and others from more individualistic societies investigate its causes and identity the one(s) responsible and possibly be punished (at times by dismissal). By contrast, Japanese traditionally tend to cover up inconvenient information, including placing blame on those who might be responsible for damaging behavior. Since most decisions are made by the total group, it is almost impossible to identify an individual as the one responsible. While unanimous decisions reinforce wa, they

also reinforce conservative attitudes and limit a

company's ability to adjust to rapid changes.

The consequence of this unanimity is a fatalistic shikata ga nai ("it cannot be helped" or "nothing can be done about it"), and the issue is officially ignored or forgotten. The result of this attitude is that problems are not solved if they threaten the status quo. Another reaction to problems whose solutions might threaten the group's wa is saki okuri ("to put off doing"). As a result of these two attitudes, needed changes of policy are ignored as collective decision systems become counterproductive. Decisions are delayed until a major crisis demands immediate action. At that point, high levels of wa result in total cooperation, but the response may be too late or inadequate.

Because of the tradition of lifetime employment (shu-shin koyo) and the importance of seniority (nenko system) for advancement, Japanese corporations experience low levels of labor turnover, leading to high rates of saki okuri, and corporate changes of major policy become much more difficult. On the positive side, collective decision-systems can result in many improvements advanced by lower-level employees, even by assembly-line workers, though in these cases, suggestions for improvements are relatively minor. Such ideas gradually filter up to the higher levels of the bureaucracy. By the time a proposal reaches the highest levels, the whole organization has reached approval; everyone affected has the chance to give input, which in turn maintains wa and increases intra-corporate communication. Everyone is now ready to execute the idea. Note that while junior- level (and lower-level) workers usually initiate ideas in a corporation, only senior officials make the final

decisions. In addition, no one would dare suggest a change of policy unless certain high-prestige senior officials have already indicated general approval. The junior author once met a team of Honda assembly line workers. They proudly showed their award for an idea that had been recently implemented, in this case a series of exercises performed during the mid-morning break. Their idea to make their break more efficient had reached higher levels and then had been approved by senior officials. In addition to their award being presented publicly in the plant, the team was given a dinner hosted by the team leader.

3.6. Loyalty

Japanese interpersonal work relations are based on cooperation that in turn is forged by the development of loyalty through mutual interests and identification. These relations are developed over long periods of time with close attention paid to how these relationships benefit each member of the work group. Japanese corporations hire recruits largely on the basis of character and especially on the potential for company loyalty and ability to get along with co-workers. The need for this approach is a reflection of the practice of lifetime employment in larger corporations. Specific skills might become less needed during a career of over forty years; it is better to hire generalists who are capable of developing new work techniques as conditions change.

The Japanese educational system produces graduates with a general education unless they earned engineering, legal, or medical degrees. At the same time corporations prefer applicants who have

a general education because the specific knowledge of how to work will be taught as needed. In essence, the ideal new hire is a generalist capable of being a sociable and loyal worker. New employees are hired as general members of the corporation and not to hold specific positions. In the past, specific assignments were distributed after several years of employment after all recruits with university degrees experienced a long-term apprenticeship of ten to fifteen years (Froomin, 1964). They were frequently transferred from one department to another to become knowledgeable in numerous operations as well as to develop a wide range of personal contacts. Gradually these younger workers were permanently located where corporate need and personal talents met. This rotation during the first years of employment is becoming less common.

During this time pay raises are completely based on seniority though the pay itself is low. A few decades ago most of these recruits lived in company dormitories for several or more years and their leisure time was filled with company-sponsored group activities. Many companies and government and municipal offices still maintain dormitories for new recruits. The resulting loyalty makes the Japanese formidable workers, especially in the white-collar sectors. Most salarymen work more than eight hours a day and corporate employees who deal with outsiders spend additional time entertaining customers or potential customers.

Lifetime employment (see section 3.7 below) influences corporate goals in a very distinctive manner. Since employees are essentially capital investments that cannot easily be discarded,

Japanese firms plan long-term and their primary

goal is market share rather than profit. The need to utilize the labor force encourages corporations to increase market share and production; profits are secondary. A corporation that is growing though profit margins are low is nevertheless respected. Profits occur when market shares are high enough. The extensive loyalty expected of Japanese workers has a negative aspect. Loyalty to a group is often accompanied by bitter rivalries with other groups. Even in Japanese corporations, groups compete against each other for extra resources and prestige. Decisions within a Japanese company take a long time in large part because consensus is achieved only after competing groups have agreed to a single policy after general compromises; often a decision cannot be made until all contending parties gain some advantage by the decision. This "buying" of cooperation makes it difficult to change prior agreements because so many parties have stakes in the status quo. There is always unanimity when dealing with outsiders, but internal conflicts exist as various units compete to promote their own interests. A major, unannounced, responsibility of corporate leaders in Japan is being diplomatic enough to achieve consensus among conflicting groups. Senior executives are ideally those whose contacts (often developed during their early years of employment) throughout the corporation are so extensive that they can unite those whose loyalties would otherwise be limited to their own department or division.

In addition, each Japanese corporation or work setting has company-specific work practices. In the movie Ramen Girl, a young U.S. American learns the specific ways of cleaning, serving, and eventually cooking in a family-owned udon noodles restaurant.

She had to thoroughly learn each step of each duty before allowed to progress to the next step. In a similar manner, corporation leaders expect their younger employees to learn the unique patterns of company protocols. This fixation for traditional mindsets, while encouraging corporate identity and loyalty, inhibits organizational change (de Mente, 2015). This pressure for maintaining the status quo is found in all Japanese institutions, including government agencies, schools, and corporations. Incremental change is encouraged, but not often- needed more radical change in traditional kata. A number of Japanese corporations, including Toshiba, Mitsubishi, and Sharp, among others, have experienced financial downturns because senior executives were unable to respond quickly to changing markets.

3.7. Lifetime Employment

No mechanism (and there are many) used in Japan to gain workers' loyalty is as effective as the practice of lifetime employment (shu-shin koyo). According to Oh (1976: 14), the Japanese employment system, also known as nenko, "gives employees job security and promotes strong corporate loyalty, high motivation and group effectiveness." It can be compared to a marriage or belonging to a family.

The importance of lifetime employment in the corporate value system is illustrated by a 1958 legal controversy. The Tokyo District Court heard a case involving Honda Motor striking workers. These four union members had occupied the roof of Honda's executive headquarters and had also disrupted operations. The four had been dismissed on the rationale that their behavior had been excessive as

well as illegal. The strikers sued to regain their jobs, an unusual action at that time. The court ruled that although their behavior had been illegal and Honda was within its rights to act as it did, loss of lifetime employment was too extreme a punishment and ordered the four re-instated.

The practice of lifetime employment has declined in recent years, especially for lower-level workers. Lifetime employment remains significant for primarily white-collar salarymen and executives in large corporations. The nenko policy offers labor stability in a period of rapid economic growth and allows the retention of experienced employees trained at the company's expense. Nenko also is a reward for corporate loyalty. The worker who is productive and helps maintain wa in his groups will be rewarded not only by more responsibilities but also by promotion into important positions, higher salaries, and secure employment.

The life stage of new recruits is called kogai (an apprentice or one being trained within the firm) which reinforces the notion that skilled workers who leave their corporate "families" lack gratitude for their training and acceptance. Lifetime employment discourages employees at all levels from seeking better financial offers (and faster promotions) and in fact, few good offers would be available because prospective employers have to first promote their own "family" members. Changing employers would result in lower pay and less emotional security. Since seniority, age, and pay are so intertwined, a new worker who is older than other recruits would not easily fit in a company's hierarchy and would not be offered higher pay than senior workers of the same age. A new recruit aged forty years old and most likely married would not

fit in with younger, single employees, nor would he be accepted by older workers. The fact that, among fully-employed Japanese men aged 50-54, more than one-fourth have worked for only one employer (U.S.: 4%) suggests the nenko system remains significant (Ono, 2006). The practice of lifetime employment is nevertheless declining, especially in companies in very competitive fields.

Lifetime employment encourages Japanese workers to identify closely with their employers in the same way family members define themselves through their family memberships. This identification with one's employer results in a work ethic in which everything else, even marriage and parenthood, takes second place to work. Ten- hour workdays and weekend work-related duties are common among Japanese salarymen.

Lifetime employment necessitates long-range planning since labor must be used efficiently in the long-term, and dismissing workers is difficult (superior job seekers would not be attracted to a company known for its disloyalty to its workers.). A solution is extensive in-house training to make lifetime employees increasingly efficient. The result is an increasingly well-trained labor force. The system is also forward-looking because the performance evaluation of workers includes the evaluation of potential as well as past performance. Potential is a major factor in considerations for future promotions since previous accomplishments may have been easily accomplished or external economic conditions. were highly supportive. Jeremiah J. Sullivan (1992) notes that a person's annual evaluation includes the response to the question "What are you doing to improve yourself in the long run?" In addition, team

spirit and group loyalty are more important than ability or specific task success. Japanese companies also offer a wide range of educational programs for those wishing to upgrade or develop their skills.

The lesser emphasis placed on specific task successes also results in a long-term evaluation over the years; one good year or two is discounted when a person is being considered for a promotion. On the other hand, less capable members are given duties that are less demanding Currently, such workers, unless respected for their cooperative and moral-building behavior, are likely to be transferred to affiliated companies or asked to retire.

The result is that each division in a Japanese large business contains three types of workers (de Mente, 2012: 56). There are young trainees/recruits who have been employed for five or so years. The second group of workers is made up of more experienced, capable workers who have developed large social networks with colleagues and customers and who will soon be promoted. The third group is made up of older workers who are no longer highly productive and who have not been able to adjust to changing technologies or work demands, such as the need to learn a foreign language. The members of this last group are retained because of their past loyalty and contributions. They must be given full respect, and they are used to meet visitors and clients. Their age and seniority give them prestige, and they attend meetings and negotiations to give an aura of respectability to official activities.

Another characteristic of lifetime employment is that Japanese corporations do not have well- defined job descriptions for each position. Lifetime workers are expected to perform any duty that needs to be done. Positions have responsibilities,

but each member is expected to share in each duty. Not helping colleagues would be considered selfish behavior and showing a lack of wa. Since each division includes experienced as well as non-experienced workers, new recruits learn by watching and helping. The result is that detailed job descriptions are seldom needed for office workers. It is assumed they will be motivated to perform any activity.

Honda Motor has attempted to use the best characteristics of the lifetime employment system. The founder, Soichiro Honda, established the "expert system," based on his belief that all workers should develop all of their talents. Workers are expected to upgrade their skills by becoming certified as "experts" in more than one specialization by attending lectures and practical courses (Honda did not value formal education very much, especially at the university level. He preferred learning by doing.). Workers now become increasingly more valuable to the company over their lifetime (Alston, 1986:94).

Carlos Ghosn, president and chief executive officer of Nissan Motor Co. since 1999, redesigned Nissan to make it more efficient. He finds that lifetime employment has great advantages, though Ghosn is French-educated and was not raised in the Japanese cultural complex. He states (Ghosn, 2003: 17):

...it seems to me that the best companies had a job structure based on seniority. In other words, they have an ethos of respect for older employees. At the same time, however, they have the flexibility to promote younger employees to top positions. A good company

creates an environment that encourages young employees with potential to flex their muscles and aim higher.

With regard to lifetime employment, I see this as an extremely valuable practice. If a corporation hopes to earn the loyalty of its employees, it must demonstrate loyalty toward them. By offering its workers the security of a long-term career, a company can foster true loyalty within its workforce.

Carlos Ghosn did not accept all aspects of Japanese business protocols. While he recognized the advantages of lifetime employment, Ghosn often promoted younger employees over more senior personnel. Those who did not respond enthusiastically (and creatively) to his reforms at Nissan were set aside and the responsibility for enacting his reforms given to more dynamic (often younger) managers and engineers (Nathan, 2004). More recalcitrant and conservative employees were not dismissed but rather kept on and given fewer responsibilities, as is the Japanese custom. Ghosn tried to develop a merit system of reward while at Nissan, but the resistance to this novelty was so great the new scheme was abandoned. Ghosn was known as a person who would accept traditional policies when necessary, such as pay raises based on seniority and lifetime employment.

Ghosn nevertheless broke formal ties with 1,100 of Nissan's keiretsu (sister companies) because they were, as was the custom, overcharging the company for parts. This is one advantage being a keiretsu of a large corporation ("The Impatient Mr. Ghosn," Forbes: May 5, 2006). Ghosn was nevertheless admired by the Japanese public in spite of some

of his unorthodox (ie., western) administrative policies. He was given the nickname, "Mr. Fix it" when Nissan began to show a profit. Ghosn was also called, "Seven-Eleven" for his weekly workload of sixty-five hours.

Political scientist Francis Fukuyama coined the term "vetocracy" to describe a national (or organizational) internal stalemate because too many competing groups refuse to allow for change. Some groups in an organization or government have stakes in the status quo and fear change would threaten their positions of power or values. In Japan, "vetocracies" exist in commercial, educational, and governmental institutions because respect for seniority allows older member of various groups the power/influence to resist change.

A large part of the success of Carlos Ghosn during his leadership of Nissan was his ability to ignore the resistance to needed change among senior managers and less qualified mid-level personnel. He instead gave junior managers the authority to institute organizational reforms. Ghosn had two major advantages not enjoyed by Japanese senior executives: Ghosn was an outsider who owed nothing to those in power; he was also given total authority to transform Nissan as he saw fit. To his credit, Ghosn did not try to transform Nissan into an European-type corporation. Instead, he knew when to yield to tradition and when to insist on change.

There are a number of safety valves in lifetime employment systems that are seldom recognized. James R. Lincoln and co-authors (1990) found employees of larger firms are able to change their occupations by changing positions within

their firms. This allows the prevention of work burnout for those becoming bored or disillusioned with their first set of responsibilities. This system of rotation within a firm accompanied with reeducation makes workers more efficient under changing economic demands and allows for job rotation within the corporation. This makes the Japanese workforce more flexible and adaptable than its counterparts throughout the industrial world and eliminates the need to dismiss workers during economic downturns.

Second, unwanted older workers are asked to retire. The customary age for retirement is sixty- five or older, depending on corporate policy, though some may decide to retire earlier. At that time, corporate workers are given the choice of retiring when their employers no longer need them. Those who were no longer efficient are able to save face by accepting early retirement. More and more companies are enforcing a policy of early retirement in order to cut labor costs in part because older workers are paid more than younger ones. Some workers use their retirement bonuses to open small retail stores or become suppliers for their former employers.

Third, larger corporations are able to send older workers to work for their subcontractors and suppliers. This "descent from heaven" or amakudari, as the process is called, allows smaller companies to hire experienced workers with extensive networks in larger companies. Such a person knows the standards and policies of the larger company and helps the supplier with problems that develop in relation to the parent company. The pay is less and the worker's prestige is no longer as high, but he saves face by not only

working, but also by giving the impression he is still working indirectly for his original employer.

Fourth, an estimated seventy percent of Japan's corporate workers enjoy lifetime employment (other workers may not officially have lifetime contracts but may have such in practice). Workers are allowed to change occupations, employers, or both as they wish. While inter-firm mobility is relatively rare (Japanese workers change jobs an average of only three to five times during their work careers), it is possible, though difficult, to escape from a disliked work situation. Lifetime workers tend to be employed in the larger corporations and receive higher salaries as they gain seniority; these jobs are considered to be the best and offer high prestige. Changing employers is most common in small companies and among unskilled and semiskilled workers.

Fifth, Japanese corporations are hiring more part. time and temporary workers, especially those who are less skilled. While there is reluctance to dismiss even temporary workers, companies are less loyal to temporary workers. In addition, most women until recently were not considered for lifetime employment. In the past, Japanese expected women to work a few years, marry, and then leave the work sector. It was expected that married women would devote their time to being housewives, care for their children, and devote considerable time helping their school-age children with their schoolwork. There are special educational programs that parallel children's class work. When children begin to attend junior high school, women often return to work if they wish or if they need the financial rewards, although many are considered part-time workers. Increasingly, most married women with

full-time work now are able to keep their positions while taking a paid year's leave for childcare. Such women can take a second year off without pay while their full-time status is maintained.

Younger Japanese now feel less corporate loyalty and most now accept changing employers in a more favorable light. Companies themselves are less likely to promise lifetime employment, though they try to avoid dismissing workers unless they have been hired by the hour or by the week. The ideal, however, is for corporations to live up to the traditional practice of lifetime employment, especially for key personnel.

One aspect of lifetime employment is the existence of "window workers" (madogiwa-zoku). These are inept or burned-out workers, or have been made redundant through changing technology. If such workers are considered loyal and have contributed to the company in former years, they are given a desk near a window and given little else to do but look out the window. They are given few responsibilities and remain with their employers until they retire or they voluntarily leave. These are offered large retirement bonuses as an encouragement to retire early. Most workers refuse to leave since a middle-aged man without work or an employer is given little respect in contemporary Japan. Some may accept early retire due to the embarrassment of being rejected by their superiors. However, since there are few job openings for mid-career applicants, these "window workers" have little choice but to wait until they can retire at the usual age and collect their retirement benefits. The fact is that they no longer are part of the work group and have been set aside places them in exile until they leave the company

There are a number of advantages to the system of lifetime employment. It not only encourages loyalty among workers, but also allows the company to invest in workers who are expected to stay with the company for forty years or more. Japanese companies expend more resources in training their personnel than do U.S. American companies. Since a worker in Japan becomes more expensive to the firm due to automatic promotions and/or pay raises, Japanese managers are motivated to make workers more valuable by continuous training. Younger workers are expected to enroll in evening and weekend courses in order to develop a wide variety of skills. Many white-collar workers now enroll in English language lessons either on their own or in programs sponsored by their employers.

A unique Japanese value is that skills developed through a corporate program are thought to belong to that company rather than to the individual. An employee who learns a technique and leaves his employer to use that skill as an entrepreneur or employee of another company is seen as having taken with him the equivalence of company-owned furniture or equipment. There has been a court case of a corporation suing a former employee who had changed employers shortly after he had earned a MBA degree in an U.S. American university. The employers wanted recompense for having subsidized the education of its former employee. Corporate training is another way of more tightly binding an employee to his/her employer.

The pattern of lifetime employment results in a mindset very different from that found among U.S. American workers. Because of the need for extensive post-education training and frequent job

rotation to familiarize new workers with a wide range of co-workers, Japanese tend to be generalists; they are taught to be concerned with the company as a whole since most have "floated" through a number of divisions and sections. Even older workers continue to be rotated, and most Japanese managers are assigned to different divisions every few years. By contrast U.S. Americans tend to be specialists. They are hired to fill specific positions they may hold for their entire careers in a specific organization unless they are promoted. This position stability makes occupational burnout more likely; by contrast, Japanese workers are less tied to a specialty and therefore less likely to experience job burnout. Frequent rotation allows superiors to rotate inadequate subordinates to less meaningful positions without a great loss of face. On the other hand, an inadequate manager might be asked to relocate to a distant part of the country or to a less prestigious position as an encouragement to leave the company. Many managers in their forties or early fifties fear relocation where local high schools are inferior and would not prepare their children to enter the higher-ranked universities. Such a manager with teen-age children preparing to enter a university might be forced to relocate without his family or else accept early retirement.

3.8. Networking

No skill is as important as the ability to network. Little in Japanese business is accomplished without extensive networking and consultation. As a result, it is supremely important to be able to relate to others and to be part of extensive communication

channels. All business communication is best conducted face-to-face and includes sharing meals and drinking events. Using these occasions to extend one's networks is considered as insurance since additional contacts might be useful in future dealings. The person who has the most extensive set of personal contacts will be the most successful in business. Networking develops the sempai-cōhai (senior-junior) relationship in which the senior partner is expected to help the junior partner. For example, a foreign businessperson could ask an older Japanese acquaintance for advice on a business matter. If the other person accepts being a sempai then he might be able to informally talk to Japanese counterparts to find out why negotiations are blocked or why a decision is taking so long to be finalized. By asking someone for a favor, a person is indicating his cohai part of the relationship and is offering him loyalty as well as assistance. In addition, extensive networks offer more visibility. Those persons with large networks are less likely to be evaluated as unknown quantities or as strangers. Since Japanese dislike working with strangers, being a friend of a friend shortens the period it takes to become trusted and accepted as a potential colleague.

3.9. Open Offices

A distinctive characteristic of Japanese offices is an open spacing design with no partitions between desks. There are few private offices in Japanese corporations, except that sometimes the company CEO is given a private office though most CEOS share their offices with personal assistants and even with vice presidents. At Honda Motor

Company, all thirty-seven directors share one large open area (Kriska, 2011: 50). Some department/ section heads may have private offices, but the practice is for persons to share offices at all organizational levels.

The typical arrangement is a series of desks arranged side-by-side with one row facing the other. The division head or general manager (bucho) has his desk at one end of the row farthest from the door. Since rank and age are so important in Japan, the youngest members of the team sit farthest from the group's leader and nearer the door. The leader can easily observe and talk to every member of his team; co-workers can ask one another questions. By contrast to U.S. American practice, workers can hear one another's telephone conversations. Besides the issue of limited space, the assumption among Japanese is that this type of seating arrangement re-enforces group solidarity by facilitating communication and the sharing of information. Files are held in common cabinets along the wall.

Open offices in Japan do not indicate everyone is considered equal. The function of open offices is to integrate individuals into the group. The team or division supervisor, although his desk is near his subordinates, nevertheless has a better chair and desk than the others. Ordinary workers receive plain, functional office chairs. These chairs are often uncomfortable and used to work and not to relax. A perk of promotion is to be given higher- prestige chairs. When a salaryman is promoted he is given a hijikake-isu, or a more ornate armchair with padded armrests. He is also likely to be given a bigger desk to indicate his rank, though his work- duties may not change very much. His office chair

becomes more and more ornate and comfortable as he is promoted until he receives a leather- upholstered chair and large desk. One can walk into an office and notice by the type of office chair who has been recently promoted or rewarded. A more comfortable chair indicates who enjoys more prestige.

Many team division leaders have been promoted to their position because of seniority. They may have done respected work in the past but they have been rotated as well as promoted and they may not thoroughly cognizant of their new duties and responsibilities. However, group leaders rely on one or more chiefs of staff or deputies. These futokoro- gatana may actually manage all work-related behavior and are the functional leaders. Often their desks are located near the bucho or kacho (see Appendix B). They should not be ignored and they should be kept informed if possible of all activities. They will soon be promoted as they gain the prerequisite seniority. Their major responsibility is to support the formal leader in technical manners while superiors are concerned primarily with maintaining wa, as well as maintaining wide webs

of contacts

3.10. Written Materials

Japanese respect the written word though that is seldom enough to convince a potential business colleague to do business with someone. According to a JETRO publication, samples of products are necessary to show potential buyers their products' value and quality. Samples and models allow for closer inspection to evaluate whether they meet strict Japanese standards. However, written

descriptions of one's products are also necessary. There are mandatory standards that are legally fixed and no product can be sold in Japan unless the relevant standards have been met. These are concerned primarily with consumer protection and safety. There are five general mandatory standards laws, and appropriate products must meet these standards to be certified. All products, whether domestic or foreign, must meet these mandatory standards and be certified as having done so.

The Japanese Industrial Standard (JIS) also establishes voluntary standards for over 8,000 industrial products. These standards include basic criteria for terminology, symbols, and units. There are also "voluntary" standards for agricultural, pharmaceuticals, and forest products. While voluntary, many Japanese consumers will not buy products that have not met the JIS standards. Many products now carry the JIS mark to show they have met the appropriate JIS standards. There are similar voluntary standards for agricultural products (JAS). Certification is written in Japanese since the primary consumers are Japanese.

Japanese wholesale buyers need to see models of products because they are necessary for evaluating their potential for consumer approval. As important, however, is the need to provide potential buyers with catalogs and brochures. Little business is conducted in Japan without catalogs. They should be written in Japanese. English language catalogs are translated for senior officials who do not read English easily and the translation of technical data takes time.

In addition, the wise foreign businessperson in Japan brings with him company brochures describing his own company in detail. No

commitment even to meet together is made unless such brochures have been made available. The brochures contain photographs of the major officers with enclosed biographies. There should be photographs of plants and offices plus descriptions and photographs of the community or communities where the company is located. Brochures should also contain information that highlights the company's good points. It is bad form to personally brag in Japan but a chart showing market share or total sales in comparison with competitors is acceptable. There should of course be photographs of products accompanied with

technical information.

Japanese research a company in great detail when considering meeting members of a foreign company. This allows the appropriate officials to decide if a face-to-face meeting is in fact worthwhile. They will investigate all aspects of the foreign company even to the extent of asking a Japanese consulate or embassy for information not available from the usual financial sources. Making such information available in brochures is the first step in the evaluation process. The brochure should also include a list of customers and a summary of the company's business experience with Japanese companies. Brochures should contain so much information that Japanese researchers do not feel a company is withholding information.

Japanese frequently write personal emails and letters to one another, and all business communications include personal notes. Letters begin with a seasonal greeting by mentioning the season or weather. The second sentence asks about the recipient's health and general well-being. There is also a mention of personal interests such as

a person's interests in golf or tennis. One also mentions the last outing made together. The next paragraphs deal with the reason for the letter.

3.11. Success Is Incremental

Because of their fear of failure and their cultural bias due to their high levels of uncertainty avoidance, Japanese are content with series of small victories. When change takes place in Japan, it generally takes place in small steps after careful study and consensus building. This of course takes time but fits the Japanese risk-averse preference. In addition, major change demands large amounts of preparation and consensus building, which of course takes time. Carlos Ghosn (see Section 3.7 above) managed to hurry the decision-making process of Nissan's executives to decide on building a plant in one year instead of the usual three years. Ghosn's success at Nissan was highly unusual and probably could not be duplicated under normal, non-crisis conditions. Large-scale change has occurred in Japan during times of extreme crises. Ghosn became CEO when Nissan was almost bankrupt. The resulting near-panic allowed Ghosn to introduce radical changes impossible in normal times.

This emphasis on small, gradual changes in all matters allowed Japanese corporations to produce innovative and quality goods when they were known as copiers of cheap products. Once Japanese became familiar with western products, they were able to step-by-step improve on the originals. The goal is to become so thoroughly familiar with a product that workers can not only copy but begin to improve on its features (de Mente, 2003: 70-1).

Through wa and loyalty, Japanese workers are expected to know their duties so well that they are able to improve their work and products, no matter how small the improvements are.

Foreigners expecting to do business with Japanese should first offer proposals limited in scope. The proposals should also be presented as continuation of the past rather than abrupt breaks from tradition. In addition, Japanese dislike changing policy in an abrupt fashion because doing so suggests rejection of past decisions by the group and therefore a rejection of the members/leaders involved. It is always easier to add to a policy than to ask Japanese managers to consider adopting new policies. The pessimism of the Japanese also deters against rejecting tradition and established policy. They prefer to continue current policies when these policies promise to continue gradual increases in revenue and success. Few Japanese are willing to reject past group decisions on a gamble for greater profits. They are not gamblers unless forced to be so by drastically changing circumstances or outside threats.

There are three general goals in the Japanese workplaces. The first and most important is the development of high level of wa. Japanese believe higher levels of wa will always lead to success. Those losing in a competition (business or otherwise) are assumed to have lacked wa and a willingness to do their best for their groups. High levels of group harmony encourage workers to maximum efforts and devotion to group goals (de Mente, 2003: 103).

The second aim of a work group is to meet goals set by superiors after worker's consultation and input. Corporations cannot survive long without

economic success, but achieving long-range profits cannot be achieved until wa exists among all workers. If wa does not increase profits or corporate goals, then management has the responsibility to increase levels of wa because it is assumed that failure was mostly the result of too-low levels of wa and will.

The third type of success is overachievement due to maximum effort. This is one reason why salarymen do not leave work early; they do not want to be seen as not working enough. While U.S. Americans may take work to their homes, salarymen need to be seen by others as working at the highest level possible. Minor errors or failures are more easily forgiven when the one responsible is identified as having done his best and has apologized for his mistakes. Not all failures are due to personal error; some failures are due to external factors over which a person may have no control. In addition, failure can be seen as learning experiences. Generally speaking, much of training in Japan is learning by observing and by doing. In these cases, mistakes are part of the learning process. These mistakes, however, will be corrected and the person shown the proper way to work. One of the duties of Honda Motor intern Laura Kriska (Kriska, 2011: 117-18) was to mail her superior's (and mentor) correspondence. Her responsibility was to place a stamp on each letter and hand it over to one of the office ladies (OL) from the mail room. As a minor act of rebellion, Kriska placed a stamp on its envelope at a slight angle. A short time after the deviant envelope had been picked up, a supervisor from the mailroom personally handed the letter back to Kriska and indicated the stamp was not correctly placed.

Nothing was said, but the lack of kata behavior was clearly expressed. Obviously, Kriska had not followed correct procedures and had exhibited a lack of wa. Laura's only choice was to remove (under inspection) the offending stamp and replace with a correctly-placed stamp. The lesson is that good workers are those alert for errors in protocols and who seek to do their best at all times. Motivated workers always seek to better their actions for the good of the company. In Laura's case, there were no lectures of openly-spoken criticism, just an indication that she had not met company's standards. She had learned that each detail was as important as any other.

In spite of the tradition of resistance of change, Japan has produced a number of nonconformist hero-entrepreneurs. One contemporary hero is Konosuke Matsushita, founder of Matsushita Electric Company in 1917. The company eventually became the world's largest manufacturer of electrical goods with well-known products under the names Panasonic, Quasar, and others. Matsushita left a secure employment after several promotions to start his own company. With no formal education, he invented a battery- powered bicycle lamp. During the 1920s, bicycle lamps used candles or oil for illumination. These were dangerous and lasted roughly thirty minutes before replacement was needed. He also developed an improved and more efficient light socket.

Matsushita was not successful at first and pawned his wife's kimono when he needed capital. After retirement, Matshushita published over forty books on his ethical and work-related philosophies. One of his managerial innovations was to focus on sales and the development of relations among

sales persons and customers rather than solely promoting manufactured products.

Another entrepreneurial hero of contemporary Japan is Kazuo Inamori, born in 1932. Inamori established both what is now known as Kyocera Corp. and the phone carrier KDDI. He was also appointed CEO of Japan Airlines Co. and saved it from total economic collapse. A Zen Buddhist priest and trained as an engineer, Inamori is well respected in Japan and elsewhere as a philosopher- manager. His books on management have been best-sellers for decades.

Inamori and seven colleagues left their employer to start a new company (Kyocera) on more dynamic principles. Friends and acquaintances provided capital for the new company (one friend mortgaged his home to provide capital). During the second year of Kyocera's existence, ten first-year employees hired straight from high school sent a petition (written with their blood) asking for guaranteed pay raises and bonuses for all. After days of negotiation (partly at his home), Inamori convinced them they could trust him and the company to do their best for them, and that they would be properly rewarded over time, or they could leave the company if they could not personally trust him. He concluded, "If it turns out that I am running this company purely for my own interest, then you can put a knife through me and take my life." The ten workers stayed with the company (Inamori, 2013).

Inamori believes that too many Japanese managers are selfish with short-term profit orientations. He also feels the stockholders are often selfish capitalists and demand higher profits and/or dividends when they should be patient and more conservative (http://icas berkeley.edu/

cjs/). Inamori's management philosophy is based on two general principles: the centrality of positive attitudes and the development of what Inamori calls amoeba management.

Corporate success is based on workers' positive attitudes ("Never complain; act cheerfully"). Negative and "selfish" mindsets lower productivity and creativity. On the other hand, happy workers will accept high-level goals and become better (including more reactive and proactive) workers. of the workers' triad characteristics of positive attitude effort ability, the first is the most important (Official Website of Kazuo Inamori). The major responsibility of managers, according to Inamori, is to nurture workers to develop their talents and their sense of being supported by their superiors and company. Inamori explains his managerial philosophy as follows (Takato, 2015):

If you want eggs, take care of them. If you bully or kill the hen, it's not going to work.

One strategy of Inamori during his early years managing Japan Airlines Company was to surprise late-working workers with an impromptu visit carrying a package of beer (bottles are larger in Japan). He would offer beer to the members of the work unit or team and, after a few drinks together, ask them to tell him what they really thought about their work and company (Oi, 2012). This procedure is called "nominication" (communication with drinks). The assumption (and practice) is that a few drinks loosen inhibitions and "belly" (honest) talk will take place.

One unorthodox aspect of Inamori's managerial philosophy was the insistence that inadequate (unhappy, non-creative, negative) workers should

resign and leave the company. At the start of his tenure at Japan Airlines, Inamori "retired" one-third of its labor force to increase efficiency. While more common today, these dismissals were unusual at the time. A second aspect of Inamori's unorthodoxy is his willingness to promote on merit rather than only on seniority. Finally, Inamori declares that a manager must be willing to acknowledge his faults if workers believe a policy of his is wrong. Good managers are flexible and listen to their subordinates, or "making a decision in the morning and changing it in the evening."

Inamori's central part of his managerial philosophy, amoeba management ("small kingdoms"), refers to giving workers the power to plan and execute policy by being encouraged to become more involved in their work and become more creative, essentially known in the U.S. as "bottom-up" management. Small groups of workers track their production (often daily and hourly) and suggest how to improve their own activities ("management by all"). Workers should be capable of becoming better workers by accepting the challenge of achieving higher goals. I In Inamori's terms, "passive" workers must become "active" (self-motivated) workers. Essentially, to be successful, a corporation should become an extended family with each "member" seeking the common goals.

Inamori insists managers should push their workers. To allow workers to do less than their maximum does not develop their talents, nor will they be encouraged to meet the challenges of personal development (i.e., become better workers). Inamori says, "A small good (being easy on workers) is like a great evil" because pushing workers makes

them better persons as well as better workers. They should constantly be challenged to develop their talents for their own good and the company's. Although an unorthodox entrepreneur, Inamori's philosophy fits very well into the traditional Japanese mindset.

3.12. The Somu Bu and Kokusai Bu Divisions

The first written contact with members of a Japanese corporation most foreigners have is with the Somu Bu (General Affairs Department) or the Kokusai Bu (International Affairs Department) sections. The Somu Bu coordinates with other departments to facilitate interdepartmental activities such as mail, faxes, and the maintenance of central files. Some of the personnel are familiar with English and other languages and capable of translating letters not written in Japanese. This bureau has responsibility for first communications until a specific division takes charge. The major function of the Somu Bu is coordination, but similar divisions in smaller companies also take charge of all foreign correspondence.

The Kokusai Bu department deals with all foreign issues. Kokusai Bu personnel translate letters and deal with the first sets of correspondence. They also make arrangements for meeting foreigners, setting up schedules for foreign visitors, and the like. Some have the necessary expertise in foreign legal systems. Personnel in these two divisions do not have the authority to make decisions but instead facilitate dealing with foreigners and foreign issues. Their activities may delay any response since they must find a destination to an outsider's first

communications while they translate letters for officials not familiar with the language in question. The office also conducts research on foreign companies and their personnel.

3.13. Giving a Speech

There are many opportunities to give speeches when dealing with Japanese if only to toast them and their companies during drinking sessions. It is best to be short and polite when giving a speech; this is the time to offer tatemae messages. After thanking the introducer or host, U.S. Americans typically start their speeches with a joke or humorous comment to establish a façade of friendliness and equality. The tactic of using a joke to break the ice is a major mistake when dealing with Japanese. Since Japanese are formal with foreigners and with people they do not know well it is tempting for a U.S. American to act informally and make one or more jokes in order to reach a more informal stage as quickly as possible.

By contrast, Japanese do not want to establish an informal atmosphere since this behavior is limited to more private moments and to less-formal occasions. Instead, Japanese begin their speeches with apologies to show the speakers are humble and respect the high-ranked guests. An apology also places the speaker on the mercy of his audience. Apologies mean nothing factual and are not to be taken seriously since they are expressions of courtesy rather than literal statements. The junior author was present for a speech by a vice president of Honda Motor during a business conference in Japan. After being introduced by three persons in turn to show respect to the speaker, the Honda

Motor official first apologized for not being able to speak English properly. However, the person had studied in England almost fifty years before and his BBC-type accent and diction were better than most of the academics' present, including the author's. Speeches given at the start of business negotiations are short and there is little mention of the matters to be discussed. Instead, the speaker compliments the audience or the other negotiating team and stresses themes of friendship, fairness, and the desire to achieve a long-term relationship. There is also delivery of messages of friendships from the higher-level officials of the company who are not present. At all cost, however, humor is completely avoided.

Toasts during social occasions are simple and refer to the friendship between the two companies and nations. Except for welcoming speeches, toasts are short to allow everyone to drink and to take a turn in offering toasts. It is bad taste among Japanese to drink alone and a toast offers the opportunity to drink along with others. The proper way to drink is to catch someone's eye or get the attention of the whole group, lift the glass slightly, and give a short toast or offer a "kampai." Those present can now drink. Toasts can be silly ("to the full moon") as the evening progresses, but toasts remain short

3.14. Holidays

There are fifteen official national holidays. When a holiday falls on Sunday, the following Monday is an official day off and business offices are closed on that day. Most companies and government offices close during the New Year's season (December 28-

January 5), "Golden Week" (April 29-May 5), and during the O-Bon festival (August 12-15). Many officials also take off a day before or after an official holiday and are seldom available to meet with foreigners.

JAPANESE OFFICIAL HOLIDAYS New Year's Day

3.15. Meetings Japanese Style 3.15.1. Meetings, Meetings, and More Meetings

Meetings involves many kata. They are generally not held to make decisions or accomplish what

foreigners consider to be anything productive. Real decisions are made outside of meetings during informal sessions using the nemawashi (see section 3.15.3. below) method of reaching a pre- meeting consensus. Meetings serve primarily to express shared feelings after decisions have been made through personal interactions. A JETRO (1999) publication for distribution in the US. emphasizes the social nature of meetings in Japan. Consequently, dealing with Japanese businessmen demands many face-to-face meetings including those taking place in informal settings. There is no substitute for personal contact and contact is

maintained through frequent gatherings. Japanese being work-oriented, office meetings at time can last well into the evening, followed by socializing at a restaurant or izakaya. Be prepared for long meetings, especially at the beginning

The time spent travelling to Japan for a visit is outweighed by the favorable impression made by personal visits. Speech is faster than written messages while personal contacts allow for greater communication through intonation of the voice and body language. It is also easier to be vague or to say nothing when writing. Meetings are the most efficient means of communication among Japanese and meetings are not to be neglected in favor of faster but more impersonal forms of communication.

For formal meetings, it is important for a number of participants to take notes. Japanese attendees take numerous notes and then compare them to produce the most correct record for any meeting. These notes become official documents and will be produced should someone says something different in future discussions. Notes become part of a long

memory that maintain continuity. Note-taking also indicates interest in what is being discussed. Top- level participants need not take notes, but all or most subordinates should take notes. They should also later compare notes for accuracy, since the Japanese counterparts who were present will certainly discuss each other's notes in order to develop the most correct account possible.

Since no decision will be made by one individual, it is important to bring large number of copies of any material handed out. Other persons than those present will also need to see all materials handed out. These materials should be of good quality to present one's company in the best light. Encourage those present at a meeting to take as many copies of all materials as desired. The extra copies will be passed out to others.

3.15.2. Meetings Etiquette

Formal body positions are maintained at all times. One does not slouch or appear to be relaxed. Japanese at times may close their eyes during a meeting, especially if they do not have a central part in the discussions. Closed eyes do not indicate disinterest or rejection of what is being discussed. This posture is a unique form of showing concentration and respect to the speaker. In addition, Japanese do not maintain constant eye contact with speakers or other meeting attendees. Lowered eyes in the past were considered indications of respect and humility and were obligatory in the presence of higher- ranked individuals. It is still common to maintain lowered eyes to show respect to superiors. Japanese generally do not point to another person. It is bad

form to point at someone during a meeting since doing so make the person stand out from the group. Proper behavior does not allow for waving of hands or extreme facial expression as emphasis.

Attendees are expected to talk quietly and not raise their voices to make a point. Talking loudly is not interpreted by Japanese as enthusiasm ("The surface of the lake must be calm"). An image used by salarymen is that of a swan swimming on a lake in a graceful manner without making waves and seeming to move effortlessly, although its legs are churning at great speed and effort under the water, and therefore out of sight.

It is important not to dominate a meeting. Ideally, leaders speak less than subordinates ("The mouth is a source of trouble"). Speak less and present more factual materials when presenting a position or argument. Samples, models, and literature are more effective than what is said. When asked a question or to make a comment, pause slightly as if thinking before speaking.

Seating is rigorously structured. A guest should not be the one to sit down first; she should wait until the correct seating is indicated. Japanese executives' time is strictly scheduled, and there is the likelihood they will attend another meeting after the current one is concluded. The result is that meetings are seldom extended. There are exceptions, so the foreign guest should be prepared to stay if invited to do so. At times, an extension has been already tentatively planned, including a dinner invitation. These will be rare events, but they do occur.

The place of honor during a meeting is the chair or seat farthest from the door. In order to be seen as humble, the guest of honor waits to be asked to sit

at the appropriate seat. At times, foreign visitors are escorted to an empty conference room by an official greeter, a receptionist, or a lower-level executive. The hosts will enter a few moments later. After greetings, the guest is led to the proper seat (see Alston, 1990:43).

Travelling in Tokyo is uncertain due to heavy traffic during peak times. A common courtesy when going to a meeting is to call ahead and to inform the host you're one the way to the appointment location. At times, the host may offer to provide transportation for guests. If so, one or more assistants will often be present to show courtesy to the guest(s).

3.15.3. Pre-Meetings

Japanese do not disagree in public and meetings seldom include open disagreement. It is best to carefully prepare those who will attend a meeting so that they know what material will be presented during the next meeting. The person proposing a new idea should discuss his ideas informally or in writing before a proposal is officially presented during a meeting. It is necessary to achieve consensus before attending a meeting on the material to be discussed. Neglecting these pre- meeting preparation results in the impression of not being prepared, and the Japanese would not know how to react. They themselves would have to hold informal meetings to discuss any new idea unexpectedly presented during a meeting. Meetings are more productive when those attending have already seen the background information available and have had the opportunity to discuss the proposal in informal settings.

The discussions taking place before a meeting is formally scheduled are called the process of nemawashi or "groundwork."
