This is a gardening term describing the careful digging around the roots of a tree or bush to be transplanted to another location. All of the lesser roots are clipped off two years before transplantation. The process allows new root hairs to grow so the tree is less likely to experience shock after being dug up and replanted. The process is also used to encourage fruit trees to bear more fruit. In a business context, nemawashi consists of developing consensus on an issue during more informal pre-meetings and social gatherings. This necessarily takes time since there is an avoidance of confrontations. Instead the "roots" of an issue are carefully prepared by presenting general suggestions. Those who reject a proposal during the nemawashi stage can ask for more information or even express negative feelings. Since the conversations are informal, no one feels insulted if an idea or proposal is not accepted enthusiastically at this stage. Nemawashi also keeps everyone informed of ongoing issues so they become prepared before a formal meeting takes place. A meeting is called to formally present, vote on, and approve the proposal after nemawashi has taken place.

Shinomiya and Szepkouski (2002) stress that meetings are not the venues for arguing. forcefully presenting or defending a position as typically experienced in U.S. American meetings. Confrontation takes place in informal settings. However, consistent negative reactions, even if presented in a gentle manner, should be understood as outright rejection; in such cases, it is futile to continue to support a proposal. The foreigner can

try to discover without being confrontational the reasons why his proposal was ignored (ie., rejected) by asking Japanese counterparts out to dine and drink and gently ask about the failed proposal. They may explain why a proposal was rejected in an indirect manner so as not to hurt anyone's feelings.

3.15.4. Formal Meetings

The first stage is a formal visit with the purpose of getting acquainted and to establish trust. Japanese do not like to deal with strangers and the first few meetings are gatherings to develop friendships. These are similar to aisatsu meetings when senior officials socialize with subordinates before discussing business. Often, the socializing period is conducted in a room with comfortable furniture such as sofas and stuffed chairs. Tea and snacks are served while the conversation is informal and relaxed. After a period of socializing those attending the aisatsu session go to a more formal conference room to discuss business.

Formal meetings take place in formal settings with strict attention to rank and privilege. The rule of thumb is that the highest-ranking person sits with his back to the wall facing the room's door because, among Japanese, sitting as far as possible from a door denotes high rank. Behind the seat of honor will be an alcove called a tokonoma. In the past, the tokonoma was reserved for religious objects and was therefore a revered and sacred area. Today's tokonoma contains hanging scrolls with ink brush scenes and flower arrangements. This alcove is the aesthetic focus of the meeting room.

The most honored guest is seated in front of this alcove. Although he does not see the art, he is seen

by others as surrounded by these artifacts. This is the most honored seat of the room. It is customary to comment on the scene when the oldest or highest-ranked guest is asked to sit in front of the alcove. The flower arrangement is often kept in an antique flower vase and the hanging scroll may also be a valuable antique. It is also best to comment, "I don't deserve the honor to sit in front of the tokonoma" to show proper humility even though it is the main guest's right to do so as the highest- ranked guest.

3.15.5. Preliminary Meetings

There are two types of preliminary meetings when Japanese discuss whether or not to consider a proposal. The proposals presented during these meetings are either rejected or discussed for further consideration. While much negotiation goes on after these two meetings, a proposal is much more likely to be rejected at this time than later. The first type of meeting is the "study meeting" (benkyo kai) with the purpose of evaluating the merit of a proposal. Personnel meet to discuss whether a proposal has any merit irrespective of whether it originated within the company or from external sources. The proposer (often a member of the evaluating team) presents a detailed description of the proposal along with its background. The floor is then opened for further discussion. At this stage, the proposal is tentative and attendees are free to discuss all aspects of the proposal. Since there is no official sponsorship of the proposal, subordinates are in theory expected to criticize or to at least suggest skepticism if they do not favor the proposal. The custom is to hold multiple benkyo kai so

that everyone can listen to the presentations, hear others' questions (and possible hints of disapproval), and ask all the questions they wish. If the proposal is found to be attractive, the moderator (usually a management-level employee) presents a summary introduction, answers all questions, and is essentially the advocate for the proposal. He is supposed to be the one most familiar with the proposal and his support is crucial. If this moderator is enthusiastic he lobbies informally for the proposal and seeks acceptance at a larger, more formal meeting. If this is the case, the benkyo kai becomes a formal group with an appropriate budget. If the spokesman is unenthusiastic and does not wish to lobby for its acceptance, then a proposal is often rejected during the first meeting.

Foreigners wishing to give their proposals the highest likelihood of acceptance must convince an employee of the value of their proposals, attend the benkyo kai sessions themselves, or designate a consultant to make the presentations in person. Non-Japanese who decide to represent themselves during these sessions should be fully prepared and expect more and far-reaching questions than they habitually answer.

The second type of preliminary meeting used in Japan is the very informal "leapfrog" meeting. The "leapfrog" meeting is similar to the first type of preliminary meeting though the former is more formal. "Leapfrog" meetings among Japanese are totally exploratory and do not involve any specific goal or topic, nor will any decision be made. "Leapfrogs" are in essence exploratory meetings similar to brainstorming and buzz sessions in the U.S. The leader encourages ideas from the meeting's participants (Foy, 1980) and does not have an

agenda or specific goal. Often members from different divisions and different organizational levels gather together to discuss future needs of the company and the leader encourages the participants to make as many suggestions as possible. The purpose of leapfrog meetings is to encourage input from lower-level and middle-level workers who may not have other avenues to submit their suggestions or inputs for fear of disturbing the group's wa. Many Japanese corporations place great importance on customer feedback to develop better service ad products. Typical leapfrog meetings found in Japan often start the discussion with the analysis of customer feedback.

3.15.6. "Large" Meetings

Another common type of meeting in Japanese corporations is the "large" meeting with thirty or more persons in attendance. This type of symbolic meeting is called either teireikai (regular meeting) or houkokukai (briefing session), and more often occurs in larger organizations. These meetings are held to reinforce feelings of cohesion and loyalty rather than to make decisions. Highly ritualistic, such large meetings may be scheduled to gain input of a proposed plan though no formal decisions will ever be concluded at this time. Generally, those present receive information (which they probably knew already) of important corporate issues and

concerns.

"Large" meetings are also held to indicate rank and prestige because attending these meetings is an indication a person has a high enough prestige level to be invited to these meetings. While no major decisions are made during these meetings,

the fact that someone is invited to attend results in the participants becoming part of the decision- making process in other meetings. Invitation to attend such meetings is also used as an indication an employee is respected and is ready for additional responsibilities.

Large meetings are commonly held before a business proposal is formally discussed. These meetings exist to establish trust among potential business colleagues. The visitors present are being evaluated in terms of whether they can be trusted enough to become colleagues. Non-Japanese often become impatient with these meetings because they forget the symbolic significance of being an accepted member of various groups. The first part of such meetings is taken up with social chatter, often discussing what the participants have in common. It is expected at this stage to ask about a visitor's superiors and common acquaintances. If they are not mentioned, there is the possibility the issue is on the brink of being rejected. The chances are that these omissions suggest a disinclination to do business with that person and his company.

It is appropriate at this stage to present gifts from yourself or from your absent superiors. If your Japanese counterpart has visited America several times (it is likely he has), then appropriate presents are books from the areas visited. If a senior manager likes American baseball from his stays in the U.S., the donor can present him with a baseball shirt or an autographed baseball. Always apologize for the inadequacy of the gifts presented to show proper (and expected) humility. The hosts will likely invite their opposites to a meal in the near future to show their friendliness and appreciation for the gifts. It is also important to praise the other's

company to show respect and appreciation for the company's reputation. If the hosts do not respond in turn with equal praise of the visitor's company, they are indicating a lack of respect and probably negotiations are stalled.

The get-acquainted part of the meeting ends at this point. It can now turn into a working session or it may be adjourned to meet again soon. If the meeting is a first meeting, the chances are there will be an adjournment before real work is accomplished. The stage is now set for conducting actual work during the next meetings. If the meeting is not adjourned and continues as a working session, the senior officials will leave at this point. Their attendance was purely symbolic to show friendliness and to judge the character of the guests. Their departure indicates work can now begin and the meeting has now shifted to another function. If the formal meeting is the first meeting the chances are that another meeting will be scheduled. This second meeting is called the kaigi meeting. Kaigi meetings are more informed work- sessions. This is the time when presentations and negotiations seriously begin.

3.15.7. Long Meetings

The general expectation is that there will be many long meetings before a contract becomes finalized. Although decision-making is centralized, no leader adopts a policy without extensive consultation. Meetings take place to discuss proposed changes in policy during which participants are extremely polite and make vague comments. No one would dare be the first to make a direct comment for fear the idea would be rejected or is one that

is not acceptable to the highest officials present. These meetings are structured so participants gradually discover the preferences of top managers and colleagues. Gradually a consensus is reached reflecting the positions of all concerned.

There are two alternatives if the leader senses resistance to his proposal. He can subtly change his position to one more agreeable to those present. It is more likely, however, that the leader will adjourn the meeting on an inconclusive note. He will then begin the practice of nemawashi (see section 3.15.3. above). This practice entails personal discussions with peers and subordinates during social events such as private dinner parties, golf games, or friendly business lunches. At these times, the leader discusses his plan and tries to convince others to agree. A new meeting (often a "large" meeting) is called only when a private consensus has been reached. The leader asks formally for others to support his plan; the subordinates then vote unanimously in favor of the proposal and corporate harmony is maintained. Joseph Froomkin (1964) notes that decision-making in Japanese corporations is now more democratic than in the past. Nevertheless, contemporary managers at times present their proposals for agreement rather than letting subordinates initiate proposals. 3.15.8. Proper Meetings Behavior Those who attend meetings of any type remain formal and maintain a positive, agreeable demeanor. From the Japanese perspective, there is never an acceptable excuse allowing a participant to show anger or lose his temper. Meetings are also not the places to argue vehemently. Disagreements

are indicated in an indirect way and no one directly argues against another person. Overt criticism can cause someone to lose face and disturb the wa of the group.

There is also a colleague previously selected as the one to speak. His responsibility is to present the best case for an idea. Others do not interrupt during this presentation to add information or to voice comments. Other participants are not expected to offer any input, as is the custom in the U.S., because meetings in Japan are seldom places for open exchanges of opinions and viewpoints. Instead, questions and comments come at the end of the session after the formal presentation. Often, disagreements are voiced in an ambiguous manner or not at all. Disagreements are subtly presented in more informal settings during meals or drinking

sessions.

Japanese do not interrupt one another during a meeting. Nor would they directly disagree in public. In addition, few Japanese interrupt a presenter to ask questions or make comments. If general input is wanted from those attending, the presenter needs to ask individuals to react at the end of his presentation. One Japanese speaker is prepared to give a specific presentation, and interruptions through questions are very disturbing. Japanese speakers are simply not prepared for the give-and- take so common in meetings of U.S. Americans.

Meetings following the U.S. American format are often the settings for argumentation. Each person is expected to present his or her idea and defend it against all others. At the same time, others are expected to criticize others' ideas and promote their own. The style of U.S. American meetings is similar to a Darwinian "survival of the fittest"

during which one proposal is shown to be the best. Japanese meetings, on the other hand, are seldom scenes of overt discussions and debate; individuals present information and proposals and give out subtle indication of support or lack of support. They are not, however, places where direct criticisms are expressed.

Those who want feedback after a proposal has been delivered call on individuals and ask them for their opinions. Most may be hesitant to give an impromptu response but some may volunteer a limited amount of opinions if encouraged to do so. Most answers will be of a general nature and probably vague and supportive.

3.15.9. Using Translators

Interpreters

and

It is necessary to have one's own interpreters at all times except during social occasions, though using one's own interpreters during evening outings and other social occasions are also recommended. Interpreters are a necessary part of one's team. Since age is highly respected in Japan, interpreters who are older are given more respect than one who is younger. Younger interpreters are more likely to have lived and studied in Japan, as well as majored in Japanese studies at U.S. university programs. However, an older interpreter, while perhaps not as bilingually-fluent, may receive more respect from negotiating opposites. He or she (there are now many female interpreters and translators in Japan) may also be familiar with the Japanese negotiators. Having a translator familiar with opposite interpreters may be, at times, an advantage (de Mente, 2012: 10). Some foreign businessmen feel

comfortable with using another's interpreters but doing so is a major strategic error for two reasons. Expecting your host to provide an interpreter is impolite and an interpreter becomes quickly overloaded when interpreting for both sides.

Interpreters listen and speak for you. They can also later explain the nuances of what was said during a meeting. Each meeting should be preceded by a session with the interpreter to discuss special vocabulary to be used, if any, and to listen to the interpreter's suggestions. The time spent being de-briefed by an interpreter after each meeting is also well worth the effort and time. Many gradations of politeness and signs of interest or disinterest cannot be conveyed during a conversation or quickly interpreted during an ongoing conversation, but they can be explained in detail after a meeting.

The importance of having a strong relationship in Japan involves the interpreter-client partnership. Hiring an interpreter on a temporary basis does not encourage trust and relationship building. A temporary interpreter may not be totally loyal to his employer, nor make the efforts necessary for good translations; he or she may also tell the temporary employer what she wants to hear. This interpreter may also not be familiar with the company's products or policies. He or she is in essence operating in the dark. In addition, a locally. hired interpreter may be seen as an outsider by the Japanese involved. They may be reluctant to discuss company secrets in front of someone they see as an outsider who may not be trusted. Our recommendation is to use the same interpreter for all negotiations. The same interpreter should also be available for evening entertainments.

The best situation, however, is to have an in- house interpreter who can be identified by Japanese negotiations as someone who is part of the other's company. This person will be more familiar with the products and goals of his negotiating side. He may have also been involved in earlier correspondences, and been present when visitors came to the foreigner's headquarters.

A phrase in Japanese may have several meanings according to the specific words used. There are five levels of politeness in the Japanese language and using one instead of another indicates very different emotional moods. The term arigatou means "thank you." By contrast the term "arigatou gozaimasu" also means "thank you" but is more polite and friendlier. Such nuances cannot be conveyed during an ongoing conversation but can be reported when a meeting is analyzed after the meeting.

It is important not to look at either interpreter when one is translating. You wish to use the time taken by a speaking interpreter to study the body language of your counterparts and to gauge their reactions to what they are hearing. The proper behavior is to look as whomever you are speaking to and ignore the translator. Doing so pays respect to the listener and is less distracting. The interpreter should be an invisible tool during a meeting. You should look at your interpreter once in a while to make certain she is not getting too tired or seems confused or harried. Those might be indications you are talking too fast or that it is time to ask for a break. Even when a subordinate is speaking, it is important to look repeatedly at the highest official

present.

The interpreter is never interrupted. Japanese is a compact language and a comment of a few words in Japanese takes several paragraphs to interpret into English. A simple "hello" in English may become a paragraph to show respect to the Japanese being greeted. It does no good to question your interpreter as she struggles to listen in one language and speak in another. It is also necessary to speak no more than two sentences at a time or the interpreter might lose her train of thought. U.S. Americans become especially frustrated when speaking is limited to one or two sentences at a time, waiting for the translation, and then speaking another two sentences. This slow progress is necessary unless one speaks Japanese.

An interpreter may at times take a moment to look up in a dictionary a word or phrase. While this is not ideal, there are always occasions when an interpreter is faced with a strange word. Be patient and do not indicate impatience with the delay. It is better that your interpreter feels comfortable enough to look up words she does not know rather than making a mistake by guessing. Some of these delays are caused by unfamiliar specialized vocabularies and the use of idioms and slang; such delays may at times be the result of inadequate consultations before a session took place.

A useful tactic is to conduct dry runs of a negotiation session. This alerts your interpreter as to your strategy and specialized vocabulary and helps build rapport. The interpreter becomes familiar with your style of speaking and can offer advice at this time on how to proceed in the most efficient manner. A mock session also allows negotiators to experience how interpreters operate. This accustoms them to the slow pace of talking

since using an interpreter doubles or more the length of a meeting. Often, the interpreter writes down what has been said and then translates these sentences into Japanese. An interpreter may also need a rest after a period, thus adding to a meeting's length. Be prepared to say half or less during a meeting in which an interpreter is used. Meetings become even less productive when each party uses an interpreter. Finally, always meet with your interpreter after a meeting to get debriefed. She will no doubt be sensitive to the mood of the meeting and can describe body language, the meaning of certain gestures, the language used, and signs of how presentations were being received.

There are times when two interpreters are necessary. Long meetings should include more than one interpreter so that one can rest and observe while the other translates. A second

interpreter can also study the body languages of the members of the other party. Two interpreters are also useful when social occasions are involved. Drinking sessions go more smoothly when two interpreters pass along messages to two groups drinking together. The senior author was once the interpreter for a group of Japanese who were unaccustomed to dealing with foreigners and did not know what to say to their guests. The Japanese drank heavily and refused to talk much; the senior author spent the whole evening making conversation while his colleagues became drunk with sake. The evening would have been more successful if he had a partner to help.

Translators read and translate documents from one language to another. It is best to use one's own translator though this is less vital than using one's own interpreter (they are often the same person).

International-class hotels in Japan always contain facilities and personnel for translating and printing material. They may have difficulty translating highly specialized or technical vocabularies, and in these cases, bringing an experienced translator as a member of a team becomes an advantage.

Responses to any written communication are faster if sent in Japanese. Most communications in a foreign language are sent to the Kokusai Bu (Foreign Affairs Office) to be translated. This necessarily takes time. It is also polite to suggest that a Japanese counterpart can write in Japanese to be rendered into English by one's own translator. Such a tactic allows a translator to judge the emotional content of a letter (how polite it is, etc.) that becomes lost after being translated into English.

3.15.10. Hosting a Business Party

There will be occasions when the foreigner in Japan for business reasons wants to host a party, if only to reciprocate for being a guest at many gatherings. Parties are customarily hosted in restaurants, though younger Japanese increasingly host smaller parties in their homes. Most business-related parties, however, take place in hotel banquet rooms and in restaurants. Most foreigners host parties at their hotels' restaurants. International-class hotels offer catering services and large banquet rooms.

The first thing to consider besides the guest list is making certain guests know where the party takes place. Tokyo has relatively few named streets and there is no sequential numbering system for buildings. It is customary to send a detailed map along with the invitation unless the venue is very well known. Most Japanese businesspersons are

familiar with the locations of Tokyo's larger hotels where foreigners are most likely to stay and host parties.

Business parties take place during the workweek and start soon after the end of the workday. No one wants to travel home to the suburbs for an hour or two and then commute back for a social gathering. Moreover, parties end fairly early so guests can catch the last trains to their home destinations.

Only employees attend business affairs; spouses are not part of the business scene and seldom attend business functions. The exceptions are when the business associates and spouses have known each other for a long time. In general, however, spouses do not attend such gatherings. There is always nervousness in terms of who has been invited, or not invited, to a party. Being invited to the proper party or meeting is a great concern to Japanese. Often, an invitation that includes all persons in a team or division is posted on a bulletin board so the invitees can see who will also attend. A posted invitation also reinforces the collective nature of the event.

Guests at a social function prefer knowing with one another and it is best to invite members of the same company. The U.S. American cocktail party where strangers are expected to interact with one another, introduce themselves, and entertain one another is a very uncomfortable setting for Japanese. Japanese do not like to interact with strangers and they do not enjoy socializing with those whose ranks and prestige are unfamiliar or different. It is best to include in the guest list only those who already know each other, since in Japan strangers and competitors have little to say to one another. Being engineers, for example,

is less important than being employees from competing companies. The host introduces guests who may not know one another since few Japanese, especially those who are older, want to introduce themselves to strangers.

3.16. Suggested Readings

Bird, Allan, editor (2002) Encyclopedia of Japanese Business and Management. London and New York: Routledge.

Brannen, Christalyn (1991) Coming to Japan on Business. Berkeley, California: Stone Bridge Press. Rowland, Diana (1985) Japanese Business Etiquette: A Practical Guide to Success with the Japanese. New York: Warner Books.

Yoshimura, Noboru and Philip Anderson (1987) Inside the Kaisha: Demystifying Japanese Business Behavior. Boston, Massachusetts: Harvard Business School Press.

4

NEGOTIATIONS

4.1. Introduction

Rosalie Tung (see section 4.29. below) states that there are twenty-five failures to every one success when negotiating with Japanese. There are many reasons for this discouraging estimate. Failure to reach an agreement is more likely when foreigners offer short-term arrangements and when they ignore the social dimensions of doing business. Many foreigners become too impatient with the pace of negotiation and unintentionally end negotiations too soon. Japanese negotiators are very patient and are willing to delay for years if necessary to get the contractual conditions they

want.

Negotiating failures are primarily the result of cultural misunderstandings rather than a lack of attractive economic offers. Knowing how the Japanese negotiate and the strategies they use decrease the likelihood of negotiating failure. Remember that Japanese culture is highly

uncertainty avoidance in character, and fear of failure is stronger than potential success. There is also the matter that change in Japan tends to come slowly in a step-by-step (kaizen) manner.

U.S. Americans also mistakenly rely too much on impersonal modes of communication. They use faxes, e-mail, and telephones more than Japanese. While some pre-negotiation information can be exchanged via mail and faxes, important information is delivered personally because face- to-face communication is best. If there is a problem or misunderstanding, the best strategy to adopt is to personally apologize. Japanese talk less politely (or not at all) to those who have angered them. Whether or not a personal dislike for a negotiator has developed can be easily judged from the way Japanese officials talk. Since business is personal in Japan, blocked communication can be opened by changing personnel to re-establish communication channels for more positive, trustworthy relationships.

The need for positive social relations for successful negotiations derives from a central aspect of Japanese business culture. Robert Charles Azar (2016: 5) stresses that business is a relationship-driven activity among Japanese, making them uncomfortable when dealing with strangers. Strangers ("outsiders") are unknown quantities to whom nothing is expected. This reluctance to deal with them includes a disinclination to share information with strangers. Consequently, negotiations remain stalled until trust is achieved and then information will be more freely exchanged.

4.2. The Negotiating Mindset

Japanese have historically felt their country to be a poor nation lacking material resources, poor in general, and surrounded by stronger, potentially threatening nations. Contemporary Japanese commercial and diplomatic negotiators continue to be on the defensive when dealing with foreigners. Japanese negotiators on their part are less adaptive and less accommodative than negotiators from other nations because the former fear the consequences of accepting business offers that might harm the Japanese nation or their companies. They make concessions very reluctantly because they also fear being overwhelmed. This mindset also makes them very cautious as they carefully weigh every nuance observed or imagined during negotiations. For important issues such as the importation of resources, food supplies, energy, or exports to other countries, Japanese negotiators exhibit a "fortress mentality" which makes them suspicious of foreigners, especially when they are not on personal terms with their negotiating counterparts.

Japanese commercial negotiators have become more aggressive during the last decades and many have dealt with foreign negotiators to such an extent that they now feel more comfortable when dealing with foreigners. In addition, many Japanese in recent years have lived in other countries and they have learned how to negotiate in the western styles. Nevertheless, Japanese negotiators to date generally maintain their distinctive defensive negotiating style.

Japanese negotiators seldom establish alternative strategies. They may not have fallback positions since they have spent their pre-negotiation time establishing a consensus among various competing

groups (sometimes called "tribes") with vested interests in the issue to be negotiated. Each new offer or counteroffer has to be discussed with colleagues and superiors.

4.3. First Socialize

The key to successful negotiations with Japanese is to be trusted and evaluated as a dependable person. This necessitates socializing a number of times. Westerners who expect a short negotiation based on a discussion of technical details and a closing discussion of prices and conditions will be greatly disappointed. Japanese negotiators feel rushed and threatened when they are not given enough time to learn about their own negotiating counterparts. This includes learning to "read" the others' emotions and emotional states as well as learning how to differentiate between honne and tatemae. Doing so necessitates several drinking sessions in informal settings. Japanese feel uncomfortable dealing with near-strangers, and they need to feel they have established personal relationships with their opposite members. All negotiation is centered in the knowledge that the negotiators know their counterparts. Just as Japanese dislike communicating and socializing with strangers, they also dislike negotiating with those they do not know well since strangers have no empathy toward each other.

It is necessary to discuss family, personal interests, and tourist issues at this stage. Those going to Japan to negotiate should be familiar with the major culturally important sites in Japan, especially those in Tokyo and nearby areas. At the minimum, westerners should indicate their wish to

visit the major temples and museums in Japan. A knowledge of and an appreciation of cultural items such as bonsai trees, ikebana flower arranging, sumo wrestling, national foods, and the like indicates respect for the Japanese culture and hence respect for the Japanese negotiators. This stage among U.S. Americans is usually very short and often completed during introductions. This is illustrated by the fact that within minutes after the start of the first meeting the participants call each other by their first names. There is, however, no attempt to gain additional knowledge of negotiation opposites until later, if at all. By contrast, Japanese negotiators believe that there are unlimited amounts of time to get acquainted and to get to know one another because business goals are at first secondary in the development of sociability. The way this acquaintance process is done is to ask the others about their hobbies and other interests. There will always be questions on how they are enjoying their stay in Japan (if the negotiations are taking place in Japan) and a polite interest in family and company. The first meeting is a formal one. No real negotiations will be done at this time, the purpose of this meeting is to develop trust and become acquainted. Often senior company officials attend this meeting to show respect to their visitors. They may arrive late or leave in the middle of the meeting to show their importance.

This socializing stage is important because it sets the stage for future dealings. Japanese are not as contract-oriented as westerners (though they have learned to being legalistic when dealing with foreigners) and they feel that contracts can be renegotiated or adjusted when conditions change. The concept naniwabushi refers to explaining why a

contract cannot be fulfilled and still maintain one's honor. Since the future is uncertain, drastically changing conditions could allow partners to reject a contract. For example, if energy prices increase to such an extent that a product can no longer be sold for a profit under the original contract, the traditional view among Japanese is that the contract now becomes void. Most Japanese contracts contain a phrase stating that contractual disputes will be solved through mediation. It is expected that disputes will arise, because Japanese are pessimistic about the future due to their high levels of uncertainty avoidance. Contracts based on friendships are flexible, and business-related "friends" should be willing to adjust to changing conditions placing one of the partners at a disadvantage. First meetings are important because they establish trust and feelings of friendship that form the base for possible contractual changes, should they become necessary.

Hodgson et al. (2000) give the example of a U.S. American executive whose company was near bankruptcy because of rapid costs in energy. When the Japanese partners heard of the American's) dilemma, they volunteered to re-negotiate their contract so that the U.S. American company could continue to supply the Japanese company and still maintain a small profit. In another example, a rapid change in monetary exchange rates resulted in a new contract that benefited the U.S. American partner. Because they had established enough trust with their Japanese partners, contracts could be changed to help the disadvantaged partner. The acceptance of a long-term relationship makes the future less threatening and is expected to help both partners adjust as events unfold to the detriment of

one or both partners.

4.4. Knowing the Priorities

Unfortunately, cross-cultural negotiations involve a great deal of misunderstanding largely because each nationality holds different goals and priorities and have unique ways to express its desires. Japanese negotiators dislike offering their foreign counterparts too much information while U.S. Americans by contrast willingly share information as long as the exchange is seen fair and equal and no commercial secrets are involved. In addition, Japanese negotiators are more group-oriented than individualistic. They prefer to reach an agreement that pleases everyone. By contrast U.S. Americans see negotiations more as combat in which there are losers and winners.

A major priority, perhaps at times the only priority, for U.S. Americans is achieving a quick agreement acceptable to both sides. By contrast a major priority for Japanese negotiators is the establishing of long-term relationships based on trust. The latter are willing to compromise in order to create a feeling of debt in the opposing party. U.S. American negotiators also compromise if doing so results in immediate compromises by the other party. This "tit-for-tat" strategy allows for limited compromises that are moment-specific rather than part of a long-run strategy. It is best to keep track of the compromises made by the Japanese and assume they were made not only to advance the negotiations but also for long-term strategies. It is a violation of trust to refuse to compromise in the future while assuming that earlier compromises are irrelevant.

4.5. The Use of Silence

Japanese negotiators use fewer words than U.S. Americans. They seldom interrupt a speaker with questions and they do not use humor to get the attention of others or to break the tension during negotiations. They are comfortable with periods of silence while they consider a response or what has been said. All of these practices irritate or at least unbalance U.S. American negotiators.

Silence is often used to avoid saying "no" while extended periods of silence may mean a wish to withdraw from the negotiations themselves. Silence also occurs when Japanese negotiators wish to think about a point they dislike and to give the other party a chance to reconsider. If a period of silence is followed by an abrupt change of topic then chances are that the earlier point is being rejected or needs to be discussed further, probably under more informal conditions. Stay calm and add to the silence when it exists, and try to "read the air" to understand why the gaps exist in a conversation (see section 5.11).

While silence may communicate disagreement, silence is also an indication of respect and dignity. At any case, Japanese are much more comfortable with silence than most westerners. At other times, silence is a strategy to make most westerners uncomfortable and perhaps forces them to say more than they wish. Silence tends to place. westerners off-guard, and their nervousness may make the foreigner talk too much to fill what to him is an uncomfortable void in the conversation. Japanese negotiators seldom ask for clarifications of points they do not understand. Asking questions may indicate ignorance or inattention, and

therefore asking questions may cause the person to lose face. Instead, Japanese negotiators discuss unclear issues among themselves and, if necessary, bring up them during later meetings. It is therefore important to be patient with requests to go back to previous issues thought already settled. It is possible that they now disagree or feel unclear about an issue. Showing patience at this point and a willingness to go back to earlier discussions builds trust and the confidence to discuss more difficult issues. A reluctance to go back to previously discussed issues causes the Japanese negotiators to withdraw from the negotiation, or at least discourages them from asking questions.

4.6. Slow Decisions

Japanese negotiators habitually discuss on-going negotiations with their colleagues and superiors. Decision-making among Japanese is often slow since the ideal decision is one that must be supported by total consensus. As a result, decisions among Japanese negotiators take three to five times longer to reach than in the west. New points on the negotiating agenda or new information also must be thoroughly understood and agreed upon before a decision can be reached and reported to the foreign negotiators.

A further reason why westerners consider the Japanese decision-making process so slow is that they do not like to make mistakes. Making a mistake is embarrassing and causes a loss of face. Japanese study each problem very carefully before making a decision in large part because they believe that it is better to avoid making an error by doing nothing temporarily than to make an error through hasty

action. By contrast, U.S. Americans would rather reach a decision rather than none at all; a bad decision can always be changed or reconsidered at a later time while the reverse is true among Japanese. They would much rather delay making a decision if only by being vague or by asking for more information. Japanese believe that doing nothing is better than being too hasty and as a result making mistakes. Japanese negotiators are also capable of sensing when their opponents are becoming impatient and are capable of slowing the pace of negotiations if doing so gains them an advantage.

4.7. Preparations

Preparation before negotiating in Japan other than learning how to communicate with Japanese counterparts is mental. Negotiations with Japanese also take longer than expected, irrespective of where they take place. It is necessary to be very patient since Japanese negotiators will not be rushed and will also insist of being thoroughly prepared. Japanese are fact-oriented and respect those who have a thorough command of relevant information. The Japanese education system stresses the memorization of facts and Japanese negotiators bring this skill to the negotiating table. Negotiations do not begin in earnest until all Japanese negotiators feel they have all information available.

The best chances for success include developing a personal relationship with the highest-level official possible. This person might become your representative and hopefully your sponsor. The chances of success are greatly increased if this person accepts your proposal. Even if this person

has only marginal responsibility during the ongoing negotiations, he becomes a mentor and helps avoid cultural landmines. He becomes the person to talk to when negotiations stall or if Japanese negotiators increasingly become vague and non- cooperative.

At the very least, adequate pre-negotiation preparations include learning the biographies of the leaders of the Japanese company. This includes educational experiences, personal hobbies, and past occupational history. A Japanese negotiator may have attended one of Japan's most prestigious universities or attended a foreign university, and such biographic information should be mentioned during the preliminary social gatherings.

A time-saving strategy to use before negotiations begin is to provide the Japanese with good- quality catalogs written in Japanese. Such materials include lists of one's company's customers and summaries of the company's dealings with other Japanese companies. The latter information is a subtle indication of the ability to deal with competitors if necessary. While photographs of products are useful, the best strategy includes bringing samples to Japan whenever that is feasible. This allows counterparts to evaluate products for possible use by Japanese consumers or by other Japanese companies. Samples also are concrete examples of company's activities and Japanese negotiators will not be satisfied until they study and handle the foreign company's products.

4.8. The Invisible Negotiators

There are a number of "absent" negotiators to consider during negotiations. The first

types of shadow negotiators are members of the Japanese national government. There are a number of governmental ministries greatly influencing business when international issues are involved. There are three important government organizations that direct in great detail international business: the Ministry of International Trade and Industry (MITI), the Ministry of Finance (MOF), and the Japan External Trade Organization (JETRO). MITI officials establish and enforce industrial and international economic policy. No corporate decision-maker decides on a corporate policy or signs a contract without the certainty that the relevant ministry is supportive.

There have been very few corporate presidents who have resisted "guidance" from MITI and other government agencies. An exception was Soichiro Honda. After building the world's best-selling motorcycles, Honda decided he could diversify by manufacturing automobiles. However, MITI'S policy at that time was to consolidate Japan's three smallest auto manufacturing companies into one large enough to better compete with western automakers, and MITI rejected the idea an additional auto manufacturer. Soichiro Honda ignored MITI's guidance and first built a successful sports car.

Government officials customarily give "informal administrative guidance" (gyó sei shido) to private companies on both formal and informal bases. Some of this "guidance" comes in the form of commands while others are delivered in a more informal manner; both types are usually followed, however. No Japanese executive would dare do anything that might displease ministry officials or be considered harmful to Japan. The concern for

national goals is illustrated in corporate creeds and statements of allegiance. All corporations have long statements or pledges that describe their goals. Employees frequently recite these slogans before their morning calisthenics or the start of their shifts. A survey of corporate philosophies showed that "good product at a reasonable price" was most frequently cited, as might be expected. However, the second most frequently mentioned value was "contribution to the nation" (cited in Kono and Clegg, 2001). The interaction among corporations, high-level government officials, and politicians is called "the iron triangle." Little that is important can be achieved without the agreement and involvement of all three power-centers.

Finally, Japanese officials are concerned with the fate of their nation in some way. The nationalism of older, more senior corporate officials is strong and few senior managers would accept a contract they felt did not also benefit the Japanese nation. Most of the older Japanese (younger Japanese are more individualistic) see their nation as extension of their families. In the same way, government officials see their tasks as promoting the nation as a whole as well. Government officials set policies for all international trade and few corporate executives would dare ignore government guidelines even if they were extra-legal "suggestions."

Japanese higher-level officials seldom take part in daily negotiations. They appear for symbolic reasons during the first meeting. Yet negotiators report to these absent leaders and follow guidelines. Leaders are often promoted because of their age and loyalty and may not be familiar with the technical details of any specific negotiation. Their responsibilities lie more in maintaining wa,

maintaining morale, developing and maintaining relationships with other leaders, and helping to establish and direct long-term policies. Their subordinates have the responsibility of dealing with day-to-day details and keeping their leaders informed.

Japanese society is made up of multiple groups arranged horizontally and these groups (called "tribes") are often very competitive with one another. Faced with an outside threat, on the other hand, formerly antagonistic groups unite and the outsider is faced with a solid front. The truth is that members of groups having similar goals compete for prestige and resources. As a result, a negotiating position has generally been thoroughly discussed within an organization by those who are involved in the specific issue being negotiated. Most negotiating stances are therefore the result of delicate consensus-making among competing groups. As a result, negotiators find it difficult to change any of their positions arbitrarily since doing so threatens the intra-company consensus on the issue. If the opposite negotiators offer unexpected demands or even concessions the Japanese negotiators on their part may not feel comfortable in responding until they consult with others interested in the issue being negotiated. Most pre-negotiation decisions are the result of political decisions that cannot be easily changed. Negotiators may be forced to seek a new consensus by essentially re-negotiating with members of powerful cliques. If there seems to be no progress in negotiating a new issue, it is best to discuss another matter until the previous issue is negotiated internally.

Japanese negotiators and their colleagues

negotiate among themselves to decide whether strategy and tactics need to be changed and to discuss whether their superiors are satisfied with the direction and progress of negotiations. All large Japanese corporations have "sister companies" (see section 1.10.) to which they are closely allied. These companies hold large shares of each other's stocks and they also have financial ties to the same banks and insurance companies. These inter-related companies are often suppliers and distributors to each other and hold many financial interests in common; they often interchange personnel when necessary, as when a person retires but wishes to continue working. Sister companies are expected to offer discounts to one another and depend on one another for favors, including advantageous prices. No large contract with a foreign company is possible unless the contract being discussed is favorable to these sister companies or at least when their officials do not reject the contract in question. The best source of negotiating success is when a contract also involves some of these sister companies to their advantage. As a result, negotiations are slowed because these sister companies are often taken into account in the negotiations. The fact that there are large numbers of cross-held shares of stock encourages cooperation.

These external and intra-organizational concerns necessarily slow down negotiations. There are often periods when further negotiations are useless until government officials indicate their agreement. Western negotiators suspecting there is a lack of momentum among negotiators achieve the best results by being patient and by informally seeking the reasons for this slow-down. It is best at this

juncture to invite fellow negotiators to a meal and a few drinks and subtly find out why negotiations are not proceeding according to expectations. There is a strong likelihood that government officials and members of other groups have not yet arrived at a consensus as to whether to support or veto specific negotiating issues.

1.9. Gift Giving

Japanese belong to a gift-giving culture and gifts are exchanged during almost every occasion, including during the start of negotiating sessions. The first meeting will not include task-oriented discussions and it is appropriate to offer gifts to everyone present at this time. Japanese enjoy receiving gifts, and inexpensive gifts at this time are appropriate. Gifts with company logo such as caps, pens, or desktop items are acceptable. The value of these items needs not be high because they are symbols indicating friendship and sociability. It is important that everyone present receives a gift if practical. Personal gifts are acceptable if given privately, though it is unlikely for a foreigner to have a private moment with one Japanese. A gift from one absent CEO to another is appropriate if they have already met. A present of golf balls is acceptable if they have played golf together. In this instance, the gift reminds everyone that a personal relationship already exists to form a base for future business dealings.

A sign that a relationship is being developed is the request to take a group portrait. If the negotiations are doing well, this is the time to arrange for an evening's entertainment that includes formal group portraits. Occasionally,

Japanese counterparts are given enlarged copies of the group's photograph. It is also appropriate for a member to take a few photographs during meals and drinking sessions.

4.10. Who Speaks First

The first formal session of the negotiations customarily includes a number of set speeches given in order of the ranks of the speakers. Senior officials are expected to say a few words of welcome and no speech needs to be very long or technical. At times, an outside spokesman may be asked to give a short speech. The person so appointed would most likely be the original introducer or even a high-level government official. If the negotiations involve issues of a manufacturing or agricultural nature, then the proper first speaker is often a government or agricultural official from the appropriate ministry or agency.

Westerners negotiating with Japanese should also be alert to the fact that a member of the Japanese team will be asked to give a short preparation even though this person has not said much during the earlier sessions. He is speaking because his superior wants him to give a presentation. He should not be ignored or asked questions since the reason for his speaking is to pass on a message the leaders may not want to verbalize themselves.

In the same way, members of a Japanese team may also speak in a prearranged order on a topic. Their contributions are formal and it is unwise to interrupt the sequence or react to a specific presenter. It may be that the leaders want to give each member of the team experience in presenting his thoughts or they wish to present a complete

picture of a topic on the agenda. Any interruption at this time is inappropriate and it is best to remain silent until all speakers have finished. U.S. Americans tend to discuss each point as it is expressed and find it difficult to wait for others to have their say. Nevertheless, it is important to wait out all speakers before making comments in turn or asking questions.

4.11. Never Interrupt

U.S.

Interruptions during meetings among Americans are common. The U.S. American low- context uses of language results in knowing what a speaker is going to say next and it is common to finish a sentence for someone. This shows support and understanding for what is being said. By contrast, Japanese believe that interruptions are violations of good manners and are too aggressive to be acceptable. Interruptions are also common during Japanese-U.S. American negotiations because Japanese are accustomed to pause when they talk. These pauses show the speaker is thinking and is taking what is being, said seriously. Unfortunately, such pauses on the part of Japanese speakers are often understood by U.S. Americans to be invitations to talk. In addition, U.S. Americans do not like silent periods in conversations and will immediately say something when a pause in a conversation occurs. Japanese find such interruptions disturbing and reject those who interrupt too much. Alan Goldman (see section 4.29. below) has coached Japanese to shorten their conversational pauses so that they are less likely to be interrupted by U.S. Americans.

Among Japanese, silence (chinmoku) is not

considered as a lack of something to say, but instead as a vital part of any communication. Communication pauses contain meanings as important as the spoken word conveys messages and information. Communication pauses are frequent even among friends and relatives. Those who are emotionally close are expected to be able to infer the meanings of pauses (Davie and Ikeno, 2002). The term ishin-denshin is defined as "heart to heart communication," in which one uses intuition and knowledge of others to communicate with fewer words. The heart among Japanese is the center of one's self and emotions.

There are also negative aspects to silence during a conversation. Silences may indicate trust and friendship, but also may indicate disagreement and a lack of understanding. It is difficult for a Japanese executive to admit he does not understand part of a presentation. He may also not want to admit openly he disagrees with the speaker, or that the speaker is not being clear; he may consider saying so openly would be impolite. Under such circumstances, it is easier to say nothing than cause the speaker and/ or listener to lose face. Silence is also used to avoid giving trade secrets.

When asked whether a delivery will take at a specific time, a salaryman will prefer to resort to silence when it is impossible to do so. In such cases, silence is used as an alternative to admitting failure as well as an alternative to saying "no." This type of silence is called "to sweep the dirt under the rug" (kusai mono ni futa wo suru).

Consequently, silence is accepted by Japanese as modesty, rejection of a presentation, respect for others' feelings, disagreement, misunderstanding. or a sign of trust and "belly" rapport. It is important

never to interrupt pauses or consider them as a waste of time. Japanese know silences make westerners uncomfortable and nervous, making them more likely to talk too much, perhaps to divulge secrets or cede a significant negotiation

item.

4.12. Letters of Understanding

A negotiating tactic saving time and avoids frustration is providing letters of understanding throughout the negotiation. These letters consist of personal summaries of agreements; they are essentially presentations of the progress being achieved. Any decisions negotiators feel has been reached should be included in these memos in case there exists a misunderstanding. If the other party disagrees, the letters alert everyone that misunderstandings exist and should be discussed during the next meetings.

4.13. Ask Questions

Japanese seldom volunteer information because they believe that information is too difficult to obtain to be given freely. Nevertheless, Japanese are willing to impart information when asked. Asking questions suggests humility and awareness the other party has superior knowledge. It is customary to ask a question containing an apology to show that the speaker is aware of asking for a favor and that he is placing himself into the other's hands because of the other's superior knowledge.

4.14. Affirmative Responses

Japanese prefer to be positive since a negative response suggests one party is at fault. In the same manner, progress during negotiations is stressed rather than the slow pace of negotiations or that many issues seem controversial. Responses by Japanese are almost always positive since a negative response is seen as critical or impolite. Unfortunately, western negotiators often misunderstand the supportive messages expressed by Japanese negotiators such as "so desu" ("that's right"), "hai" ("yes"), and "naruhodo" ("I see"). These are merely attention markers spoken to indicate respect and that one is listening and paying attention. Such words do not suggest agreement or understanding. Don R. McCreary (see section 4.29. below) hypothesizes that these verbal displays of etiquette are also indications of respect and dependency. The speaker shows subordination by "agreeing" to whatever is being said. Such behaviors, however, are tatemae (being polite) rather than gestures of true agreement.

By the same token, Japanese negotiators avoid saying a direct "no." Instead, they are more likely to say, "We must study this proposal more closely," "It would be difficult," and "Let's discuss this point later on." Another potential "no" is the response "I'll do my best." This last phrase is most likely a negative response. In Japanese society, trying one's best is often as important than being successful because a person may fail due to external conditions outside his control; if he has done his best, then he is at least partly blameless, especially if he apologizes for his lack of success.

4.15. Dislike of Certainty

Japanese hesitate to make definite statements dealing with the future. They feel that life is uncertain and that no one can predict the future. While it is necessary to plan in detail for specific future events such as a delivery date, Japanese realize that many factors exist that are outside their control, such as a change in currency or a storm causing delivery delays. How can one, Japanese negotiators hold, honestly promise to deliver a shipment at a specific date when the future is so uncertain? High in the value of uncertainty avoidance (see section 1.11) Japanese try to prepare for future disruptions, while they fear unanticipated events can appear.

As a consequence, Japanese negotiators dislike making absolute statements concerning future action and prefer stating schedules in vague terms such as "we hope to deliver your shipments according to schedule." Negotiators have learned to be somewhat more specific when dealing with U.S. Americans and other westerners in general, but foreign negotiators should not become impatient at Japanese ambiguity. Japanese are capable of delivering shipments on time but they hesitate to say so in an unequivocal manner. A response similar to "I think we can meet this date" can an affirmative answer rather than showing doubt.

From the Japanese perspective, being willing to do business with someone indicates a high level of trust has been achieved. Trustworthy friends accept some ambiguity since insisting on complete certainty suggests a lack of trust. It is best at this stage to allow Japanese negotiators vague statements with the understanding that an optimum date is understood, though "friends" are willing to allow leeway in an uncertain world.

Japanese partners have the reputation of meeting schedules as stated in contracts; nevertheless, a vaguely worded schedule gives the Japanese a sense of security. The contract will be detailed while oral statements are vague.

4.16. Attention to Details

The first chapter notes that the Japanese pay almost obsessive attention to details. Mark Zimmerman (1985) found that he gained respect when he could recall in detail discussions from previous negotiation meetings. His custom was to dictate into a recorder all he could recollect as soon as possible after each session and then have his notes transcribed and available for the next meeting. Remembering as exactly as possible a previous discussion impresses Japanese negotiators and allows for the correction of any erroneous memories. Japanese themselves have several participants taking detailed notes during a meeting. They then compare notes to make them as accurate as possible. U.S. Americans who prefer to "wing it" and ignore details as unimportant place themselves at a disadvantage as well as lose the respect of their counterparts. U.S. American negotiators prefer to bundle related details to facilitate faster while Japanese negotiators prefer to take up each detail separately. The Japanese approach to negotiation is to consider each detail as equally important and they do not like to bundle issues together. This approach takes more time, as Japanese thoroughness demands a close analysis of each detail in a proposal. It is best to be patient and prepared to spend as much time on unimportant as on important details.

A unique aspect of the Japanese attention to detail is their preference for a complete agenda before formal negotiations begin. The first item of negotiation is the development of an agenda because Japanese prefer to know which points will be discussed during negotiation sessions. This knowledge allows for careful preparation and intra- company consensus before the public negotiations begin.

4.17. Amae and Negotiations

Japanese feel uncomfortable unless they understand the hierarchical relationships of persons involved in the negotiations. They prefer to know who is where on the status ladder since the one recognized as a superior (older brother) is expected to act in a benevolent fashion. The older party is expected to offer concessions and treat others as younger brothers. By the same token, being defined as the subordinate "child" (younger brother) results in the expectation of receiving concessions and benevolence. However, the amae roles of father/son and older brother/younger brother are often reversed during negotiations and the negotiator can ask for concessions after having offered some concessions of his own. To Japanese such role reversals are proof of true harmony and cooperation as well as trust.

A failure to observe the amae relationship occurred during the post-war period. The Japanese economy had been nearly destroyed during the Second World War. The only major power with the finances to enjoy consumer goods was the United States of America. Corporate leaders in U.S. companies dealing with Japanese companies

during the first decade after the war treated the Japanese harshly and demanded contracts very favorable to them while the Japanese population was on the verge of starvation. Instead of accepting the role of older brothers and offering better terms, U.S. companies demanded harsh contracts when a little charity would have been highly appreciated. As a result, little personal loyalties or amar feelings developed between U.S. American and Japanese corporate officials. Japanese corporate officials refused to deal with their post-war buyers after they developed newer markets and more competitive products. It would have been better if U.S. companies had been more charitable and had developed feelings of loyalty with their Japanese partners.

4.18. Honne, Tatemae, and Negotiations

Extensive face-to-face interaction, socializing, and ceremonies during negotiations are necessary because of the need to break down the barriers established by tatemae and to reach the honne level in relationships. A tough negotiator who does not express his true feelings or intentions is said to "not show his honne." That is, he does not give a hint of his true position or reaction to his counterpart's negotiating offerings. In the U.S. American idiom, he has a poker face.

A problem is that tatemae might reflect a lack of desire to do business with a foreign company for unstated reasons. An outright rejection is seen as too impolite, and polite discourse continues until the foreigner realizes that an agreement is not going to happen and goes away without making an embarrassing scene. When tension emerges during

a negotiation, the solution is to discuss the problem in a more informal setting such as a golf course and at a bar to try to re-establish honne and amae relationships.

4.19. Negotiating Tactics

Japanese prefer low-key, non-aggressive negotiating behavior. The process is to first deal in generalities, allow the development of trust, and also evaluate the emotional atmosphere. Showing impatience at this stage tells the Japanese negotiators their counterparts are in a hurry and eager to return home. In this case the typical Japanese response is to slow the process in the hope the impatient negotiators cede some advantages just to finish as quickly as possible. It is also best not to tell Japanese negotiators when the U.S. Americans expect to leave Japan. A date of departure tells Japanese how long to delay if they sense the foreigners might cede conditions to finish on schedule. The proper tactic when asked when the foreign team expects to leave (the excuse will be to facilitate planning banquets, etc.) is to respond that no specific time has yet been established.

Donald Hendon, Rebecca Hendon, and Paul Herbig (1996) found that the favorite negotiating tactics of U.S. Americans were, in descending order of preference, "Take it or leave it," "You've got to do better than that," "Remind the opposite members of the competition," and "make a high initial demand." Only the last tactic is effective when dealing with the Japanese mindset. Making a high initial demand allows opposite negotiators to make concessions, thereby placing Japanese counterparts in their debt. Japanese negotiators prefer the more

flexible the "Give-and-Take" tactic since flexibility in negotiations as well as in social relations is a Japanese virtue. This tactic implies that no one should be given too much advantage and that cooperation offers the best chance for success.

By contrast, the second and third most-favored negotiating U.S. tactics are counterproductive because both are too demanding and seen as too aggressive. The "You've got to do better" tactic proposes an ultimatum very likely to be understood as arrogant and unfriendly. The third tactic-a mention of competitors-is also seen as unfriendly. A different tactic producing less resistance is blaming an absent superior or the home office when a condition is unacceptable. Saying a superior is adamantly to a proposal allows saying "no" in an impersonal fashion.

Japanese take a long view and their perspective is longer than the western frame of reference. An offer for a long-term relationship suggests a few minor points of disagreement are irrelevant if the two companies involved can become long- term partners. This tactic is best offered at the beginning of the negotiations to establish a general atmosphere of partnership.

Japanese negotiators are more willing to compromise when they expect the other party to compromise. This is accomplished by ceding negotiating points; however, the other party must also adopt a spirit of graciousness in turn. It is also acceptable to remind the other party of past compromises. It is appropriate to suggest, "We have given in to your demands several times and we feel it is your turn to do the same." It is also acceptable to ask for information on a previous demand to suggest the demand is either unusual or

inappropriate. The question, "Why are you insisting on our paying for the transportation of our products when we have never done so with other companies?" suggests disbelief in the rationale for a request and surprise that the demand was made. Japanese ask more questions than is the norm in the west and they understand this "asking questions" strategy as expressing disagreement. Asking questions is one major way Japanese indicate their feelings and interests while maintaining a tatemae form of politeness.

Japanese negotiators often expect that their foreign counterparts will intuit the things they wish to communicate but do not say openly. There may be several minutes of silence on the part of the Japanese when something difficult needs to be said but cannot be expressed for fear wa will be threatened. In this case it is best to also remain silent and let the Japanese negotiator re-start the negotiations.

Silence is broken politely by asking for something to drink. Asking for a drink allows for a few minutes of non-business interaction, especially if the requested drink is not easily available. A strategy is to make idle conversation while drinking a cup of tea or a glass of juice since others will most likely also drink something to be polite. At this time, negotiators discuss the weather, golf courses in Japan, or any other topic except the official business at hand. A person can easily spend thirty minutes in this way to avoid being impolite. At the end of the period, the negotiators begin to discuss another negotiating topic and avoid the issue that originally caused the tension and resulting silence. Another way to remain patient during periods of silence is to doodle on a pad of paper, or sketch out

next week's agenda. The important thing is to never show impatience. Above all, it is necessary to be patient. Japanese negotiators know U.S. Americans and westerners in general are impatient and are usually on tight schedules. Japanese are willing to outwait the other negotiators in the hopes that impatience will cause the latter to make mistakes. 4.20. Pre-Negotiation Procedures

A common courtesy is the translating of all background material into Japanese when the negotiations take place in Japan. Japanese are information-hungry and appreciate being given as much information as possible. Offering as much technical information as is consistent with the need for secrecy speeds up the negotiating process. Foreigners planning to negotiate in Japan often ignore the importance of the shōkai-sha (introducer). First contacts initiated by Japanese corporations almost always involve a shōkai-sha. He is the one who approaches representatives of a foreign firm with a request to consider establishing business dealings with a foreign company. John Graham and Yoshihiro Sano (see section 4.29. below) strongly recommend having the shokai-sha on the negotiating team. Since Japanese do not like to deal with strangers, a third-party such as the introducer recommends the two parties and begins the process to develop trust. He becomes very important whenever negotiations reach an impasse. He is the one who delivers bad news or feelings of discontent to the negotiating parties. The shōkai-sha attends the first ceremonial meetings when little or no business is discussed. He is also present when the signing of a contract is

celebrated.

Such mediators are necessary because Japanese managers do not like cold calls or correspondence from those they do not know. Local branches of international banks and accounting firms are good sources of shōkai-sha. Embassies are also good sources of personnel who have both extensive contacts and prestige. Finally, Japan contains numerous U.S. Americans who have spent their working lives in Japan, know the Japanese language, and have developed extensive contacts. Some Japanese specialists live in the U.S. and consult with those expecting to do business in Japan.

4.21. Prepare for Lots of Breaks

Be prepared for numerous breaks during the negotiations. There is often a need for in-house consultation when unexpected issues emerge. Japanese negotiators habitually ask for breaks when they are not certain that a consensus exists for a specific compromise or demand. Japanese negotiators also like to think about certain aspects of negotiations and they wish to do so without the pressure of others present. A very productive strategy when dealing with Japanese negotiators is to end the negotiations early in the afternoon and at times invite everyone to eat supper together. Eating together after a negotiating session allows the participants to settle issues in an informal manner. Allowing the Japanese negotiators to leave early also gives them the chance to talk over a few points and make some decisions before formal negotiations resume.

Since Japanese salarymen socialize often with customers and colleagues, negotiating sessions

I should not last more than the official time set in the schedule. Morning sessions should also end according to schedule. The only addition to negotiating sessions should be for eating and drinking together. The practice of having meals during negotiating sessions is foreign to Japanese practice.

4.22. First offers

Traditionally, Japanese peddlers were known for making outrageous first offers (Hodgson et al., 2000). They then lowered the prices for their goods when potential customers objected. Modern Japanese businessmen often do the same when dealing with near-strangers. If they do not feel secure in how to evaluate foreigners, the first offer is likely to be very high on the assumption that the client has the responsibility to suggest in turn a lower price. It is important not to act outraged or to claim to be insulted by an unrealistic first price. The unrealistically high first offer (or low if the Japanese is the buyer) is partly a consequence of insecurity when dealing with foreigners. The proper response is to politely reject the first offer and begin the negotiation in earnest. It is best to ignore the first offer if it is unrealistic in order not to embarrass the Japanese negotiators. First offers are more realistic when the two parties know each other and have a shared history. When this is the case, negotiations go much more quickly than before and the parameters of give-and-take become narrower.

4.23. Ignoring Statements

Japanese negotiators ignore statements demanding an embarrassing answer or one they do not want

to express. This is commonly done in the general society in order to maintain wa and to avoid showing disrespect.. Do not assume statements are not being understood if they are ignored or that Japanese negotiators are not paying attention. Make a note of such avoidances and feel out the negotiating counterparts under more relaxed surroundings. In any case, ignoring a question or statement indicates the question was either inappropriate or potentially embarrassing.

4.24. Be Patient

The first advice consultants and scholars offer when discussing the Japanese negotiating style is to be patient. Japanese have a fear of failure that might result in a loss of face. Their high levels of loyalty to their employers also encourage a sense that loss of negotiating advantage is a personal as well as a corporate loss. A survey of U.S. American negotiators conducted by Rosalie Tung (1984) indicated that ninety percent believed that "patience on part of U.S. team" was important or very important. There is a story of a Japanese Emperor living in the fifteenth century who gathered three feuding warlords. The Emperor asked them, "What would you do if you had a nightingale in your hand and it wouldn't sing?" The first warlord said, "I would kill the bird." The second said, "I would force it to sing." The third, who later became the shogun and leader of Japan, said, "I would wait until it sings." The moral is that decisions often have to wait until the right time. Patience is especially important when someone is negotiating a change of policy, as when a product new to the Japanese company is being proposed.

Japanese do not like rapid, unanticipated change, or to be rushed. They prefer slow, controlled, incremental change. The Japanese word for slow improvement is kaizen (see section 1.4 above). By contrast, the opposite of kaizen is change that is sudden, unpredictable, and chaotic or konran. This word has very negative connotations. The resulting differences between the two types of words is a preference for slow change and a reliance on precedence (kata) as a guide for how to act. Negotiations framed as major changes of policy disturb both kata and wa

Japanese negotiators hold a polychronic view of time in that they believe they have as much time as they need to complete a successful negotiation, and negotiators will not be rushed.
