The Japanese government negotiated with the American government for seventeen years before officials allowed U.S. orange exporters full access to the Japanese market. Finally, U.S. beef and orange growers were allowed unrestricted access to the Japanese retail market in large part because government officials thought that by doing so the U.S. government would be more amenable in another on-going negotiation dealing with national defense issues. The Japanese take the wider-view as well as the longer-view.

4.25. Do Not Come on Strong

It cannot be stressed enough that being aggressive with Japanese negotiators is counterproductive. Japanese negotiators bluff, indirectly threaten, and try to upset westerners by ambiguity and silence; however, they maintain low-key demeanors and strongly worded attacks are doomed to fail. Being

too forceful and demanding cause loss of face and respect. Follow the Japanese custom of using silence and ambiguity rather than being forceful. Saying "no" is too abrupt and unfriendly. It is better to soften any negative response in order not to appear aggressive. One strategy is to preface each negative response with an apology. Saying, "I'm sorry, but your asking price per unit is too high" places you in a subordinate position by the apology; it is now the other party's chance to appear benevolent by, ideally, making a concession. Japanese negotiators accept the practice of compromising as long as the other party also does the same. The Japanese saying "the bamboo that bends does not get blown down" reflects the Japanese value of compromise.

Another act that can be taken as too aggressive by Japanese negotiators is long eye contact. John L. Graham and Yoshihiro Sano (see section 4.29. below) warn that Japanese negotiators maintain eye contact with those they are speaking less than one-third of the time than do U.S. Americans. Staring is seen as an attempt to discover the other's feelings and therefore interpreted as too intrusive. Japanese want to keep their feelings hidden during negotiations and especially when dealing with strangers. It is better to avoid long periods of eye contacts when talking to Japanese under any situation.

In addition, a strongly negative response can unintentionally end or seriously disrupt the negotiations. Japanese would not consider exploring an issue if a negative response were too strong because the aim of negotiation is to achieve a consensus rather forcing one party to accept an unwanted position. No Japanese wants to continue discussing an issue if one person is

strongly against the proposition being discussed. U.S. Americans generally indicate rejection or displeasure by strongly expressing their feelings. This behavior almost always closes off discussion. The U.S. American may be ending negotiations without realizing it by being too expressive even if the intent was to begin a compromise process.

U.S. Americans often ignore the prestige differences among the members of the Japanese negotiating teams and focus their attention on the wrong members of the group. This is especially common when a junior member of the Japanese team delivers a very technical message. It is almost instinctive for U.S. Americans to respond to the speaker by communicating to him alone through eye contact, verbal interaction, etc. The Japanese speaker is representing the negotiating team and all responses are aimed at the group in general with special attention paid to the (older) leaders. Since Japanese leaders generally speak less often than their subordinates during a meeting, U.S. American team members often ignore the leaders and focus on the subordinates who have probably been appointed as speakers. Eye contact should be maintained with everyone at the meeting in a more or less random fashion with more attention given to the leaders of the negotiating team. The proper behavior after a lower-ranked specialist has finished his presentation is to begin eye contact with the highest ranked official and respond to that

official.

4.26. Your Negotiating Team

Japanese negotiating teams tend to be larger than those used in the west. In fact, the larger the

initial size to a negotiating team, the greater importance given to the negotiations. Larger teams also show respect for the opposite numbers as well as for the issue at hand. The Japanese negotiators may feel that the westerners do not consider the negotiations very important if their negotiating team is very small. Members from smaller companies can send smaller teams, but their sizes determine how much respect they will be given. Larger negotiating teams also reflect the wealth of the company and are a matter of prestige. The Japanese also assume that smaller U.S. American teams indicate the latter place less significance in the issues to be negotiated.

Japanese negotiating teams often include personnel from throughout the company since total corporate consensus is a major goal. These members add to the size of the Japanese negotiating team though they may not say much during the negotiating sessions. They are present mainly to observe and report to their own divisions. They may also have little expertise in the details of the current negotiations. Their function is not to negotiate but to make certain that important issues are discussed throughout a company and consensus in previous agreements is maintained.

Larger teams on the part of the U.S. Americans allow more persons to study the body language of the Japanese negotiators, study the less overt meanings of what is being said, and possibly catch more nuances. Moreover, larger teams allow the dividing of teams into smaller sections for entertainment. Often lower-ranked members pass valuable information to their counterparts at the end of a drinking or eating session. It is useful to encourage lower-ranked personnel to entertain one

another while higher ranked officials entertain one another as equals. Japanese act more relaxed when everyone present holds the same rank.

One or two of the US. American negotiators should be older officials with higher-ranking positions. Japanese respect age and older persons have greater prestige and higher positions. The highest-ranked officials leave after the preliminaries are finished. Their participation during the first meetings is primarily ceremonial, gains the team prestige, and indicates greater interest in the negotiations. The problem is that if only lower-ranked representatives are members of the negotiating team, the Japanese assume their opposites are not serious about achieving a contract and they will as a response also send lower-ranked personnel with little decision-making power.

Japanese respect those who show loyalty to their employers and group members. Japanese mythology offers many examples of persons who became heroic because of their loyalty. A popular story is The Forty-Seven Ronin (Akó Róshi) in which samurai retainers planned for years under humiliating circumstances to kill the courtier responsible for the suicide of their lord. When they finally achieved their revenge by killing the one who had forced the suicide, they met at their former lord's grave and committed suicide themselves. In a similar manner, dedication and loyalty to one's employers are seen as positive personality traits in today's Japan.

Another related trait of successful negotiators is having a cast-iron liver. Japanese entertain business associates much more often than westerners, and business dinners often include late-night drinking sessions. While it is not necessary to

drink vast amounts of alcohol, there will be ample opportunities to toast frequently and to at least appear partially inebriated. Japanese business culture is a drinking culture and it is difficult to avoid drinking alcoholic beverages.

The last trait necessary for complete success when dealing with Japanese negotiators is the ability to speak and understand the Japanese language. There is no substitute for knowing the language of foreign business associates. Europeans are aware of this need and most European businesspersons speak English, which has become the international business language, as well as another language foreign to them. In addition, the foremost schools for France's bureaucrats and future executives now demand their graduates to be fluent in two foreign languages. All Japanese students now take English from the ninth grade through high school and all Chinese students will soon be required to study the English language.

How many U.S. Americans can read a business proposal written in Japanese? How many can carry on an informal conversation in Japanese at a bar or while entertaining in a restaurant? The answer to both questions, unfortunately, is "too few," and U.S. Americans have the reputation of being the most mono-lingual of all nationalities. Full acceptance in international affairs demands fluency in the language of foreign counterparts. The presence of negotiators who understand and speak Japanese becomes a significant advantage if only to overhear what opponents are saying to one another. Consequently, having some co-members who are fluent in the Japanese language adds greatly to the likelihood of a successful negotiation.

4.27. Signing Celebrations

To U.S. Americans, signing a contract is often an afterthought and is seldom considered a very important activity. By contrast Japanese treat the signing of a negotiated contract very seriously, in the same way a U.S. president publicly signs an important bill in the Rose Garden of the White House with a large number of witnesses and speeches. Signing a business contract should also be taken seriously and accompanied with a formal banquet during or soon after the signing ceremony. Each person signing the contract is given a good quality pen to use to sign and to keep as a souvenir. During the following banquet, better gifts than have been previously exchanged are offered. Champagne is served at the time of the signing and the major executives present give short speeches. Extra guests are invited to both the signing and the banquet if the contract is an important one for both companies. These guests include representatives of the host companies' banks, advertising, insurance, and legal companies as well as the representatives from the major suppliers and subcontractors. Even a representative of the interpreter's company is invited as a gesture of appreciation.

The evening's ten to fifteen course banquet is extremely formal. There are more gifts to exchange, though that is optional. Leaders of both companies again offer speeches celebrating one another's friendship and long-term relationships. This is the time to express pleasure that amae and wa relationships have been established. The expectation of profit and economic success is downplayed at this time.

4.29. Suggested Readings

Alston, Jon P. (1990) The Intelligent Businessman's Guide to Japan. Tokyo, Japan: Charles E. Tuttle Company.

Blaker, Michael, Paul Giarra, and Ezra Vogel (2002) Case Studies in Japanese Negotiating Behavior. Washington, DC: United States Institute of Peace

Press.

Goldman, Alan (1994) Doing Business with the Japanese: A Guide to Successful Communication, Management, and Diplomacy. Albany, New York: State University of New York.

Graham, John L. and Yoshihiro Sano (1989) Smart Bargaining: Doing Business With the Japanese. New York: Harper & Row, Publishers.

Hodgson, James Day, Yoshihiro Sano, John L Graham (2000) Doing Business with the New Japan. Rowman & Littlefield Publishers, Inc.

Marsh, Robert M. (1988) The Japanese Negotiator: Subtlety and Strategy Beyond Western Logic. Tokyo: Kodansha International.

McCreary, Don R. (1986) Japanese-U.S. Business Negotiations: A Cross-Cultural Study. New York: Praeger Publishers.

Tung, Rosalie (1984) "How to Negotiate with the Japanese," California Management Review 26 (4):

62-77.

5.1.

55

WORKING WITH

JAPANESE

U.S. Blue-Collars Working for Japanese

U.S. American blue-collar workers generally approve working for Japanese. The Japanese model for factory-type work is often more humane and worker-oriented than their U.S. American counterparts. Work rules are adapted to allow for more job rotation and personal responsibility, and work itself becomes more efficient and productive without increasing physical effort (Graham, 1995). Japanese managers re-design work so that workers can "work smarter." This finding has not been found in all Japanese-managed factories and some Japanese managers and supervisors have gained the reputation of pushing their workers to degrees unknown in U.S. American-managed factories.

Assembly-line workers in Japanese-style factories do not work less than those in other countries and the work can be physically difficult. Companies exploiting workers are known as "black kigyo." These organizations are recognized as unfairly treating workers and ignoring the workers' well- being and interests.

However, Japanese-run factories located in the U.S. are more productive and the morale of their workers is overall higher than among similar workers in Japan. The pace of work is often accelerated because Japanese managers assume workers in the U.S. are as loyal to the company's goals as the workers in Japan. Some Japanese managers have also been surprised that line manufacturing workers in the U.S. do not identify closely with their employers, many have refused to wear company-approved logos on their clothing and work uniforms and have also refused to discard their own caps, tee shirts, etc. carrying logos other than the employer's. Some workers dislike doing morning calisthenics and singing company songs. Japanese managers view these activities as necessary to develop wa.

These cultural conflicts can be reduced as both Japanese managers and U.S. workers learn more about one another. One custom that eliminates many cultural misunderstandings is the practice of sending selected US. employees to Japan to expose them to the Japanese way of working. In Japanese-managed automobile factories located in the U.S., the results of Japanese-style management have been fewer assembly errors per automobile and faster assembly times than similar U.S.-owned

facilities.

At the same time, Japanese companies offer

cultural training to employees expected to be assigned to the US. Such programs include attention to U.S. American negotiating practices, leadership styles, and the like. Lauri Graham (1995) found that training programs focused on the development of social cohesion among workers, developing workers' initiative, and involving new workers in six through eight months of instruction. Three key principles were stressed:

Maintain or enhance the other person's self-

esteem.

Listen and respond with empathy. Ask for help in solving the problem. The above principles reflect the Japanese concern for wa, as does the following quotation from line assembly line workers' company's handbook (Graham, 1995:95):

Involve yourself enthusiastically. We are not asking associates [permanent workers] to work without compensation, but we do ask that all of us help establish a workplace where creativity comes naturally. It is natural to cooperate with each other as a team, trying to eliminate all possible waste, looking for ways to improve, and keeping each one's own work area a clean, happy place.

5.2. U.S. Managers Working for Japanese

Japanese managers are aware of the high labor- turnover rates in the US. Japanese managers have also experienced or learned about the high -to them-turnover of U.S. expatriates located in Japan. As a result, foreign managers are likely to be treated as temporary workers who soon leave

to their employers. It is very important to convey to Japanese colleagues a desire for job stability. Otherwise, Japanese colleagues will not spend the time establishing personal relations. The bonding process among Japanese takes time and few will make the effort if they feel the other person will soon disappear. Indicate satisfaction with the current work situation and express the wish to be an employee for a long time, if true. This can be done by making the effort to bond with colleagues and by taking the time to socialize even if it means being intrusive. Join in all common meals, attend all meetings, and take part when others go out to drink together.

Another way to become part of a work group or team is to perform favors for other members. The mere fact of volunteering for unwanted chores indicates loyalty to the group. These acts also establish debts that others feel obligated to pay back by being more social. A U.S. American who voluntarily empties wastepaper baskets periodically is seen as an enthusiastic and loyal employee. Westerners working for Japanese should also present proposals that clearly offer advantages to the company or team. One strategy is to present new ideas along with co-authors. Superiors are aware who originate proposals but they become more appreciative when new ideas are presented as originating from a team or division.

Similarly, U.S. Americans are taught not only to stand out but also to take charge, thereby gaining personal credit for their initiative. They also show their superiority by defending their own ideas and criticizing others'. These behaviors are completely counterproductive in a Japanese company. Leaders know which ones of their subordinates are superior

-after all, this ability is a major part of their administrative responsibilities but a façade of solidarity and individual anonymity is maintained to enhance wa.

The sense of privacy is different in Japan from the west. There is much less of a territorial sense in Japan and people are much less likely to experience unrestricted privacy. Visitors to a person's office often knock softly several times at the door, open the door, and enter without permission. Japanese managers are used to easier access to others and they see nothing wrong in entering another's office or work area after announcing themselves.

Japanese workers are expected to be impassioned about their loyalty to the company. As a result, there is less overt self-serving behavior. Workers are supposed to be concerned for the good of the company rather than for their own career advantages and personal reward. A survey reported by Robert March (see section 5.18. below) indicated that Japanese stationed in the U.S. spent an average of ten hours a week entertaining customers and Japanese visitors after work hours. A way of becoming appreciated and building up favors is to volunteer to help with these visits.

Western managers working for Japanese should visit the home office in Japan at least twice a year. Japanese prefer face-to-face interaction and periodic visits result in recognition and more visibility. Japanese expatriates attempt to visit the home office as frequently as possible because of the Japanese saying "Those who are absent are forgotten." Foreign managers will never be completely accepted by Japanese superiors, but visits on their part and many exchanges of letters may result in the Japanese treating them less as

guests and more as fellow employees.

5.3. The Gaikokujin Complex

Many Japanese feel uncomfortable working with foreigners and develop feelings of awkwardness and nervousness when with foreigners, in large part because they do not know how to behave toward foreigners. This sense of awkwardness is defined as iwakan (sense of incompatibility). There is also the fear of having inadequate foreign language skills. Some Japanese feel inferior to westerners, especially U.S. Americans, because they have been told since childhood that Americans are smarter, more inventive, and more advanced than Japanese.

In addition, Robert M. Marsh (see section 5.18. below) notes Japanese have never been trained to make friends easily since friendships are based on group memberships. Foreign strangers are i especially threatening when they are not part of a Japanese group. The result is the gaikokujin complex, whereby foreigners are seen as especially alien and discomforting. Such feelings are found among all nationalities to one degree or another, but they are acute when Japanese (and those from other high uncertainty cultures) deal with foreigners, in part because of their relatively high levels of uncertainty avoidance: foreigners are generally defined as threatening. These feelings are reinforced by foreigners' behavior: too brash, too aggressive, too blunt, and too different in general. The recommended strategy for foreigners to lessen the gaikokujin complex (affecting over sixty percent of the Japanese population) is to make each encounter as tension-free as possible. This includes

learning how to be politer and less threatening (from a Japanese perspective).

5.4. Japanese Biases

Many Japanese hold gender-related values conflicting with those of foreign workers. Not many women in Japan have been promoted to responsible positions, and most male Japanese resent dealing on an equal or subordinate manner with women. A great attraction for Japanese women in working for foreign companies is the greater possibility to be promoted and given authority to manage others and make decisions. In the past, some Japanese companies located in the U.S. experienced lawsuits and criticism due to their traditional sexist biases. Japanese are also unused to dealing with racial minorities and there have been instances of a reluctance to hire African-Americans in their U.S. American-based factories.

Another widespread perceived bias among Japanese is the strongly held belief that non- Japanese are lazy, including U.S. Americans. Japanese believe that U.S. Americans lack a strong work ethic. Such attitudes are based in part on a mistaken view of the social character of work. Work to Japanese includes a willingness to socialize with colleagues, work overtime, and take part in corporate activities. The latter activities include taking part in corporate-sponsored athletic activities, coming to work early to exercise, to listen to moral-building exhortations, and accepting direction on private matters. By contrast, workers from more individualistic cultures expect to have private lives strictly separated from work.

The issue of perceived laziness includes a

stereotypical view of a lack of concern for quality and a continuous search for improved quality. Japanese managers do not understand why quality standards are often lower in other cultures and why subcontractors, delivery personnel, and workers do not understand the need for high-quality perspectives. Older Japanese have grown up listening to innumerable lectures and slogans stressing the need for ever-higher quality performance at all levels, and they do not understand that workers from other nationalities have not been subjected to such messages. As a result, Japanese nationals retain as much authority as possible to maintain quality performance, and they hold the majority (66%) of managerial positions in Japanese foreign-based enterprises. By contrast, only twenty percent of managers in U.S. American-owned affiliates in Japan are non- Japanese (Fernandez, 1993).

Western workers are family-oriented and expect to spend their evenings and weekends with their families rather than spend them with coworkers. U.S. workers, for example, often entertain neighbors as well as friends developed outside of work while such activities are rare among Japanese salarymen. Non-Japanese also enjoy and expect more time with their families and leisure activities apart from company-sponsored recreational programs. No Japanese would ever miss work to attend a baseball game or hunt during the first day. of the season, although such absenteeism occurs in the U.S.

There are other sources of biases. Japanese managers are not accustomed to female employees who are married and have children. The number of working mothers used to be extremely small, and

source

of

Japanese managers do not understand that a female employee can be loyal to her employer as well as to her spouse and children. Work for married women with or without children used to be limited to part-time, though more women are found in full- time positions today, in large part due to Japan's contemporary labor shortage. Currently, sixty-eight percent of Japanese adult females participate in the labor force, though two-thirds of working women work part-time. Half of women return to work after the first child is born (Johnson, 2017). Another inter-cultural misunderstanding is the willingness of U.S. Americans to litigate and seek settlements of conflicts through the courts. Japanese discuss an issue extensively when there is conflict and they prefer to settle out of court than risk a formal lawsuit and involvement with the courts. Japanese feel U.S. Americans are too legalistic and too willing to settle work-related conflicts in the legal courts. Such attitudes are also found in the Japanese attitude toward labor unions. To Japanese, western labor unions are too adversarial and lack a feeling of partnership and sense of corporate community.

5.5. Learn Japanese and Japanese Culture

The Japanese language is one of the world's most important contemporary languages, and it is important to at least know a few basic phrases. Japanese is a very difficult language for westerners to learn and the U.S. State Department places Japanese in the "Most Difficult Language" category. State Department officials estimate that it takes at least eighty-eight weeks of intensive study to achieve fluency in Japanese at the professional

level. However, a survival level of Japanese can be obtained in less time. Some fluency in Japanese is necessary to do well with Japanese businessmen. At the very least, non-speakers need to socialize with colleagues during after-work drinking sessions without having someone constantly translate. Most university-trained younger Japanese know some English but many find speaking English exhausting and stressful, while they prefer to speak Japanese when relaxing. At the very least one needs to be able to speak a little Japanese when drinking with others. There is also the universal feeling that foreigners should be able to use the local language.

Japanese script uses four systems of characters. There is the traditional kanji or ideograms based on Chinese characters. There are two phonetic systems, katakana and hiragana, made up of forty-six characters each. Katakana is used to write foreign names and words borrowed from other languages, mainly English in recent decades. Hiragana is a phonetic writing system similar to western-type alphabets. It is used to indicate how to pronounce kanji characters. Hiragana is used in railway stations and some restaurant menus. Hiragana is used to help children and strangers who haven't mastered kanji. Last, there is an alphabet using English (Roman) letters, or roma-ji. Railway destinations signs now include kanji in the top row, below that is hiragana, and lowest is roman- ji. Foreign visitors attending the 2020 Olympics will be able to find their destinations. There will be ample multi-language signs, and omotenashi, or traditional hospitality.

Most words borrowed from English include the added vowels "ro" and "ru" to make them pronounceable by Japanese, such as fasuto fu-do

(fast food), tabako (cigarette), hotto doggu (hot dog), asupirin (aspirin), and sarariiman (businessman).

It is also important to become familiar with Japanese customs, especially general etiquette. No one will be successful dealing with Japanese unless adept in being polite in the Japanese way. A start is to take part in a cultural training program to sensitize the future expatriate in the subtleties of Japanese culture.

5.6. Less Praise

U.S. Americans commonly praise individuals while Japanese praise groups. U.S. Americans commonly praise an individual's character ("She is hardworking") or belongings ("I like your tie"). Japanese may react negatively to such praise by feeling different from others and by becoming uncomfortable because they have been singled out. It is better to be like everyone else and remain anonymous than standing out. This feeling reflects the saying "The raised nail gets hammered down." Avoid praising individuals and instead comment on groups or teams. Public recognition is appreciative, and public praise gives the recipient the responsibility to work even harder. Japanese offer indirect praise though a foreigner may not recognize it as such. Superiors use the phrase "Thank you for your trouble" to praise someone who has done well or who is working on a project. The foreigner needs to listen carefully for indirect praise.

Japanese supervisors seldom comment positively on their subordinates' work performance. It is assumed that workers will always do their best and are motivated to do so. As a result, supervisors

correct but seldom praise. A U.S. American once asked why his superior did not praise his work though he had in fact been praised but did not recognize it. A superior might take a subordinate out for a meal or stop to talk to him while he is working at his desk. The superior may even offer his subordinate advice to show he cares enough to offer personal advice.

5.7. Less Space

Working within a Japanese context means working in close physical proximity to others. Space is at a premium in Japan and offices as well as homes and public establishments are smaller than in the west. There is less physical space in Japanese work environments and desks are placed next to others while members of a division work literally elbow-to-elbow. In the past team members shared telephones and files. There is also less privacy in Japan and workers are seldom alone for any period of time. That is, space is used as much as possible, aisles are narrower, and co-workers are physically closer together when they congregate. Few Japanese, even chief officials of an organization, enjoy private offices. Some U.S. Americans have commented on their feeling claustrophobic while in Japan and in Japanese offices.

5.8. More Work

As pointed out in earlier chapters, Japanese are dedicated workers. U.S. Americans working for Japanese should also show their loyalty to companies and to their teams by working longer hours. It is shameful to leave work before other workers have left. Much of this extended time is

symbolic to show loyalty as the saying "Come to work early, leave work late, but don't do any work" indicates longer workdays express social cohesion and conformity rather than a desire to be more productive. When their bosses leave, then the subordinates in turn can leave. It is also important to attend all meetings appropriate for one's position and organizational level. It is still important for those who do not speak Japanese to take part in all discussions. One U.S. American attended all meetings to which he was invited even though he spoke very little Japanese and could not understand the conversations. At the end of each meeting his superior would ask him in English if he had anything to add. He would say something neutral whenever possible to symbolically contribute as a member of the group. His superior appreciated the fact he showed group loyalty by his attendance and symbolic agreement.

5.9. More Socializing

Communication among Japanese workers is passed from mouth-to-mouth rather than through more formal channels. In order to stay current on all corporate issues, it is necessary to socialize and show a wish to be part of the group. This means occasionally socializing during evenings. Business colleagues in Japan socialize to keep up with office gossip and politics and to maintain a reputation for corporate loyalty. Drinking together after work may be necessary to become accepted and become informed of information not available in other forms of communication.

A common feature of Japanese business is drinking tea or coffee with others. Tea is drunk

upon arriving at work, in the mid-morning, at noon, in the mid-afternoon, and often during the beginning of meetings. The question "Ocha wo nomi-masenka?" in its literal form is translated as "Let's have some tea." The phrase is also a request for a personal session. Japanese cities teem with small coffeeshops where tea, coffee, and light snacks are served. Such invitations are taken very seriously since they are offers of sociability and may mean the asker wants to offer advice or share valuable information in an informal situation.

A sign a foreigner is well into the process of being accepted is being taken to a place where customers are expected to take off their shoes. Japanese relax with friends and family by sitting together with their shoes off. Japanese take off their street shoes when entering their homes and, in some cases, when entering their offices. In the past, changing footwear suggested a respect for the workplace and showed that one thought the office was equivalent to home (in addition, pre-modern streets in Japan were not paved and shoes would at times be muddy or dusty when entering offices and stores). Under those conditions, many clerks and managers wore office slippers and everyone placed their outdoor shoes in a corner. Few contemporary Japanese would take their shoes off in the presence of strangers and superiors unless they were in a restaurant with traditional seating of mats (tatami) covering the floor. By contrast an informal setting almost demands informal posture, including taking off shoes. The proper response to seeing others take off their shoes in a drinking place is to do the same to show acceptance of the informality and membership in the group.

Socialization includes daily greetings. The U.S.

American manner is to greet others in an impersonal way and go on. Japanese see such greetings as too hurried and impolite. It is important to spend some time with each person met in the halls or when going through an office. This behavior is polite and extends personal networks. A person is never too busy to talk to someone unless an excuse or apology is personally given. Alison Lanier (1992) recommends learning at least a few words of Japanese to use as greetings. Phrases that support wa include "hello," "thank you," and "please." Frequent apologies such as "excuse me" and "I'm sorry" are also offered frequently.

Japanese expatriates are used to living in a society where everyone is polite, colleagues socialize often, and friends are primarily co-workers. The result is that many Japanese expatriates feel a sense of loneliness when they work outside Japan. It is important to show friendliness with Japanese colleagues. Fearful of being rejected by foreigners, Japanese expatriates are shy when not in the company of fellow Japanese. Coupled with their feelings that their foreign language skills are inadequate, few Japanese establish friendships while living in a foreign country. When a Japanese colleague is about to be returned to Japan, it is appropriate for his U.S. American colleagues to host a farewell party, though the Japanese colleague would be too shy to mention this custom.

5.10. Less Criticism

There is no greater difference between Japanese and U.S. American styles of communication than each culture's style of criticism. U.S. American

criticism is direct and blunt; Japanese criticism is indirect and subtle. One way to complain to a Japanese manager or indicate displeasure is to say that a proposal is "interesting." This comment is a criticism of the statement without attacking the wa of the situation. Other comments indicating criticism are "This is an unusual idea," and "This idea needs further thought." The previous comments indicate disapproval in one degree or another. Another way of indicating disapproval is to begin a sentence with "Perhaps..." The "perhaps" suggests something is not completely accepted.

Japanese dislike direct criticism because it threatens a person's face and self-identity. De Mente (1987) notes that this hesitancy to criticize results in the belief that doing nothing is often safer than doing something that might result in criticism. The word jichō means "to be prudent" and reinforces the Japanese custom of conforming to the group and leaders in order to obey all orders as fully as possible. Foreigners act properly when they avoid criticizing Japanese subordinates except in a very, very diplomatic manner.

If there is a need to criticize a subordinate, a superior often asks an intermediary to do so. U.S. Americans define indirectness as sneaky or cowardly; the reason why criticism is made indirectly, of course, is that the superior does not want to disturb the office's wa. Using indirect means to deliver criticism is defined as professional behavior among Japanese because the senior colleague did not become angry or make direct, possibly hurtful, comments though he has the authority to do so. Criticisms are also delivered not by attacking the person who made a mistake but by focusing on why a mistake was possible;

in addition, mistakes are seen as "problems" rather than a cause for criticism. The focus here is on the group finding a solution to the problem at hand rather than blaming an individual. Superiors have the responsibility to make certain that past errors do not happen again; the context of a problem is more important than the person responsible for the mistake. A Japanese when asked what is done when a problem exists stated that (JETRO, 1999: 21):

... if there is some bad situation, we Japanese will identify the problem and investigate the causes. And then we will involve many people connected in order to establish countermeasures, and we are apt to spend a lot of time trying to take care of the problem.

U.S. Americans customarily use the sandwich style to convey criticisms in writing. The top part of the "sandwich" is praise; the middle part states the criticism; the third part is a positive statement like the first. This two-part positive and one-part negative message form often confuses non-U.S. Americans since they are not certain whether the praise outweighs the criticism. The Japanese, by contrast, find it difficult to place criticism in a note of any kind. They are more likely to deliver bad news and criticisms verbally and in an informal situation rather than in writing.

5.11. Be Less Linear

We have stressed throughout this book that the Japanese way of communicating is indirect. It follows a spiral pattern going from vagueness to gradual precision. By contrast, U.S. Americans more direct in

are

their communication.

Communication is linear in that points are presented in a direct, logical fashion one after another. An argument is built up bit by bit in a sequential fashion. They present the main points of their proposal in a direct fashion that is opposite to the Japanese manner of discussion. It is more effective to be indirect and circular when communicating with Japanese.

In western cultures, the speaker has the responsibility to be clear and understood. Among Japanese, the listener has the responsibility to understand whatever is being communicated by the speaker. That is why close friends understand each other better than they understand strangers. It takes time for new members of a group or team to fully understand the subtle messages of those more senior. Those who cannot understand "belly" talk are described as "kuuki wo yomenai," or "unable to read the air." Even Japanese at times do not completely understand what is being

communicated.

U.S. Americans are likely to use numerous facts and present statistics to support their arguments. By contrast, Japanese prefer to present hard data during the later stages of a discussion. The first part of any discussion deals with suggesting one's earnestness and dependability. U.S. Americans also customarily challenge others' presentations in public. The Japanese practice is to express disagreement later in private settings. The direct approach of presenting, criticizing, and responding is too linear for Japanese. Japanese prefer to first seek agreement on a broad range of issues before discussing what separates the two parties.

U.S. Americans pride themselves in being straightforward and blunt. This is not the Japanese

way. Japanese lie to avoid telling someone the unadorned truth and thereby causing a loss of face. To the Japanese, harmony is central even if it does not reflect reality. When a Japanese tells a lie to promote wa, the best strategy is to pretend to accept the lie. Falsehoods that do not fool anyone are not meant to mislead but rather are told for the sake of maintaining harmony in the tatemae manner. Thus, when a fellow worker is being praised when everyone knows the person failed in his assignment, the best course of action is to conform and praise the person. An obvious lie should not be taken literally but rather in the spirit it was given (Sullivan, 1992). Jeremiah L. Sullivan suggests understanding why a falsehood has been stated rather than making a correction that is bound to embarrass others.

5.12. Brag Less

A manner of communicating of U.S. Americans that irritates Japanese listeners is how U.S. Americans promote themselves. Being more individualistic, U.S. Americans see work as accomplished by individuals rather than by groups. They are more likely to say, "I can do this" rather than "We can do this." By contrast, Japanese are more group-oriented and praise is given to all members of a group. U.S. Americans are also more likely to volunteer for an assignment. By contrast, Japanese seldom volunteer for a task. They prefer waiting until a leader recommends and asks a person to perform the task in question. The Japanese way is waiting to be asked to do a task rather than to come forward.

5.13. Indicate Loyalty

No one can succeed unless she convinces Japanese of her loyalty. Japanese expect others to be loyal, resulting in greater dedication to work and long working hours, socializing during evenings and weekends (for special occasions), performing "voluntary" tasks such as daily calisthenics, never complaining of work duties, and participating in corporate team activities. The good of the company is paramount over individual interests and loyalty is continuously expressed at work. Dennis Laurie (1992: 331) lists the necessary qualities needed for those who work with and for Japanese; the ten most important characteristics are:

Patience

Self-Control

Team Orientation Puritan Work Ethic

Tolerance for Ambiguity Respect for Age and

Rank

Long-Term Horizon

Enthusiasm for Work

Hunger for Information Sensitivity to Japanese

Culture

Those who cannot accept this profile will be unhappy, frustrated, and unsuccessful when working with Japanese. There will be tests for loyalty and enthusiasm and it is important to accept any duty no matter how petty. Many Japanese corporate employees, generally those who are hired in service-sector occupations, attend a military-style boot camp soon after joining their companies. The activities of these boot camps, aside from rigorous physical and group activities, include demeaning tasks such as cleaning toilets and begging and singing on street corners. These activities teach corporate recruits to obey their

superiors and that nothing is impossible or degrading if it is for the good of the company. Foreign employers who have not graduated from such programs are nevertheless tested for their loyalty and determination to work for the good of the company. No one is accepted, or successful, in a Japanese company if he does not accept these tests. The tests include obeying all orders from superiors and performing unofficial job duties. Concepts of corporate loyalty to Japanese managers include unlimited self-sacrificing much as family members help each other without expecting short- term rewards. Another way of showing loyalty is to socialize with colleagues when the occasion arises. Loyalty to group and organization is also expressed by showing respect to superiors. It is important to act formally with superiors and never criticize them because criticism suggests disloyalty. Respect for a superior expresses respect for rank and age as well as respect for the organization. Subordinates need to be very indirect and vague when criticizing a superior. Loyalty also means the avoidance of defending oneself when mistakenly blamed for an action. There is a tendency in the west to avoid being blamed if at all possible. The Japanese practice is to accept any criticism without complaint or defense. The ideal behavior in such cases is to apologize and promise to do better.

5.14. Select a Mentor

A central element for success when working with Japanese is the selection of a mentor willing to help a junior colleague learn what is necessary. Mentors tell subordinates which meeting should be attended; as important, he introduces him

to others. He also recommends the partner for promotions, suggests when he is ready for a specific appointment, and tells his own superiors when the one being mentored is ready for more responsibility. A foreign employee should similarly accept one or more younger Japanese to advise. They will expect him to advise them on business as well as non-business-related issues. Doing favors for colleagues during the beginning of the relationships makes the foreigner their mentor.

Mentors, called sempai (see section 1.10. above), take personal interests in their partners (cohai or kobun). Westerners would normally feel that their Japanese mentors' direction and advice are too personal and invasions of their privacy. This has to be accepted if the mentor-client relationship is to work in the Japanese fashion.

There is also a necessity of finding a co-worker to take a personal interest and help the kōhai fit into the work group. This person can tell the kohai which communications he has missed and advise him how to get along with others. He will make certain he is invited out to drinking sessions, show him how to deal with superiors, and teach him how work is accomplished. This person may even suggest what to wear! Ideally one's mentor should be a "spanner," that is, a Japanese who speaks English, understands the expatriate's home culture, and can serve as a bridge between Japanese and non-Japanese. Corporate spanners usually have lived outside Japan for some years and have gained insights on a foreigner's culture.

5.15. The First Interview

Hopefully, the go-between who recommended the

applicant to members of a Japanese company will be present during the first interview for a position in a Japanese-managed company. This immediately gives the person a social identity and an implied recommendation. During the first interview, in addition to technical information related to the position being sought, the recruiter asks a number of personal questions. Since being hired into a Japanese company is almost like being adopted into an extended family, Japanese interviewers want to know personal items about applicants. They are trying to evaluate the interviewee's potential for loyalty, sociability, and cooperation. Job candidates in Japan list their hobbies and leisure interests in their résumés, often in great detail. Those who enjoy playing golf describe their handicaps and their golfing experience. The discussion also includes personal likes and dislikes.

Such questions may seem irrelevant and invasive to western applicants. Japanese interviewers not only want to become familiar with the total personality but they also want to show the company expects personal relationships with employees. One of the most important elements of the reputation of a Japanese company is one known as having personal relationships with all "partners" or "associates," as employees at all levels are often called. A company that is not highly paternalistic will not attract the best job applicants. Japanese interviewers also ask about an applicant's family, where brothers work, and the father's occupation and place of work. For Japanese job applicants, it is said that major banks and other financial institutions often hire private detectives to learn something about the applicant's family (and possible minority status), what neighbors

think of the applicant and family, and the family's reputation in general. On the other hand, an applicant for a position in a Japanese company also asks personal questions about the company. He asks about sociability issues to show these dimensions of the workplace are important to him.

By contrast, Japanese interviewers are casual about an applicant's academic experiences. University grades are usually not considered important while the reputation and rank of the university are taken into account. For those seeking a blue-collar job at a Japanese-owned company, the potential for intra-company training is more important than specific skills and academic knowledge. Japanese want to develop motivated and loyal workers. Their intelligence or knowledge is secondary to loyalty and trainability, traits highly valued by Soichiro Honda. Japanese executives hold low evaluations of US American work habits and they prefer to hire those who can be trained to be efficient and cooperative in the Japanese manner. Japanese often view U.S. Americans' work experience as bad habits to be changed before the worker can become efficient.

The ability to learn is central for a job applicant's evaluation. Japanese managers prefer to rotate workers often during the first few years of employment. This rotation allows the new worker to meet co-workers, develop diverse work skills, and gives him a generalist's knowledge. Lateral work-rotation in the U.S. is often seen as a sign of management displeasure. In Japan, horizontal rotation is often the precursor to promotion and more responsibility. Promotion in Japanese- controlled companies takes the form of a spiral where one step upward is preceded by several

lateral moves. Lack of rotation to other duties and responsibilities is a probable sign of negative evaluations. In such an atmosphere, the ability to handle new responsibilities is more important than what one has learned in school.

5.16. Japanese To English

U.S. Americans unfamiliar with the Japanese language encounter many ambiguities whenever Japanese speak English. Japan's educational system emphasizes test-taking and evaluations based on written examinations. The result is that many Japanese read and write English better than they speak. The Japanese language has five vowel sounds while English has over twice that number. Consonants in Japanese are always connected with vowels and it is common for English-speaking Japanese to add vowels when a word ends in a consonant. The words "fast" and "first" may both be pronounced as "fa-su-to" and "bus" changed to "ba-

A term that creates cross-cultural confusion is "okagesamade." The word means "Thanks to ...," as in "Okagesamade (to all) for completing the annual report in time." The term also refers to others not present as in "Thanks to many persons, we made a profit this year" in response to the question "How is business?" The "to many persons" in this case refers to customers, workers, suppliers, and not to the one answering the question. However, westerners translate the word as "Thank you" within an individual context. By contrast, Japanese define the "you" as collective and plural rather than individual and singular. They assume that success was achieved because many persons cooperated and the

term is used more to show appreciation from one group to another. The typical western response would be "Not at all" or "I was glad to help." These are wrong responses while the correct responses are "Well done to all" and "Congratulations to everyone."

There is an occasion when Japanese seemingly behave in a rude manner when in fact they merely wish to avoid conflict. English-speaking Japanese have been taught that "had better" is politer than "should." A Japanese may be thinking, "You should meet my supervisor" when in fact he says, "You had better meet my supervisor." A JETRO document notes that the thought, "I think you are mistaken" is mistakenly to be verbalized as, "You had better change your mind." Such instances of appearing to be making demands are the result of improper knowledge of English rather than of actually making demands. On the other hand, the emphasis on harmony means that a demand be delivered as indirectly as possible and can be misunderstood by the more direct U.S. Americans.

Another common confusion is to pay too much attention to those who are able to speak English. The person fluent in English is not necessarily the most important person present, nor is he necessarily a decision-maker. The person in a group who is understood often becomes the focus of discussion. He becomes the refuge with whom speaking becomes easier. However, the English- speaker may merely be someone who has foreign experience. If he is younger than those present, it is almost certain that this speaker holds a subordinate position. Focusing on this person violates the corporate hierarchy and chain- of-command because the real leaders in the group are being

ignored. It is important to resist the temptation to focus on colleagues who can be understood.

5.17. Hosting Japanese

Hosting Japanese in the U.S. or elsewhere entails a complex set of unique etiquette expectations. Business-related social meetings almost always include alcohol. If senior officials or older persons (the two are synonymous) are present it is best to go to a place that is not crowded and fairly quiet. Senior officials prefer more luxurious places that complement their dignity. Until all participants know each other better, entertaining remains dignified and formal. The host should consider taking Japanese to a Japanese restaurant where the food and drinks are familiar to the guests. Doing so implies "We are familiar with your culture," and "We will not force you to adapt to our ways while you are our guests." However, many Japanese restaurants in the U.S. have westernized many of their selections. Since many non-Japanese do not generally like the taste of raw fish as served in sushi and sashimi, Japanese restaurants located in foreign countries often serve these items covered with sauces, sesame seeds, and the like. It is best to invite Japanese guests to more traditionally- authentic Japanese restaurants.

Japanese tourists in general enjoy shopping while in foreign countries. Prices for consumer luxury goods are more expensive in Japan than elsewhere. The schedule for a group of Japanese visiting the U.S. should include time for shopping. Many Japanese executives play golf and they enjoy shopping for golf equipment. In the unlikely event that spouses are also present, a special shopping

tour for spouses, including lunch, is appropriate and appreciated. In addition to personal shopping for goods they see as bargains, Japanese tourists need to shop for gifts for their friends and relatives. Japanese tourists are among the highest spenders while visiting the U.S. in part because they are obligated to offer numerous gifts and souvenirs when they return. Returning Japanese often shop frantically in airport duty-free shops to fill their gift lists before flying back to Japan (Tokyo's Narita airport also has excellent shops for departing foreigners). Japanese spent US$16.5 billion while in the US. during 2012 (Adwar, 2014). Other high- spending tourists were Brazilians (US$9.3 billion), British (US$12.6 billion), Chinese (US$8.8 billion), and Germans US$ 6.9 billions).

Japanese appreciate good-quality Japanese food and many are not very adventurous when dealing with foreign foods. A visit to at least one good Japanese restaurant, if available, should be included in a visit. In addition, many Japanese have never seen large meat portions common in the U.S. Those who have never been in the U.S. or are older may not be comfortable with large platefuls of beef. Kabobs and smaller steak cuts are more appropriate. Most Japanese also feel that eating meat sandwiches with hands is barbaric and many feel uncomfortable touching meats. It is better to avoid suggesting ribs and similar "finger foods" unless the Japanese guest has had experience with U.S. American foods. Japanese guests expect hosts to be sensitive to their needs and wishes, and hosts should be able to determine what guests want without the need to ask. Since asking for something may be seen as impolite, Japanese guests prefer to wait until the host offers them something they want. The

U.S. American practice is to offer guests numerous alternatives and ask them to choose. This approach does not work well with Japanese visitors. Japanese prefer to be told what they will enjoy. Instead of asking, "Would you like to watch a baseball or basketball game?" it is better to first know whether a guest prefers baseball to basketball and then announce, "Today, we will attend a baseball game." It is important to have personal knowledge of what guests (or at least the leaders) prefer to do while in the US. This involves prior knowledge of hobbies and personal interests.

5.18. Ask for help

Tony Silva, a consultant with over twenty-five years of experience in Japan, recommends that asking for help is one of the best ways of establishing a relationship with Japanese (Silva, 2014). Asking for help makes you seem humble and makes the other person a superior to you, since he can help you do something you can't. Being able to help a Japanese colleague begins a cycle of giving and receiving that is the foundation of Japanese society. This cycle forms a relationship of mutual dependence which are necessary to develop and maintain wa

It is almost impossible to deal with Japanese without establishing shinyo (trust) with someone. One builds shinyo by helping others, and by accepting help in return. This exchange of help and favors creates a new group between individuals (former strangers) as trust is developed through mutual favors. As a result, asking for help, especially when help has already first been given, creates alliances.

5.19. Suggested Readings

Huddleston, Jackson N., Jr. (1990) Gaijin Kaisha: Running a Foreign Business in Japan. Armonk, New York: M. E. Sharpe, Inc.

March Robert M. (1996) Working for a Japanese Company: Insights into the Multicultural Workplace. Tokyo: Kodansha International.

Ralston, David A, et al (1997) "The Impact of National Culture and Economic Ideology on Managerial Work Values: A Study of the United States, Russia, Japan, and China," Journal in International Business Studies 28 (1 Qtr.):

177-207.

APPENDIX A

GLOSSARY OF USEFUL WORDS

Akihabara

Amakudari

The section of Tokyo specializing in discounted electric appliances, electronics, cameras, computers, and similar items. The prices are about one-fourth less than in other shopping areas and limited bargaining is allowed; the Akihabara area contains a railroad station with hundreds of small stores and stalls inside and around the station.

"Descent from heaven." The term refers to the practice, especially among government bureaucrats, to retire from work at relatively young ages to accept high-level positions in industry. The former official usually joins a company in an industry he has been involved with and is thus familiar with its relations with the government. The result is that private industry contains and members numerous former officials, thereby making the government.

Chōrei

Con-vini

industry ties very strong. Workers in private industry often retire to join the former employer's suppliers and subcontractors. This also cements ties among companies with similar interests.

Japanese workplaces begin the day with chorei or pep talks. The president himself will often give a short talk. In larger companies each division or team will have its own chorei followed by some gentle calisthenics and stretching exercises even in white-collar offices. There might be the singing of the company song or chanting of the current company slogan. In some corporations a very short memo is handed out every morning to alert everyone concerning important meetings and events.

"Convenience store". Though similar to gas stations and convenience stores in the United States, a con- vini in Japan offers a greater variety of products and is open twenty. four hours, seven days a week. They can be found at almost every street corner in large and middle-sized cities in Japan. Con-vini are places where workers buy breakfast and snack items such as bento boxes, pay their utility bills, and shop on the way home. Refrigerators are small in order to fit in Japan's small kitchens,

Eki-Biru

Gaikokujin

forcing housewives and husbands to shop several times a week for perishable foodstuffs.

Large shopping areas in train stations. Major train and subway stations in Japan include large shopping areas as well as places where snacks and bento boxes can be found. Since most workers commute, eki-biru are convenient places to buy small items on the way to work or home. Such areas include restaurants, booths for takeout food, pharmacies, etc. Many now have become malls for less expensive goods. They are easy places to shop in for foreigners needing simple products and gifts. Clerks will write down the amount to pay bills or show the totals on calculators for those who do not speak Japanese.

"Foreigner" or "outsider." Used to denote strangers outside one's family as well as foreigners, the term suggests the person is not completely human. Gaikokujin are excluded from normal society because they are identified as too different to be fully accepted (see ijime). The term Gaijin is derogatory and should not be used.

Haji

Ijime

members owe total loyalty. The members attempt to influence policy through indirect means and they compete for resources with other habatsu. Habatsu are the settings for change and group rivalries.

"Shame." Ruth Benedict (1887-1948), author of the classic study of Japanese society The Chrysanthemum and The Sword (1946), characterized Japanese morality as based on shame rather than guilt. Guilt cultures stress absolute moral standards that are internalized by individuals as consciences. Shame cultures stress conformity to others; face (kao) and acceptance by others are the criteria

for behavior.

"Bullying." The importance of conformity to groups can result. in school children ostracizing and bullying those who differ, such as dressing unusually, not joining enough school clubs, being overweight, or having a physical handicap. Ijime can result in physical violence, verbal abuse, and being rejected. Since rejection is especially. hurtful

in Japan's conformist society, ijime has resulted in suicide and, less commonly, homicide.

Habatsu

Small groups or cliques (called "tribes") in Japanese organizations. They are informal groups to which

Juku

"Private tutorial schools." Most children of middle class parents

Koseki

Kondankai

attend juku schools after their regular school days. These offer cram programs that give students advantages in university entrance examinations. The highest ranked universities and their departments are extremely competitive and demand applicants have knowledge not taught in most high schools. As a result, parents who are ambitious for their children enroll them in expensive juku to give them additional advantages over other applicants.

"Family register." The koseki is a government dossier recording the major events of a person's life and parents. A koseki contains information on parents' divorce and number of children, residence and occupational patterns, whether one is illegitimate or adopted, family provincial origins and ethnicity, educational history, and more. Would-be employers used to study the koseki of recruits though access to koseki is now limited to the police. A committee with representations with members from industry, government, and finance to discuss current problems and establish policy for each industry. Decisions form unwritten agreements that nevertheless policy set standards. kondankai The

and

is

Office Ladies

Risutora

commonly used when a business situation involves questions of national security or matters of interests to an industry as a whole. Office Ladies (OLs) are young women working as assistants. Their duties are primarily supportive; they bring supplies when needed and serve tea and refreshments when appropriate. These jobs are more ceremonial than functional, though some serve as receptionists and operate various office machines such as fax and duplicating machines. In the past, office Ladies often worked because it gave them a chance to experience the world outside of home after graduation and to receive and save money for marriage, as well as having the chance to meet. prospective husbands. The 1986 Equal Employment Opportunity Law forbids firing just-married or engaged OLS, but managers generally expect OLs to leave when they marry.

"Restructuring." Risutora became one of the most frequently used words in Japan after the collapse of the bubble economy in 1990. Risutora represents the end or alteration of many traditional elements of the Japanese lifetime employment system. Many workers today, including older workers used

Shinyo

Sókaiya

to total job security before risutora, can no longer count on lifetime employment. New graduates are especially concerned that they can be laid off at any time should another downturn in Japan's economy occur. Many middle-aged workers have decided to become "free-ta" (part- time workers) because they fear they will be laid off or permanently fired. Part-time work as well as the chance of being laid off have undermined the morale and company loyalty of so-called permanent workers.

"Intuition." Japanese prefer knowing persons well before they begin business discussions. They like to feel as if they "know" someone so well that they can intuit the other's feelings and states of mind.

A sokaiya is often a member of a yakuza (gangster gang) who attends stockholders' meetings. He blackmails corporate officials through his threats to disrupt the meetings by asking unwanted questions, heckling, or making insulting comments. Some are hired by the corporations to physically intimidate stockholders who might also wish to ask embarrassing questions. Sókaiya advertise, keep offices, and now maintain web sites. Their activities are one reason why little constructive discussion takes

place during annual stockholders' meetings.

ABOUT THE AUTHORS

Isao Takei is Associate Professor at Nihon University, Japan. He has a Master's degree in sociology from Texas A&M University, and a Ph.D. in sociology from the University of Texas at Austin. His research interests include racial and ethnic relations, social stratification, and Japanese studies. He has published a number of articles dealing with Asian Americans and Japanese and American

cultures.

Jon P. Alston is Emeritus Professor of sociology at Texas A&M University and has taught in China and France. His published works include A Business Guide to China, A Practical Guide to French Business, and The Scientific Case Against Scientific Creationism.
